Hadd for Stealing Valuable Woods
States ruling: hadd applies to rare woods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on stealing valuable rare woods and explain the rationale based on their property status.

Core Idea
Hadd applies to valuable woods because they are protected (muḥraz) property, not found ownerless in Islamic lands.

Woods like teak, ebony, and sandalwood are not ordinary; they are scarce and typically stored as valuable goods. The Hanafi school confirms that the hadd penalty applies if such woods are stolen, because their property status is certain. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling and its implications for similar items.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is stealing specific valuable woods (saj, qana, abnus, sandal). The ruling is that the hadd (hand amputation) is imposed.
2
Locate the proof (ta'lil)
The author provides the reason: 'because they are protected properties (amwal muhrazah) due to being precious, and they are not found as mubah in Dar al-Islam.' This is the 'illa (legal cause).
The proof is not a direct text from Quran/Sunnah but a reasoning (ta'lil) based on customary property status.
3
Analyze the conditions for hadd
Hadd requires the stolen item to be property (mal) that is muhraz (in safe custody), reach the nisab (minimum value), and be taken with no doubt about ownership. These woods satisfy all because they are valuable and guarded.
The text emphasizes that these woods are not found growing wild as ownerless, so their status as owned property is clear.
4
Apply to similar cases
Any valuable item that is scarce and customarily kept as property (like precious metals, gems) would similarly incur hadd. Common woods that are widespread and often ownerless (e.g., ordinary firewood) would not.
The 'illa is scarcity and lack of mubah counterpart.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيُقْطَعُ فِي السَّاجِ وَالْقَنَا وَ الْأَبْنُوسِ وَ الصَّنْدَلِ لِأَنَّهَا أَمْوَالٌ مُحْرَزَةٌ لِكَوْنِهَا عَزِيزَةٌ عِنْدَ النَّاسِ وَلَا تُوْجَدُ بصُورَتِهَا مُبَاحَةٌ فِي دَارِ الْإِسْلَام
And cutting is imposed for (stealing) teak, qana (bamboo), ebony, and sandalwood because they are protected properties due to being precious among people, and they are not found in their form as mubah in Dar al-Islam.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage declares that the hadd penalty is applied for stealing certain valuable types of wood: saj (teak), qana (a type of wood used for spears), abnus (ebony), and sandal (sandalwood). The reason is that these are considered valuable and scarce (aziza 'inda al-nas) and they are not found growing wild as mubah (ownerless) in Islamic lands. Therefore, when someone steals them, the property status is clear, no doubt, and if the nisab is met, the hand is cut. The commentary likens them to gold and silver because they are universally valued.

Key Terms
مُحْرَز (muḥraz): Protected; property that is guarded and kept safely, typically in a hiraz.
مباح (mubāḥ): Ownerless property that is permissible to take; in this context, items that are found wild and unowned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the hadd applied for stealing sandalwood according to the passage?
A) Because sandalwood is very lightweight
B) Because it is not found as mubah (ownerless) in Dar al-Islam
C) Because it is always kept in a hiraz
D) Because it is used in worship

Key Takeaway
Hadd applies to valuable woods that are never ownerless in Islamic lands, because their property status is certain.

Hadd for Theft of Precious Stones
States ruling with qiyas proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi qiyas that extends the hadd penalty to theft of precious stones and identify the effective cause (illah).

Core Idea
Precious stones are like gold and silver: highly valued and never mubah, so hadd applies.

This passage from al-Hidayah clarifies that the hadd penalty for theft is not limited to gold and silver but applies to all valuable property that is never ownerless (mubah). The author uses qiyas jali (clear analogy) to include precious gems. Understanding this analogy strengthens your grasp of property law (mal) in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What act is being ruled upon? Theft of specific precious stones. What is the ruling? Hadd (amputation) is imposed.
The text explicitly states: 'And cutting is imposed for (stealing) green gems, rubies, and emeralds.'
2
Note the explicit list
The passage names three types: green gems (fuṣūṣ khuḍr), rubies (yāqūt), and emeralds (zabarjad). These are examples; the ruling extends to all similar precious stones.
The list is not exhaustive; it illustrates the category.
3
Identify the illah (effective cause)
Two reasons are given: (1) they are among the most precious properties (a'azz al-amwāl), and (2) they are not found as mubah al-aṣl (ownerless) in Dār al-Islām. These form the illah.
The illah is the combination of high value and unavailability as mubah.
4
Trace the qiyas structure
Asl (original case): gold and silver, for which hadd applies by ijma and texts. Far' (new case): precious stones. Illah: valuable and not mubah. Hukm: hadd applies.
This is a clear qiyas jali (explicit analogy) because the illah is directly mentioned.
5
Recognize the Hanafi position
This is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) in the Hanafi school. There is no intra-Hanafi dispute recorded here.
The authority weight is ẓāhir al-riwāyah (core transmitted position).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَيُقْطَعُ فِي الْفُصُوصِ الْخُضْرِ وَالْيَاقُوتِ وَالزَّبَرْجَدِ لِأَنَّهَا مِنْ أَعَزَالًا مُوَالِ وَأَنْفُسِهَا لَا تُوْجَدُ مُبَاحُ الْأَصْلِ بِصُورَتِهَا فِي دَارِ الْإِسْلَامِ غَيْرُ مَرْغُوبٍ فِيْهَا فَصَارَ كَالذَّهَبِ وَالْفِضَّةِ
He said: And cutting is imposed for (stealing) green gems, rubies, and emeralds because they are among the most precious of properties, and they themselves are not found as mubah al-asl in their form in Dar al-Islam, undesirable, so they become like gold and silver.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage extends the hadd penalty to theft of precious stones: green gems (fusuṣ khuḍr, possibly emeralds), rubies (yaqut), and emeralds (zabarjad). The reasoning is that these are among the most valuable properties (a'azz al-amwal) and are never found as mubah (ownerless) in Islamic lands; they are always owned and desired. Therefore, they are analogous to gold and silver in their property status. The hadd applies if the nisab is met.

Key Terms
فُصُوص خُضْر (fuṣūṣ khuḍr): Green gems; probably emeralds or green agates.
يَاقُوت (yāqūt): Ruby; a precious gemstone.
زَبَرْجَد (zabarjad): Emerald or peridot; a green gemstone.
مُبَاحَة الْأَصْل (mubāḥat al-aṣl): Ownerless in its original state; permissible for anyone to take.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do precious stones receive the same hadd penalty as gold and silver?
A) Because they are minerals like gold and silver.
B) Because they are precious and not found as mubah in Dar al-Islam.
C) Because they are used in jewelry.
D) Because they are heavy.

Key Takeaway
Precious stones are treated like gold and silver for the hadd penalty because they are highly valued and never ownerless, fitting the same effective cause.

Conditions for Amputation in Theft of Manufactured Wooden Items and Mats
States conditions for amputation on manufactured wooden items and mats

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which amputation becomes wajib for theft of manufactured wooden items and mats according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Craftsmanship transforms raw material into a guarded valuable, but amputation also requires the item to be light and non-fixed.

This passage from Al-Hidayah expands on the conditions for the hadd of theft (sariqa). While raw wood is not considered a guarded valuable, once crafted into utensils or doors, the item becomes legally significant because of the craftsmanship (al-sun'ah). However, not all manufactured items qualify – ordinary mats are excluded unless they are of high quality (Baghdadi). Additionally, fixed objects and heavy objects are not subject to amputation. In this lesson, we will dissect the ruling and its underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling and its conditions
State that amputation is wajib for the theft of wooden utensils and doors due to craftsmanship making them valuable and guarded. Note the exception for ordinary mats and the additional conditions of not being fixed and being light.
The ruling is a conditional wajib: amputation applies only when all conditions are met.
2
Understand the role of craftsmanship (sun'ah)
Craftsmanship elevates the item from raw material (not guarded) to a valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) that is customarily guarded. This is why utensils and doors are treated differently from raw wood.
The Hanafi principle: 'al-sun'ah tufid al-hirz wa al-qimah' – craftsmanship confers guarded status and value.
3
Distinguish between different items
Utensils and doors: amputation applies. Ordinary mats: no amputation because craftsmanship does not dominate – mats are spread even in non-guarded places. Baghdadi mats: amputation applies because craftsmanship dominates the material.
The key is 'ghalabat al-sun'ah' – dominance of craftsmanship over the raw material.
4
Note the additional conditions: not fixed and light
The stolen item must not be fixed (murakkab) – e.g., a door attached to a wall is not subject to amputation. It must also be light enough that one person can carry it alone; heavy items are not desired for theft.
These conditions reflect the 'illa of sariqa: taking a guarded valuable with stealth and ability to remove.
5
Analyze the reasoning behind the light condition
The text says heavy items are not desired for theft (la yarghabu fi sarqatihi). This implies that the hadd is only for items that are typically stolen; heavy items are not prone to theft, so the definitive proof of theft is less certain.
This is a rational extension (dalil aqli) based on custom and common occurrence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا اتَّخَذَ مِنَ الْخَشَبِ أَوَانِيَ وَأَبْوَابًا قُطِعَ فِيهَا لِأَنَّهُ بِالصَّنْعَةِ الْتَحَقَ بِالْأَمْوَالِ النَّفِيْسَةِ أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّهَا تُحْرَزُ بِخِلَافِ الْحَصِيرِ لِأَنَّ الصَّنْعَةَ فِيهِ لَمْ تَغْلِبْ عَلَى الْجِنْسِ حَتَّى يُبْسَطَ فِي غَيْرِ الْحِرْزِ وَفِي الْحَصِيرِ الْبَغْدَادِيَّةِ قَالُوا يَجِبُ الْقَطْعُ فِي سَرْقَتِهَا لِغَلْبَةِ الصَّنْعَةِ عَلَى الْأَصْلِ وَإِنَّمَا يَجِبُ الْقَطْعُ فِي غَيْرِ الْمُرَكَّبِ وَإِنَّمَا يَجِبُ إِذَا كَانَ خَفِيفًا لَا يَثْقُلُ عَلَى الْوَاحِدِ حَمْلُهُ لِأَنَّ التَّقِيلَ مِنْهُ لَا يَرْغَبُ فِي سَرْقَتِهِ
And when he makes from wood utensils and doors, amputation is incurred for them because through craftsmanship they join the category of valuable property. Do you not see that they are guarded? Contrary to the mat, because the craftsmanship in it does not overpower the material such that it is spread in a non-guarded place. And regarding the Baghdadi mat, they said: amputation is obligatory in its theft due to the craftsmanship overpowering the original. And amputation is only obligatory in something not fixed, and it is only obligatory when it is light such that carrying it is not heavy for one person, because the heavy one is not desired for its theft.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the conditions for applying the hadd amputation for theft to manufactured wooden items and mats. The general rule is that amputation requires a valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) in proper custody (hirz). Here, the author addresses items made from wood such as utensils and doors. The key point is that industry (al-sun'ah) transforms the raw material into something valuable and guarded, so amputation applies. For example, wooden utensils and doors are carefully guarded, unlike ordinary mats which are spread even in unguarded areas. However, Baghdadi mats (high-quality) are an exception because the craftsmanship is dominant, so amputation applies. Additional conditions: the item must not be fixed (ghayr murakkab) and must be light enough for one person to carry. This reasoning is based on the Hanafi principle that the effective cause ('illa) for amputation is the taking of a guarded valuable, and these conditions ensure the item fits that description.

Key Terms
الصَّنْعَة (al-sun'ah): Craftsmanship, manufacturing; elevates an item's legal status regarding hirz and value.
الْحِرْز (al-hirz): Secure custody/place; condition for amputation.
الْمُرَكَّب (al-murakkab): Fixed or installed (e.g., a door attached to a wall). Theft of such does not incur amputation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is amputation not applied for theft of a heavy wooden door?
A) It is not valuable.
B) It is not guarded.
C) The craftsmanship is not dominant.
D) Heavy items are not desired for theft.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school imposes amputation for theft of manufactured items only when craftsmanship dominates, the item is not fixed, and it is light; otherwise, the hadd is not applied.

No Amputation for Trust-Breakers, Plunderers, and Snatchers
States exemption from hadd for three categories with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three categories exempt from hadd amputation and explain why the Hanafi school excludes them based on the absence of hirz and secrecy.

Core Idea
Amputation is only for secret theft from a guarded place; open taking or breach of trust does not trigger the hadd.

This passage from Al-Hidayah delineates the boundaries of the hadd punishment for theft. It explicitly lists three types of takers who are not subject to amputation: the betrayer of trust (khā'in), the plunderer (muntahib), and the snatcher (mukhtalis). The legal reasoning centers on the concept of hirz (custody) and the nature of the taking—these acts lack the secrecy and violation of a guarded place that define sariqa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Exempt Categories
Read the passage and list the three types of people who do not receive the hadd of amputation. Note their Arabic terms: khā'in, muntahib, mukhtalis.
Each category corresponds to a different mode of taking: betrayal, open force, and snatching.
2
Understand the Legal Reason (Illah)
The passage states 'due to deficiency in custody' (li qusurin fi al-hirz) and 'because he does his act openly' (yujāhiru bi-fi‘lihi). Identify that the common factor is the lack of secret taking from a guarded place.
Hirz refers to a secure location; secrecy is essential for sariqa.
3
Analyze the Hadith Proof
The author cites the Prophet's (ﷺ) statement: 'There is no amputation for a snatcher, nor for a plunderer, nor for a betrayer.' Recognize that this is a direct textual evidence (dalīl naqlī) that exempts these categories.
The hadith is ahad (zannī al-thubūt), but the Hanafi school relies on it as a specification of the general theft verse.
4
Distinguish from Sariqa
Compare these exempted acts with the definition of sariqa that incurs hadd: theft from a guarded place, done secretly, with the stolen item reaching the nisāb. Confirm that these categories fail one or more conditions.
This reinforces the Hanafi strictness in applying hadd only when all conditions are met.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا قَطْعَ عَلَىٰ خَائِنٍ وَلَا خَائِنَةٍ لِقُصُورٍ فِي الْحِرْزِ وَلَا مُنْتَهِبٍ وَلَا مُخْتَلِسٍ لِأَنَّهُ يُجَاهِرُ بِفِعْلِهِ كَيْفَ وَقَدْ قَالَ النَّبِيُّ -صلى الله عليه وسلم-: لَا قَطْعَ فِي مُخْتَلِسٍ وَلَا مُنْتَهِبٍ وَلَا خَائِنٍ
And no amputation is upon a male betrayer nor a female betrayer due to deficiency in custody, nor upon a plunderer nor a snatcher, because he does his act openly. Moreover, the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'There is no amputation for a snatcher, nor for a plunderer, nor for a betrayer.'
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof from the Sunnah that the hadd of amputation is not applied to certain categories of takers. The first category is al-khā'in (the betrayer), who is entrusted with property but then takes it dishonestly. The reason is that the hirz (custody) is deficient because the owner voluntarily gave access. The second is al-muntahib (the plunderer), who takes by force openly. The third is al-mukhtalis (the snatcher), who takes by surprise. The common factor is that these acts are not the secret taking from a guarded place that defines the hadd theft (sariqa). The hadith explicitly excludes them. The Hanafi school strictly applies the hadd only to stealthy theft from proper custody, and this passage reinforces that principle.

Key Terms
خَائِن (khā'in): One who betrays a trust; takes property entrusted to him.
مُنْتَهِب (muntahib): Plunderer; one who takes openly by force.
مُخْتَلِس (mukhtalis): Snatcher; one who takes quickly and runs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited in this lesson, which of the following is exempt from amputation?
A) A thief who breaks into a house at night and steals valuables.
B) A person who snatches a purse from someone's hand and runs.
C) A shoplifter who hides goods under clothes.
D) A burglar who picks a lock.

Key Takeaway
Amputation is reserved exclusively for secret theft (sariqa) from a guarded place; trust violations, open force, or snatching do not incur this hadd.

Amputation for the Grave-Robber: A Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute on amputation for grave-robber.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two positions within the Hanafi school on amputation for the nabbash and explain the role of shubhah in averting the hadd punishment.

Core Idea
Doubt in ownership prevents amputation for the grave-robber despite the shroud's value.

The grave-robber (nabbash) steals a shroud from a grave. Should his hand be cut? Within the Hanafi school, there is an internal dispute: Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad say no, while Abu Yusuf says yes (agreeing with al-Shafi'i). This lesson unpacks the proofs and the principle that hudood punishments are averted by doubts (al-hudood tudra'u bi al-shubuhat).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State the positions: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule no amputation; Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i rule amputation. Note which is mufta bihi.
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
2
List proofs for no amputation
Extract the evidence cited by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: the hadith 'La qat'a 'ala al-mukhtafi' (which in Madinan dialect means nabbash), the doubt (shubhah) in ownership because the deceased has no real ownership and the heir's ownership is superseded, and the rarity of the crime making deterrence ineffective.
The shubhah is the strongest pillar of their argument.
3
List proofs for amputation
Extract the evidence of Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i: the hadith 'Man nabasha qata'nahu' and the fact that the shroud is valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) kept in a secure place (hirz).
This makes the theft otherwise satisfy the conditions for hadd.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Understand how Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rebut the opposing evidence: the hadith of Abu Yusuf is not marfu' (not authentically traced to the Prophet) or is interpreted as a matter of siyasah (discretionary policy), not fixed hadd. The shubhah prevents the hadd.
The principle 'al-hudood tudra'u bi al-shubuhat' is applied.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its basis
Conclude that the relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. The key basis is the doubt in ownership, which overrides the other proofs.
Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah also prefers this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلاَ قَطْعَ عَلَى النَّبَّاشِ وَهَذَا عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَمُحَمَّدٍ وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ وَالشَّافِعِيُّ عَلَيْهِ الْقَطْعُ لِقَوْلِهِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ مَنْ نَبَشَ قَطَعْنَاهُ وَلأَنَّهُ مَالٌ مُتَقَوِّمٌ مُحْرَزٌ بِحِرْزِ مِثْلِهِ فَيَقْطَعُ فِيهِ وَلَهُمَا قَوْلُهُ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ لاَ قَطْعَ عَلَى الْمُخْتَفِي وَهُوَ النَّاسُ بِلُغَةِ أَهْلِ الْمَدِينَةِ وَلأَنَّ الشُّبْهَةَ تَمَكَّنَتْ فِي الْمِلْكِ لِأَنَّهُ لاَ مِلْكَ لِلْمَيِّتِ حَقِيقَةً وَلاَ لِلْوَارِثِ لِتَقَدُّمِ حَاجَةِ الْمَيِّتِ وَقَدْ تَمَكَّنَ الْخَلَلُ فِي الْمَقْصُودِ وَهُوَ الاِنْزِجَارُ لأَنَّ الْجِنَايَةَ فِي نَفْسِهَا نَادِرَةُ الْوُجُودِ وَمَا رَوَاهُ غَيْرُ مَرْفُوعٍ أَوْ هُوَ مَحْمُولٌ عَلَى السِّيَاسَةِ وَإِنْ كَانَ الْقَبْرُ فِي بَيْتٍ مُقَفَّلٍ فَهُوَ عَلَى الْخِلاَفِ فِي الصَّحِيحِ لِمَا قُلْنَا وَكَذَا إِذَا سَرَقَ مِنْ تَابُوتٍ فِي الْقَافِلَةِ وَفِيهِ الْمَيِّتُ لِمَا بَيَّنَّا
And there is no amputation upon the grave-robber (nabbash) – this is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i said: amputation is upon him due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever digs a grave (and steals the shroud), we will cut his hand.' And because it is a valuable property guarded in its appropriate guard, so amputation is incurred for it. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is his statement (peace be upon him): 'No amputation upon al-mukhtafi' – and he is the nabbash in the language of the people of Madinah. And because doubt in ownership arises, for the deceased has no real ownership, nor does the heir, due to the precedence of the deceased's need. And there is a defect in the objective, which is deterrence, because the crime itself is rare in occurrence. And what he (Abu Yusuf) narrated is not raised (to the Prophet) or is interpreted as a matter of discretionary policy. And if the grave is in a locked house, then it is according to the disagreement in the correct opinion for what we have said. Similarly, if he steals from a coffin in a caravan containing a deceased, due to what we have explained.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (with mention of Imam al-Shafi'i) regarding the application of the hadd amputation to a grave-robber (nabbash) who digs up a grave to steal the shroud. Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad hold that amputation is not applied. Their evidence includes: (1) a hadith (though not found in standard collections) stating no amputation for al-mukhtafi, which in Madinan dialect means nabbash; (2) a strong doubt (shubhah) regarding ownership – the deceased has no real ownership, and the heir's ownership is superseded by the deceased's need for the shroud; (3) the purpose of amputation is deterrence (inzijār), but this crime is so rare that the deterrent effect is negligible; (4) the hadith cited by Abu Yusuf is either not marfu' (not traced to the Prophet) or is interpreted as a matter of siyāsah (executive policy) rather than a fixed hadd. Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam al-Shafi'i argue for amputation. Their evidence: (1) a hadith 'Whoever digs a grave, we cut his hand' (reported by Bayhaqi); (2) the shroud is a valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) guarded in its appropriate location (the grave is considered hirz for a shroud). The text also notes that if the grave is inside a locked house, the same disagreement applies. Similarly, stealing a shroud from a coffin in a caravan involves the same ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
النَّبَّاش (al-nabbāsh): Grave-robber; one who digs graves to steal shrouds.
الْمُخْتَفِي (al-mukhtafi): In Madinan dialect, the grave-robber.
الشُّبْهَة (al-shubhah): Doubt that prevents the application of hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason given by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad for not cutting the hand of the nabbash?
A) The shroud is not valuable.
B) The grave is not a proper hirz.
C) There is doubt in ownership of the shroud.
D) The hadith says amputation is for siyāsah.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school averts the hadd amputation for the grave-robber due to doubt in ownership and the rarity of the crime, prioritizing the principle that hudood are averted by shubuhat.

The Grave Robber (Nabbash): Hand Amputation Dispute
Intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute on amputation for grave robber.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on amputation for the nabbash, identify the proofs from both sides, and explain the role of shubha and dialect in deriving the ruling.

Core Idea
A grave robber avoids amputation due to shubha in ownership and a Madinan dialect hadith.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute: should a grave robber's hand be amputated? The majority Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) says no, based on a hadith and the concept of shubha. Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i argue for amputation. We will dissect the arguments and see how a linguistic nuance and legal doubt shape the ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule regarding the nabbash: no amputation.
This is the mufta bihi view in the school.
2
Identify the opposing view
Who disagrees and on what basis? Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i hold that amputation applies.
Note that Abu Yusuf is an intra-Hanafi dissenter.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the hadith 'Man nabasha qata'nahu' and the qiyas argument that the shroud is protected property.
Also note the claim that it is a marfu' hadith.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Understand why Abu Hanifa and Muhammad reject the opponent's proof: the hadith 'La qat'a ala al-mukhtafi' refers to the nabbash in Madinan dialect, and there is shubha in ownership.
Also: the opponent's hadith may not be marfu' or is interpreted as siyasah.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical consequence? Hanafi: no hadd, only ta'zir. Shafi'i: hadd if nisab reached.
The difference impacts actual sentencing in court.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلا قطعَ عَلَى النَّبَّاشِ وَهَذَا عِنْدَابِي حَنِيفَةَ وَمُحَمَّدٍ وَقَالَ أَبُويُوسُفَ وَالشَّافِعِيُّ عَلَيْهِ الْقَطْعُ لِقَوْلِهِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ مَنْ نَبَشَ قَطَعْنَاهُ وَلاَنَّهُ مَالٌ مُتَقَوَّمٌ مُحْرَزٌ بِحِرْزِ مِثْلِهِ فَيَقْطَعُ فِيهِ وَلَهُمَا قَوْلُهُ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ لَا قَطعَ عَلَى الْمُخْتَفِى وَهُوَ النَّاسُ بِلُغَةِ اَهْلِ الْمَدِينَةِ وَلَانَّ الشَّبُهَةَ تَمَكَّنَتْ فِى الْمِلْكِ لِأَنَّهُ لَا مِلْكَ لِلْمَيِّتِ حَقِيقَة وَلَا لِلْوَارِثِ لِتَقَدُّمِ حَاجَةِ الْمَيِّتِ وَقَدْ تَمَكَّنَ الْخَلَلُ فِي الْمَقْصُودِ وَهُوَا لْانْزِ جَارُ لِاَنَّ الْجَنَايَةَ فِي نَفْسِهَا نَادِرَةُ الْوُجُودِ وَمَارَوَاهُ غَيْرُ مَرْفُوعٍ اَوْهُوَ مَحْمُولٌ عَلَى السِّيَاسَةِ وَإِنْ كَانَ الْقَبْرُ فِي بَيْتٍ مُقَفَّلٍ فَهُوَ عَلَى الْخِلَافِ في الصَّحِيحَ لِمَا قُلْنَا وَكَذَا إِذَا سَرَقَ مِنْ تَابُوتٍ فِى الْقَافِلَةِ وَفِيهِ الْمَيِّتُ لِمَا بَيَّنَّا
There is no amputation on the grave robber, and this is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i said: Amputation is upon him, due to his statement عليه السلام: 'Whoever robs a grave, we cut him off' and because it is valuable property guarded in a similar guard, so amputation is applied. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is his statement عليه السلام: 'No amputation on al-mukhtafi' and he is the nabbash in the language of the people of Madinah, and because the doubt in ownership exists, since the deceased has no ownership in reality, nor does the heir, due to the precedence of the deceased's need, and there is an irregularity in the purpose (deterrence) because the crime itself is rare in occurrence, and what they narrated is not marfu' or is carried on policy. And if the grave is in a locked room, then it is of the same dispute in the sahih, because of what we said, and likewise if he steals from a coffin in a caravan, with the deceased inside, because of what we have explained.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute regarding the amputation of a grave robber (nabbash). The relied-upon position of the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad: the hand of a grave robber is not cut, even if the value of the shroud reaches ten dirhams. Their proof includes the hadith 'La qat'a ala al-mukhtafi,' which they interpret as referring to the grave robber in the dialect of Madinah, and the argument that there is a legal doubt (shubha) in ownership—the deceased does not own the shroud, and the heir's ownership is superseded by the deceased's need for covering. Additionally, the crime is rare, so the deterrent purpose is undermined. The opposing view, held by Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, is that amputation is obligatory based on the hadith 'Man nabasha qata'nahu' and qiyas on other protected property. The text also notes that if the grave is in a locked room or the theft occurs from a coffin in a caravan, the same disagreement applies. The later commentary of 'Ayni indicates that the hadith of Abu Yusuf is stronger.

Key Terms
النَّبَّاش (al-nabbāsh): A grave robber who digs up graves and steals the shroud from the deceased.
الْمُخْتَفِي (al-mukhtafī): In the dialect of Madinah, this term refers to the nabbash (grave robber).
الشَّبْهَة (al-shubhah): A legal doubt that prevents the application of hadd punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the shroud is not valuable enough
B) Because there is a shubha (doubt) in ownership and the hadith 'La qat'a ala al-mukhtafi'
C) Because the grave robber is not considered a thief
D) Because the deceased's family does not complain

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not amputate the grave robber's hand due to shubha in ownership and the prophetic hadith interpreted in the Madinan dialect.

Theft from Bayt al-Mal and Joint Property: No Hadd Amputation
Ruling on amputation for theft from public treasury and shared property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two cases where the hadd of amputation is not applied due to shubha of ownership.

Core Idea
Shubha of ownership in stolen property prevents the hadd amputation.

This ruling clarifies an important exception to the law of theft: when the thief has a right or share in the stolen property, the severe hadd punishment is not applied. The Hanafi school protects the sanctity of hudud by applying them only when no doubt exists. Here we examine two key scenarios: theft from the public treasury and theft from jointly owned property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two exceptions
Read the Arabic text and note the two cases where amputation is not carried out: theft from bayt al-mal and from property in which the thief has a partnership.
2
Understand the underlying principle
The reason given is that the thief is part of the public (for bayt al-mal) or has a share in the property. This creates a shubha (doubt) about full ownership, which blocks the hadd.
Hadd punishments are warded off by shubhat (doubt).
3
Apply the ruling to new cases
Consider any situation where the thief has some right in the stolen property. For example, an employee stealing from company funds where he has a profit share. The same principle applies.
The shubha must be genuine and related to ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا يُقْطَعُ السَّارِقُ مِنْ بَيْتِ الْمَالِ لِأَنَّهُ مَالُ الْعَامَةِ وَ هُوَ مِنْهُمْ وَلَا مِنْ مَالٍ لِلسَّارِقِ فِيْهِ شِرْكَةٌ لِمَا قُلْنَا
And the thief who steals from the public treasury is not amputated because it is the wealth of the general public and he is among them; and neither (is he amputated) from wealth in which the thief has a partnership, because of what we said.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a straightforward ruling: amputation is not applied in two cases. First, if a person steals from the public treasury (bayt al-mal), because the treasury belongs to all Muslims, and the thief himself is part of that public, so there is a share of ownership for him, creating a shubha. Second, if a person steals from property in which he himself has a partnership, again due to the shubha of ownership. This is a clear application of the principle that hadd punishments are warded off by shubhat.

Key Terms
بَيْتُ الْمَالِ (bayt al-māl): The public treasury of the Muslim state, belonging collectively to all Muslims.
شِرْكَة (shirkah): Partnership or shared ownership in property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The theft was not premeditated.

Key Takeaway
Hadd amputation is not applied when the thief has any ownership share in the stolen property, based on the principle of shubha.

Creditor Stealing from Debtor: Hadd and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Detailed rulings on creditor theft and amputation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on amputation for a creditor who steals from the debtor and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding goods.

Core Idea
A creditor who steals the exact amount owed is not a thief; hadd is averted by recovery of right.

This lesson examines a nuanced area of Hanafi criminal law: when a creditor takes property from a debtor without permission. The key distinction is between taking the same currency owed (considered recovery of right) and taking goods (considered theft unless claimed as repayment). We will also explore the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Imām Abū Ḥanīfa and Imām Abū Yūsuf on the latter point.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and core ruling
Read the passage and identify the first scenario: a creditor owed dirhams steals the same amount from the debtor. Determine the ruling: no amputation.
The ruling applies whether the debt is immediate or deferred.
2
Understand the application of istihsān
The ruling for same-currency theft is based on istihsān. Why? Because the creditor is effectively recovering his own right, so the act is not considered theft under the hadd penalty.
Istihsān overrides strict qiyās that would otherwise classify this as theft.
3
Apply the rule for stealing more than owed
If the creditor steals more than his due, he still is not amputated because he becomes a co-owner of the stolen amount to the extent of his right. The excess is considered disputed, creating shubha (doubt) that averts hadd.
This ruling parallels the principle that hadd is averted by any valid doubt.
4
Contrast with stealing goods and the dispute
If the creditor steals goods (ʿurūḍ) instead of currency, Abū Ḥanīfa holds he is amputated because he has no authority to take goods without the debtor's consent. Objection from Abū Yūsuf: some scholars allow taking goods as repayment, so there is shubha. The text rejects Abū Yūsuf's view as lacking clear evidence.
The practical difference: amputation (Abū Ḥanīfa) vs. no amputation (Abū Yūsuf). The relied-upon position (muftā bihī) is Abū Ḥanīfa's view regarding goods.
5
Analyze the ikhtilāf for different currency
If the debt is in dirhams but the creditor steals dinars, there is ikhtilāf: some say cut because dinars are not his right; others say no cut because currencies are one genus. This shows how the school deals with doubts in applying hadd.
This ruling underscores the general Hanafi tendency to avert hudūd when ambiguity exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ لَهُ عَلَى اخَرَ دَرَاهِمَ فَسَرَقَ مِنْهُ مِثْلَهَا لَمْ يُقْطَعُ لِأَنَّهُ اِسْتِيْفَاء لِحَقِهِ وَالْحَالُ وَالْمُؤَجَّلُ فِيْهِ سَوَاءٌ إِسْتِحْسَانًا لانَّ النَّاجِيلَ لِتَاَخْرِ الْمُطَالَبَةِ وَكَذَا إِذَا سَرَقَ زِيَادَةِ عَلَى حَقِّهِ لِأَنَّهُ بِمِقْدَارٍ حَقِهِ يَصِيْرُ شَرِيكَا فِيهِ وَإِنْ سَرَقَ مِنْهُ عُرُوضًا قُطِعَ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ لَهُ وِلَايَةُ الْإِسْتِنْفَاءِ مِنْهُ إِلَّا بَيْعَا بِالتَرَاضِي وَعَنْ أَبِي يُوسُفٌ إِنَّهُ لَا يَقْطَعُ لاَنَّ لَهُ أَنْ يَأْخُذَهُ عِنْدَ بَعْضِ الْعُلَمَاءِ قَضَاءً مِنْ حَقِهِ أَوْرَهْنَّا مِنْ حَقِهِ قُلْنَا هَذَا قَوْلٌ لَا يُسْتَنَدُ إِلَى دَلِيْلٍ ظَاهِرٍ فَلَا يُعْتَبَرُ بِدُونِ إِتِصَالِ الدَّعْوَى بِهِ حَتَّى لَوِ ادَّعَى ذَالِكَ دُرِى عَنْهُ الْحَدُّ لِاَنَّهُ ظَنِّ فِي مَوْضِعِ الْخِلَافِ وَلَوْ كَانَ حَقَّهُ دَرَاهِمْ فَسَرَقَ مِنْهُ دَنَانِيرَ قِيلَ يُقْطَعُ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ لَهُ حَقُّ الْأَحْدِ وَقِيلَ لَا يُقْطَعُ لَانَّ التَّقُوْدَ جنسٌ وَاحِدٌ
And whoever has a claim of dirhams on another, and then steals the equivalent from him, he is not cut because it is recovery of his right; and the immediate and the deferred are the same in it, by istihsan, because the deferred is for delaying the demand. Likewise, if he steals more than his right, because he becomes a partner in it to the extent of his right. And if he steals goods from him, he is cut because he has no authority to exact payment from it except by mutual sale. And from Abu Yusuf: he is not cut because he may take it, according to some scholars, as a discharge of his right or as a pledge for his right. We said: this is a statement that is not based on clear evidence, so it is not considered without the claim being connected to it; even if he claims that, the hadd is averted from him because it is a presumption in a place of ikhtilaf. And if his right was dirhams and he stole dinars from him, it is said he is cut because he has no right to take, and it is said he is not cut because currencies are one genus.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage discusses multiple scenarios involving a creditor stealing from a debtor. The main ruling: if the creditor steals exactly the amount owed in the same currency (e.g., dirhams), he is not amputated because he is effectively recovering his own right. This ruling applies whether the debt is due immediately or deferred, by way of istihsan. If he steals more than his right, he still is not amputated because he becomes a co-owner of the stolen amount to the extent of his right. However, if he steals goods (uruud) instead of currency, he is amputated according to Abu Hanifa, because he has no authority to take goods without the debtor's consent. Abu Yusuf disagrees, holding that he is not amputated because some scholars allow the creditor to take goods in lieu of the debt. The text rejects Abu Yusuf's view as lacking clear evidence, but notes that if the creditor makes a claim (e.g., that he took it as repayment), the hadd is averted due to the ambiguity. Finally, if the debt is in dirhams and he steals dinars, there is ikhtilaf: some say cut because dinars are not his right, others say no cut because both are from the same genus (currency). The passage highlights the Hanafi principle that hadd is averted by any valid doubt.

Key Terms
إِسْتِحْسَان (istihsān): A juristic preference that overrides strict analogy, used here to allow a creditor to take the exact amount owed without being considered a thief.
عُرُوض (ʿurūḍ): Goods or commodities other than cash (dirhams/dinars).
النَّاجِيلَ (al-nājīl (likely intended: al-muʾajjal)): Deferred payment or debt due at a future date.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A creditor is owed dirhams and steals dinars from the debtor. What are the two views presented in the passage?
A) Both say no amputation because currencies are one genus.
B) One view says amputation, another says no amputation because currencies are one genus.
C) Both say amputation because dinars are not his right.
D) One view says no amputation, another says it depends on the amount.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a creditor is not amputated for stealing the exact currency owed, but is amputated for stealing goods unless he claims it as repayment; an intra-Hanafi dispute exists on the latter point.

Theft from Public Treasury: No Hadd Punishment
Ruling on theft from public treasury

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft from Bayt al-Mal and explain why the hadd penalty is not applied.

Core Idea
Theft from Bayt al-Mal does not incur hand-cutting because the thief's own share creates doubt.

In this lesson, we examine a concise ruling from the Hidayah that addresses the hadd punishment for theft from the public treasury. The Hanafi school exempts such theft from the amputation penalty based on a key principle: the thief himself has a proprietary interest in the Bayt al-Mal, which introduces a doubt (shubha) that averts the hadd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is stealing from the public treasury (Bayt al-Mal). The ruling is that the hand of the thief is not cut.
2
Understand the reason (ta'lil)
The text gives the reason: 'because it is the property of the general public.' This implies the thief also owns a share, creating a doubt in exclusive ownership.
In Hanafi usul, hadd punishments are averted by any doubt (shubha).
3
Apply the general principle
The principle 'al-hudud tudra'u bi al-shubuhat' (hudud are averted by doubts) is applied here. The shared ownership is a doubt, so the hadd is dropped.
This principle is derived from a hadith of the Prophet ﷺ.
4
Distinguish from regular theft
Regular theft from an individual's private property meets the conditions of hadd (exclusive ownership, protection). Here, the condition is missing.
Thus, the ruling does not contradict the general rule of cutting for theft.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ لَا يَقْطَعُ السَّارِقُ مِنْ بَيْتِ الْمَالِ لِأَنَّهُ مَالُ الْعَامَةِ
And the thief is not cut (his hand) for stealing from the public treasury because it is the property of the general public.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage states a ruling that the hand of a thief is not amputated if he steals from the Bayt al-Mal (public treasury). The reason given is that it is the property of the general public, not an individual. Since the thief himself has a share in this public property, the element of protected property (hifz) is compromised, and the hadd punishment is suspended due to this doubt (shubha). This ruling is based on the principle that hadd punishments are averted by doubts.

Key Terms
بَيْت الْمَال (bayt al-mal): Public treasury; property belonging to the general Muslim community.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because Bayt al-Mal is not protected property.
B) Because the thief also owns a share in it, creating doubt.
C) Because the value is less than nisab.
D) Because the penalty is replaced by tazir.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not amputate the hand for theft from public treasury because the thief's own share in the property creates a doubt that averts the hadd.

Creditor Taking Equivalent Amount: No Hadd
Ruling on creditor taking equivalent amount: no cutting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a creditor who takes an equivalent amount from the debtor without permission and explain why istihsan overrides qiyas to avoid hadd.

Core Idea
A creditor taking an equivalent amount from a debtor is not a thief because he is recovering his right, creating a shubha that blocks the hadd.

This passage addresses a common scenario in debt relationships: what if the creditor secretly takes an amount equal to the debt from the debtor's property? Strict analogy (qiyas) would classify this as theft (sariqa) and impose hand-cutting. However, Hanafi fiqh applies istihsan (juristic preference) to exempt the creditor, recognizing that the act is essentially a recovery of his due right (istifa'). The legal doubt (shubha) created by the existence of the right removes the certainty required for hudud punishments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the default ruling under qiyas
The act is a creditor secretly taking an amount equal to the debt from the debtor's possession. Under strict qiyas, this is theft (sariqa), which normally requires hand-cutting.
Recall that hadd al-sariqa requires: taking secretly, from a protected place, with ownership intent, etc.
2
Recognize the exception (istihsan) and its basis
The text rules 'no cutting' based on istihsan. The basis is that the creditor is exercising his right to recover what is owed to him (istifa').
The term 'istihsanan' in the Arabic signals that qiyas is abandoned for a more equitable ruling.
3
Understand the rationale: istifa' and shubha
The creditor's action is considered 'taking his right' (istifa' haqqih). This creates a legal doubt (shubha) because he has a valid ownership claim. Hudud are averted by shubuhat.
Hanafi principle: hudud are averted by doubts (al-hudud tudra' bi al-shubuhat).
4
Apply the same reasoning when the creditor takes more than his due
Even if he takes extra, he is not cut for the excess because to the extent of his right, he becomes a partner in the property. This partnership creates a shubha that blocks the hadd for the entire amount.
The shubha covers the whole property, so even the excess is protected by the doubt.
5
Note the mufta bihi and cross-madhhab status
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is that no cutting applies in this scenario. The ruling is mukhtalaf fih (disputed) with some other schools that may require cutting if permission is lacking.
Understanding this exception helps in distinguishing between theft and lawful recovery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ لَهُ عَلَى اخَرَ دَرَاهِمَ فَسَرَقَ مِنْهُ مِثْلَهَا لَمْ يُقْطَعُ لِأَنَّهُ اِسْتِيْفَاء لِحَقِهِ وَالْحَالُ وَالْمُؤَجَّلُ فِيْهِ سَوَاءٌ إِسْتِحْسَانًا لِأَنَّ الْأَجَلَ لِتَاَخْرِ الْمُطَالَبَةِ وَكَذَا إِذَا سَرَقَ زِيَادَةِ عَلَى حَقِّهِ لِأَنَّهُ بِمِقْدَارٍ حَقِهِ يَصِيْرُ شَرِيكَا فِيهِ
And whoever has dirhams owed to him by another and steals the like of them from him, he is not cut because it is taking his right. And the immediate and the deferred are equal in this by istihsan because the term is for postponement of demand. Likewise if he steals more than his right because to the extent of his right he becomes a partner in it.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage discusses a creditor who takes an equivalent amount from the debtor without permission. The ruling: no hand-cutting (hadd) because it is considered recovering his due right (istifa' haqqih). This applies whether the debt is due immediately or deferred, based on istihsan (juristic preference). The rationale: a deferred payment merely delays the demand; the right remains the same. Even if the creditor takes more than the debt, he is not cut for the extra portion because he becomes a partner in the stolen property to the extent of his right. This partnership creates a shubha that averts the hadd. The ruling is a relaxation of the strict qiyas that would otherwise require cutting.

Key Terms
اسْتِيْفَاء (istifa'): Recovery or receipt of what is legally due, here referring to the creditor taking his right by way of self-help.
شُبْهَة (shubha): Legal doubt or ambiguity that averts the application of hudud.
اِسْتِحْسَان (istihsan): Juristic preference, a method of reasoning that sets aside strict qiyas in favor of a more equitable ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a creditor steals more than his due amount from the debtor?
A) He is cut for the excess.
B) He is not cut because he becomes a partner in the property.
C) He is cut for the entire amount.
D) He is given tazir but not cut.

Key Takeaway
A creditor who takes an equivalent amount from the debtor is not subject to hadd al-sariqa because the act is considered recovery of a right (istifa') and creates a shubha that blocks the punishment, even if he takes more than his due.

Creditor Stealing Goods: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Hadd
Ruling on creditor stealing goods versus cash, with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi majority position on whether a creditor who steals goods from a debtor is subject to hadd, explain Abu Yusuf's dissenting view, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the dispute.

Core Idea
A creditor may not take goods without consent; theft of goods triggers hadd, but doubt from ikhtilaf may avert it.

This passage addresses a nuanced scenario in hudud where a creditor takes goods from his debtor. The majority Hanafi school insists on amputation, as the creditor lacks authority over goods. However, Abu Yusuf reported a view that no hadd applies, relying on permissibility from some scholars. Understanding this dispute is crucial for identifying how doubt (shubha) from scholarly disagreement can avert punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What did the creditor do? He stole goods (uruud) from the debtor. What is the majority ruling? He is subject to amputation (hadd).
The act is theft, but the context of the debt creates a potential shubha.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Abu Yusuf reports a view that the creditor is not cut. This is a minority position within the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the opposing dalil
Abu Yusuf's basis: some scholars permit the creditor to take goods as payment or pledge (qada'an min haqqihi aw rahnan).
4
Follow the Hanafi response
The text rejects this as lacking clear evidence (dalil zahir), but acknowledges that if the creditor raises such a claim, hadd is averted because it creates a doubt (shubha) in a point of dispute.
This response balances the strength of the majority view with the reality of scholarly ikhtilaf.
5
Examine the subsidiary issue
If the debt is in dirhams and he steals dinars, there are two opinions: cut (because he has no right to dinars) or no cut (because currencies are of the same genus).
This further illustrates how genus and permission affect the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ سَرَقَ مِنْهُ عُرُوضًا قُطِعَ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ لَهُ وِلَايَةُ الْإِسْتِيفَاءِ مِنْهُ إِلَّا بَيْعًا بِالتَّرَاضِي وَعَنْ أَبِي يُوسُفَ إِنَّهُ لَا يَقْطَعُ لِأَنَّ لَهُ أَنْ يَأْخُذَهُ عِنْدَ بَعْضِ الْعُلَمَاءِ قَضَاءً مِنْ حَقِهِ أَوْ رَهْنًا مِنْ حَقِهِ قُلْنَا هَذَا قَوْلٌ لَا يُسْتَنَدُ إِلَى دَلِيْلٍ ظَاهِرٍ فَلَا يُعْتَبَرُ بِدُونِ إِتِصَالِ الدَّعْوَى بِهِ حَتَّى لَوِ ادَّعَى ذَلِكَ دُرِئَ عَنْهُ الْحَدُّ لِأَنَّهُ ظَنِّ فِي مَوْضِعِ الْخِلَافِ وَلَوْ كَانَ حَقَّهُ دَرَاهِمَ فَسَرَقَ مِنْهُ دَنَانِيرَ قِيلَ يُقْطَعُ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ لَهُ حَقُّ الْأَخْذِ وَقِيلَ لَا يُقْطَعُ لِأَنَّ النُّقُودَ جِنْسٌ وَاحِدٌ
But if he steals from him goods, he is cut because he has no authority to take from him except by mutual sale. And from Abu Yusuf that he is not cut because for him there is, according to some scholars, the right to take it as payment of his debt or as collateral for his debt. We said: this is a statement not based on a clear evidence, so it is not considered unless the claim is accompanied by it; so if he claims that, the hadd is averted from him because it is a conjecture in a point of disagreement. And if his right was dirhams and he stole from him dinars, it is said he is cut because he has no right to take it; and it is said he is not cut because coins are the same type.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a creditor steals goods (uruud) from the debtor. The majority Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) holds that amputation applies because the creditor has no authority to take such property without consent; the only lawful method is mutual sale. However, Imam Abu Yusuf reports a position that no amputation is required because some scholars permit the creditor to take goods as payment or pledge. The text rejects this view as lacking clear evidential support, but acknowledges that if the creditor raises such a claim, the hadd may be averted due to the doubt (shubha) arising from ikhtilaf (scholarly disagreement). The passage also mentions a subsidiary issue: if the debt is in dirhams and he steals dinars, there are two opinions: one says amputation because he has no right to dinars, another says no amputation because currencies are of the same genus.

Key Terms
عُرُوض (uruud): Goods, commodities, non-monetary items.
وِلَايَة الْإِسْتِيفَاء (wilayat al-istifa'): Authority to take one's due without permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because goods are not protected property.
B) Because the creditor has no authority to take goods without mutual sale.
C) Because the value exceeds nisab.
D) Because the debtor does not consent.

Key Takeaway
A creditor may not take goods from the debtor without mutual sale; theft triggers hadd unless a claim of right creates doubt (shubha) due to ikhtilaf.

Second Theft of Same Item: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Ruling on re-theft of same item after amputation, Hanafi vs Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school does not impose amputation for a second theft of the same item and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A second theft of the same item after amputation is not punished because the item's inviolability is permanently diminished by the first cutting.

This lesson examines a nuanced dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding a thief who steals the same item twice. The Hanafi ruling, based on the concept of ismat al-mahall (inviolability of the item), holds that the second theft does not incur amputation due to a lingering doubt (shubha). The Shafi'i school, citing a hadith and the completeness of the second act, argues for amputation. By studying this dispute, you will understand how the Hanafi school uses shubha to avert hudud punishments and how it interprets the relevant proof texts.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling given in the passage: the thief is not cut for the second theft of the same item. Note the intra-Hanafi difference (Abu Yusuf has a narration favoring cut).
2
Identify the opposing school and their proof
Imam al-Shafi'i (and a riwaya from Abu Yusuf) holds that the second theft does incur amputation. Their evidence: the hadith 'فَإِنْ عَادَ فَاقْطَعُوهُ' (if he returns, cut him) and the argument that the second theft is complete and even worse due to the deterrent having failed.
Also note the analogy to a case where the owner sells the item to the thief then buys it back.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: the first amputation caused the item's inviolability (isma) to fall. Returning the item to the owner only partially restores it, leaving a shubha (doubt) because the owner, item, and the already applied punishment remain the same. This shubha averts the hadd.
The Hanafi school also argues that such repetition is rare, so the deterrent purpose of the hadd is not achieved.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a Hanafi mufti would not amputate for the second theft of the same item; a Shafi'i mufti would. This difference arises from the different weight given to shubha and the interpretation of the hadith.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi (relied upon) in the school.
5
Reflect on the guiding principle
The key principle here is that hudud punishments are averted by any doubt (shubha). The Hanafi school applies this broadly, while the Shafi'i school restricts it when the act is clearly repeated.
This case illustrates how usul al-fiqh principles like istihsan and interpretation of 'amm (general) texts operate in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ سَرَقَ عَيْنًا فَقُطِعَ فِيهَا فَرُدَّهَا ثُمَّ عَادَ فَسَرَقَهَا وَهِيَ بِحَالِهَا لَمْ يُقْطَعُ وَالْقِيَاسُ أَنْ يُقْطَعَ وَهُوَ رِوَايَةٌ عَنْ أَبِي يُوسُفَ وَهُوَ قَوْلُ الشَّافِعِيِّ لِقَوْلِهِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ فَإِنْ عَادَ فَاقْطَعُوهُ مِنْ غَيْرِ فَضْلٍ وَلِأَنَّ الثَّانِيَةَ مُتَكَامِلَةٌ كَالْأُولَى بَلْ أَقْبَحُ لِتَقَدُّمِ الزَّاجِرِ وَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا بَاعَهُ الْمَالِكُ مِنَ السَّارِقِ ثُمَّ اشْتَرَاهُ مِنْهُ ثُمَّ كَانَتِ السَّرَقَةُ وَلَنَا أَنَّ الْقَطْعَ أَوْجَبَ سُقُوطَ عِصْمَةِ الْمَحَلِّ عَلَى مَا يُعْرَفُ مِنْ بَعْدُ إِنْ شَاءَ اللهُ تَعَالَى وَبِالرَّدِّ إِلَى الْمَالِكِ إِنْ عَادَتْ حَقِيقَةُ الْعِصْمَةِ بَقِيَتْ شُبْهَةُ السُّقُوطِ نَظَرًا إِلَى اتِّحَادِ الْمِلْكِ وَالْمَحَلِّ وَقِيَامِ الْمُوجَبِ وَهُوَ الْقَطْعُ فِيهِ بِخِلَافِ مَا ذُكِرَ لِأَنَّ الْمِلْكَ قَدِ اخْتَلَفَ بِاخْتِلَافِ سَبَبِهِ وَلِأَنَّ تَكْرَارَ الْعِنَايَةِ مِنْهُ نَادِرٌ لِتَحَمَّلِهِ مُشَقَّةَ الزَّاجِرِ فَتَعْرَى الْإِقَامَةُ عَنِ الْمَقْصُودِ وَهُوَ تَقْلِيلُ الْجِنَايَةِ وَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا قَذَفَ الْمَحْدُودُ فِي الْقَذْفِ الْمَقْذُوفَ الْأَوَّلَ
And whoever steals an item and is cut (hand) for it, then returns it, then again steals it while it is in the same state, he is not cut. The analogy (qiyas) dictates that he should be cut, and that is a narration from Abu Yusuf and the statement of al-Shafi'i due to his (the Prophet's) saying: 'if he returns, then cut him without delay' and because the second (theft) is complete like the first, indeed worse because of the precedent of the deterrent, and it is like when the owner sells it to the thief then buys it from him then the theft occurs. And for us: the cutting caused the inviolability (isma) of the item to fall, as will be known later; and by returning to the owner, the reality of isma returns, but the doubt of its falling remains considering the unity of ownership and the item and the existence of the cause (i.e., cutting). Unlike what was mentioned because ownership changed due to different causes, and because repetition of the crime from him is rare given he has suffered the hardship of the deterrent, so the establishment (of the punishment) is stripped of the intent, which is reduction of crime, and it becomes like when the one who was punished for qadhf commits qadhf against the same person he first slandered.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding a thief who steals the same item twice after having been amputated for the first theft and then returned the item. The Hanafi position (Imam Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) holds that the second theft does not incur amputation. The opposing view (Imam Shafi'i and a narration from Abu Yusuf) says it does, citing the Prophet's hadith 'if he returns, cut him' and arguing the second theft is complete and worse. The Hanafi response: the first amputation removes the inviolability (isma) of the item; returning it to the owner restores its apparent isma but a lingering doubt remains due to the unity of owner, item, and the already applied punishment. This doubt (shubha) averts the hadd. Additionally, the rarity of such repetition makes the deterrent purpose unachievable. The analogy is to a person who, after being punished for false accusation (qadhf), accuses the same person again: he is not punished a second time.

Key Terms
عِصْمَة الْمَحَلّ (ismat al-mahall): The inviolability of the item (property) that makes it protected for purposes of hadd.
شُبْهَة (shubha): Doubt or uncertainty that averts the application of hadd punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view, why is a thief not cut for stealing the same item a second time after being amputated?
A) Because the second theft is not intentional.
B) Because the item's inviolability (isma) is gone and doubt remains.
C) Because the value is below the nisab.
D) Because the thief is not a repeat offender.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not amputate for a second theft of the same item because the first amputation permanently diminishes the item's inviolability, leaving a shubha that averts the hadd.

Second Theft After Item Changes: Cutting Applies
Ruling on second theft after item changes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a physical transformation of the stolen item removes the shubha and thus the second theft incurs the hadd of amputation.

Core Idea
When a stolen item changes form, the second theft incurs cutting because the change removes the doubt of inviolability.

In hudud, if a thief steals an item, is punished, then the item is returned and later stolen again, normally no cutting applies due to shubha (doubt) that the inviolability of the item has been compromised. However, if the item undergoes a physical transformation (taghayyur), such as yarn woven into fabric, the change eliminates the shubha, making the item legally a new entity. This lesson examines this exception and its underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
Determine the act is a second theft of the same physical matter. The first theft was already punished with cutting.
Focus on the fact that the item was returned after the first punishment.
2
Check for taghayyur (change)
Determine whether the stolen item has undergone a physical transformation (e.g., from yarn to fabric, or raw material to finished product). Minor changes like wear or repair do not qualify.
The change must be substantial enough to legally consider the item a new entity.
3
Apply the ruling based on change
If taghayyur occurred, the shubha that would otherwise prevent cutting is removed, and the second theft incurs hadd. If no change, the previous ruling of no cutting for the same item applies.
The key is that the item's identity changed, not just its ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ فَإِنْ تَغَيَّرَتْ عَنْ حَالِهَا مِثْلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ غَزْلًا فَسَرَقَهُ وَقَطَعَ فَرَدَّهُ ثُمَّ نَسَجَ فَعَادَ فَسَرَقَهُ قُطِعَ لِأَنَّ الْعَيْنَ
He said: if it changes from its state, such as if it was spun yarn, he stole it and was cut, then returned it, then he wove it and then stole it, he is cut because the item (is no longer the same).
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage states an exception to the previous rule. If the stolen item undergoes a physical transformation (e.g., yarn woven into fabric), then a second theft of the same physical matter in new form does incur amputation. The reason is that the change eliminates the previous shubha (doubt) that was based on the unity of the item. Now, due to transformation, the item is legally considered a new entity, restoring full inviolability (isma). This aligns with the Hanafi principle that a change in substance or form removes the previous protection and the doubt.

Key Terms
تَغَيُّر (taghayyur): Change in the physical state or form of the stolen property that makes it legally a new entity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example from the passage, what happens to the thief who steals the woven fabric after having been cut for stealing the yarn?
A) He is not cut because it is the same material.
B) He is cut because the item changed.
C) He receives ta'zir.
D) The ruling depends on the value of the fabric.

Key Takeaway
Taghayyur (physical transformation) of the stolen item removes the shubha, making a second theft of that item subject to the hadd of amputation.

Repeated Theft of Same Item After Hand Cut
Ruling on recutting hand when thief steals the same item again

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on recutting the hand for a second theft of the same unchanged item, explain the shubha that prevents it, and evaluate the opposing Shafi'i argument.

Core Idea
A prior cut creates a shubha that prevents recutting for the same unchanged item.

In this passage from Al-Hidayah, a nuanced hudud ruling is explored: if a thief steals a specific item, has his hand cut, the item is returned, and then he steals the same item again while it remains unchanged, the Hanafi school rules that the hand is not cut a second time. This is based on the principle of shubha (doubt) and istihsan (juristic preference), countering the apparent qiyas (analogy) that would require recutting. The passage contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Imam al-Shafi'i and a narration from Abu Yusuf, providing a rich example of inter-madhhab legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling: the hand is not recut when the stolen item is unchanged after return. Note the key condition: the item must be in its same state.
The ruling is based on istihsan overriding qiyas.
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i and a narration from Abu Yusuf hold that the hand should be recut. Their main proof is the general hadith: 'If he returns, cut his hand.'
They also argue by analogy with a case where the item changes ownership through sale.
3
Follow the opponent's dalil
Examine the opponent's evidence: the hadith is general, and the second theft is even more blameworthy due to the prior deterrent. The analogy with the sale-purchase case suggests recutting.
The opponent sees no shubha because the hadith is unconditional.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal: the first cutting caused the fall of the item's inviolability (isma). Even after return, a shubha remains due to unity of ownership, item, and the prior cut. This shubha blocks the hadd. Additionally, the purpose of hadd (deterrence) is not served by repeating.
The Hanafi school qualifies the general hadith by the presence of shubha.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: a Hanafi judge will not recut, while a Shafi'i judge will. Also note the exception: if the item changes form (e.g., yarn woven into cloth), the shubha disappears and both schools would recut.
This highlights how a slight factual change alters the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ سَرَقَ عَيْنًا فَقُطِعَ فِيهَا فَرُدَّهَا ثُمَّ عَادَ فَسَرَقَهَا وَهِيَ بِحَالِهَا لَمْ يُقْطَعُ وَالْقِيَاسُ أَنْ يُقْطَعَ وَهُوَ رِوَايَةٌ عَنْ أَبِي يُوسُفَ وَهُوَقَوْلُ الشَّافِعِيِّ لِقَوْلِهِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ فَإِنْ عَادَ فَاقْطَعُوهُ مِنْ غَيْرِ فَضْلٍ وَلَانَّ الثَّانِيَةَ مُتَكَامِلَةٌ كَا لَّأُولَى بَلْ أَقْبَحُ لِتَقَدُّمِ الزَّاجِرِ وَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَابَاعَهُ الْمَالِكُ مِنَ السَّارِقِ ثُمَّ اشْتَرَاهُ مِنْهُ ثُمَّ كَانَتِ السَّرَقَةُ وَلَنَا أَنَّ الْقَطْعَ اَوْجَبَ سُقُوطَ عِصْمَةِ الْمَحَلَّ عَلَى مَا يُعْرَفْ مِنْ بَعْدُاِنْ شَاءَ اللهُ تَعَالَى وَبِالرَّةِ إِلَى الْمَالِكِ إِنْ عَادَتْ حَقِيقَةُ الْعِصْمَةِ بَقِيَتْ شُبْهَةُ السُّقوط نظرا إلى اِتِّحَادِ الْمِلْكِ وَالْمَحَلَّ وَقِيَامِ الْمُوْجَبِ وَهُوَ الْقَطْعُ فِيهِ بِخِلَافِ مَاذُكِرَ لِاَنَّ الْمِلْكَ قَدِ اخْتَلَفَ بِاخْتِلافِ سَبَبِهِ وَلَانَّ تَكْرَارَ الْعِنَايَةِ مِنْهُ نَادِرٌ لِتَحَمَّلِهِ مُشَقَّةُ الزَّاجِرِ فَيُعْرَى الْإِقَامَةُ عَنِ الْمَقْصُوْدِ وَهُوَ تَقْلِيلُ الْجِنَايَةِ وَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا قَذَفَ الْمَحْدُودُ فِي الْقَذْنِ الْمَقْذُوقَ الْأَوَّلَ قَالَ فَإِنْ تَغَيَّرَتْ عَنْ حَالِهَا مِثْلُ أنْ يَكُونَ غَزْلًا فَسَرَقَهُ وَقَطَعَ فَرَدَّهُ ثُمَّ نَسَجَ فَعَادَ فَسَرَقَهُ قُطِعَ لِأَنَّ الْعَيْنَ قَدْ تَبَدَّلَتْ وَلِهَذَا يَمْلِكُهُ الْغَاصِبُ بِهِ وَهَذَا هُوَ عَلَامَةُ التَّبَدُّلِ فِى كُلَّ مَحَلَّ وَإِذَا تَبَدَّلَ انْتَفَتِ الشُّبْهَةُ النَّاشِيَةُ مِنْ اِتِّحَادِ الْمَحَلَّ وَالْقَطْعِ فِيْهِ فَوَجَبَ الْقَطْعُ ثَانِيًا
And whoever steals a specific item and is cut for it, then returns it, then returns and steals it while it is in its same state, he is not cut. And the analogy is that he should be cut, and it is a narration from Abu Yusuf, and it is the saying of al-Shafi'i, because of his statement (peace be upon him): 'If he returns, then cut his hand' without surplus, and because the second (theft) is complete like the first, nay more ugly because of the previous deterrent, and it becomes like when the owner sells it to the thief, then buys it back from him, then the theft occurs. And for us (Hanafis) is that the cutting caused the fall of the inviolability (isma) of the property, as will be known later, and by its return to the owner, if the reality of inviolability returns, the doubt of its fall remains, considering the unity of ownership and the property and the existence of the cause, which is the cutting in it, unlike what was mentioned, because ownership has changed due to change of cause, and because repetition of the act from him is rare due to his bearing the difficulty of the deterrent, so the punishment lacks its purpose, which is reduction of crime, and it becomes like when the one punished for qadhf commits qadhf against the same person who was the first victim. He said: If it (the item) changes from its state, such as if it was yarn, and he stole it and was cut, and he returned it, then he wove it, and he returned and stole it, he is cut, because the specific item has changed, and therefore the usurper acquires ownership by it, and this is the sign of change in every case. And when it changes, the doubt arising from unity of the property and the cutting in it is removed, so the cutting becomes obligatory a second time.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute with intra-Hanafi variation. The ruling: If a thief steals a specific item, has his hand cut, the item is returned, and then he steals the same item again while it remains unchanged, the hand is not cut according to the Hanafi school. This is based on istihsan. Qiyas (analogy) would require recutting, and that is a narration from Abu Yusuf and the position of Imam al-Shafi'i, who cite the general hadith 'If he returns, cut his hand' and argue the second theft is even more blameworthy. The Hanafi position uses the principle that the first cutting removed the inviolability (isma) of the item; even though the item is returned, a doubt (shubha) remains due to the unity of ownership, item, and the prior cut. This doubt prevents the hadd. In contrast, if the item changes form (e.g., yarn woven into cloth), the item is considered new, the doubt is removed, and the hand is cut again. The author also provides another analogy (qiyas) for the unchanged case: if the owner sells the item to the thief and then buys it back, the ownership changes, so recutting would apply there. But in the unchanged case, ownership did not change, hence the doubt remains. The Hanafi reasoning also notes that a second theft by the same person is rare due to the deterrent effect of the first punishment, so the hadd is not re-imposed as it would not serve its purpose of reducing crime.

Key Terms
عصمة المحل (ʿiṣmat al-maḥall): Inviolability of the property: the legal protection that makes theft of it subject to hadd.
شبهة (shubha): A legal doubt that prevents the application of hadd punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school NOT cut the hand again if the thief steals the same item after being cut and the item is returned unchanged?
A) Because the hadd is only applicable once in a lifetime.
B) Because a shubha (doubt) remains due to unity of ownership, item, and prior cut.
C) Because the thief has repented.
D) Because the item is now worthless.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not recut the hand for stealing the same unchanged item because a shubha from the prior cut and unity of ownership prevents the hadd.

Theft from Mahram Relatives: The Shubha of Common Access
Ruling on hadd for theft from close relatives with proof and inter-madhhab difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on theft from mahram relatives and explain the underlying rationale of common access to the ḥirz.

Core Idea
No hadd for theft from mahram relatives: familiarity creates shubha.

This passage addresses a specific exception in the law of ḥadd al-sariqa: theft from close relatives. The Hanafi school holds that the hand is not cut when the victim is a parent, child, or any mahram relative (one whom the thief cannot marry due to blood relation). The rationale is that such relatives have natural access to each other's secure places (ḥirz), creating a doubt (shubha) that bars the ḥadd. We will explore the proof, the distinction from friends, and the Shafi'i disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states that the hand is not cut for theft from parents, children, or mahram relatives. This is the clear Hanafi position.
Mahram relatives include siblings, uncles, aunts, etc.
2
Understand the rationale for parents/children
Two reasons: (1) expansiveness in wealth – the child and parent share property naturally; (2) ease of entering each other's ḥirz without obstruction.
3
Understand the rationale for mahram relatives
Only the second reason applies: common access to ḥirz. This is supported by the Law's permission for mahram individuals to see visible adornment, indicating familiarity.
The ḥirz includes the home and secure storage.
4
Note the exception for friends
Two close friends are not exempt; theft from a friend results in cutting because the act of theft severs friendship and shows hostility.
Friends lack the blood relationship that creates shubha.
5
Recognize the inter-madhhab dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees regarding mahram relatives, comparing them to distant relatives. The Hanafi response references the earlier discussion in the Book of Emancipation where mahram status was clarified.
The practical difference: in a Hanafi court, no cutting; in a Shafi'i court, cutting applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَن سرق مِنْ أَبَوَيْهِ أَوْوَلَدِهِ أَوْذِى رَحْمٍ مَحْرَمٍ مِنْهُ لَمْ يُقْطَعُ فَالْاَوَّلُ وَهُوَ الْوِلَادُ لِلْبُسُوْطَةِ فِي الْمَالِ وَفِي الدُّخُولِ فِي الْحِرْزِ وَ الثَّانِي لِلْمَعْنَى الثَّانِي وَلِهَذَا اَبَاحَ الشَّرْعُ النَّظْرَ إِلَى مَوَاضِعِ الزِيْنَةِ الظَّاهِرَةِ مِنْهَا بِخِلَافِ الصَّدِيقَيْنِ لِأَنَّهُ عَادَاهُ بِالسَّرْقَةِ وَفِي الثَّانِي خِلَافُ الشَّافِعِيِّ لِأَنَّهُ اَلْحَقَهَا بِالْقَرَابَةِ الْبَعِيدَةِ وَقَدْ بَيَّنَّاهُ فِي الْعِتَاقِ
And whoever steals from his parents or his child or a dhū raḥim maḥram (blood relative within prohibited degrees) from him, he is not cut. The first (parents and children) is because of expansiveness in wealth and in entering the secure place (ḥirz). And the second (mahram relatives) is for the second meaning (i.e., entering the ḥirz). For this reason, the Law permitted looking at their visible adornment. Unlike two friends, because he (the thief) has become hostile by stealing. And in the second (issue of mahram relatives) there is a disagreement with al-Shafi'i, because he considered them like distant relatives. And we have explained this in the Book of Emancipation (al-ʿItāq).
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on hadd for theft from relatives. The Hanafi position: No hand is cut for theft from parents, children, or any mahram relative (those related by blood whom one cannot marry). The reason for parents and children is twofold: (1) the natural easiness in wealth (the child has access to parent's property and vice versa) and (2) the ease of entering each other's secure places (ḥirz). For mahram relatives (like siblings, uncles, aunts, etc.), only the second reason applies: they commonly enter each other's homes. The Law's permission for mahram individuals to see the visible adornment of their relatives indicates this familiarity. In contrast, two close friends (ṣadīqayn) do not have this ruling; if one steals from the other, the hand is cut because the act of theft severs the friendship and creates enmity. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees regarding mahram relatives, considering them like distant relatives, so he would cut the hand. The author notes this was explained earlier in the Book of Emancipation.

Key Terms
ذو رحم محرم (dhū raḥim maḥram): A blood relative whom one is prohibited from marrying (e.g., sibling, uncle, aunt).
الحرز (al-ḥirz): A secure place where property is kept, protected from theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school not cut the hand for theft from a mahram relative (like a sibling)?
A) Because siblings share property equally.
B) Because the Law permits looking at visible adornment of mahram relatives, indicating common access to ḥirz.
C) Because siblings are considered like parents.
D) Because theft between siblings is not a crime.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts theft from mahram relatives from the ḥadd due to the shubha of common access to the ḥirz, distinguishing them from friends and non-mahram kin.
