Theft and Ḥirz: Two Key Scenarios
Ruling on cutting hand for stealing another's property from a mahram's house

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cutting the hand for theft in two scenarios involving ḥirz and explain how shubha from access rights affects the penalty.

Core Idea
Stealing from a mahram's house creates shubha that blocks hadd, even if the property is another's.

This passage presents two contrasting cases that illustrate the Hanafi emphasis on ḥirz (secure place) and shubha (doubt) in applying the hadd penalty for theft. Understanding these cases clarifies how access rights affect the definition of theft.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and identify the two distinct cases: (1) stealing another's property from a mahram's house, (2) stealing one's own property from a stranger's house.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For scenario 1, the hand is not cut. For scenario 2, the hand is cut. Note the exact ruling from the text.
3
Identify the underlying principle
The key factor is the presence or absence of shubha (doubt) from the thief's access to the ḥirz (secure place). Access to a mahram's house creates shubha.
4
Apply to a new case
Given a new scenario (e.g., a grandmother's house, a friend's apartment), determine whether shubha exists based on the thief's relationship to the house owner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْسَرَقَ مِنْ بَيْتِ ذِى رَحْمٍ مَحْرَمٍ مَتَاعَ غَيْرِهِ يَنْبَغِي أَنْ لَا يُقْطَعَ وَلَوْسَرَقَ مَالَهُ مِنْ بَيْتِ غَيْرِهِ يُقْطَعُ اعْتِبَارًا لِلْحِرْزِ وَعَدَمِهِ
And if he steals from the house of a mahram relative the property of another, it is correct (yanbaghi) that he is not cut. And if he steals his own property from the house of another, he is cut, considering the presence or absence of ḥirz.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This is a concise ruling on two scenarios related to ḥirz (secure place). First: If a person steals the property of a third party from the house of a mahram relative, his hand is not cut. Why? Because the ḥirz (the house) belongs to a mahram relative, and there is shubha (doubt) due to the thief's common access to that house, even though the stolen property belongs to someone else. The shubha from the relationship to the house owner prevents hadd. Second: If a person steals his own property from the house of a stranger, his hand is cut. Because here, the thief took his own property, but the ḥirz is not his; he had no right to enter that house to retrieve his property without permission. Thus, the hadd applies because the theft is from a secure place to which he had no access right. This shows that ḥirz is considered in terms of the location's security, not just ownership of the item.

Key Terms
الحرز (al-ḥirz): The secure place from which theft is committed; its presence or absence affects the hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the hand cut for stealing one's own property from a stranger's house?
A) Because the property belongs to someone else.
B) Because the thief violated the stranger's secure place (ḥirz) without right.
C) Because the thief is a stranger to the owner.
D) Because the property was not in a secure place.

Key Takeaway
The presence or absence of ḥirz and the thief's right of access to it are decisive in determining the application of hadd for theft in Hanafi fiqh.

Effect of Property Transformation on Theft Punishment
Explains when cutting is required again after property transformation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a thief's hand must be cut again if the stolen property is transformed after being returned, and explain the reasoning based on the removal of shubha.

Core Idea
If stolen property changes form, the shubha that prevented a second cutting is removed, making the hadd obligatory again.

This lesson examines a ruling from Al-Quduri's matn on the effect of property transformation on the hadd punishment for theft. The key question: can a thief be punished again for stealing the same property after it has been returned and changed form? The Hanafi answer is yes, because transformation eliminates the legal identity of the original item, and thus the previous cutting no longer creates ambiguity (shubha).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is stealing property that was previously stolen and already punished by hand cutting. The ruling: cutting is wajib again if the property has changed its essential form.
The ruling is from the passage: 'وَوَجَبَ الْقَطْعُ ثَانِيًا' (cutting becomes obligatory a second time).
2
Locate the evidence
The evidence is the example of yarn (ghazl) stolen, hand cut, returned, then owner weaves it into cloth, and the same thief steals the cloth. The ruling of re-cutting is justified by analogy: transformation removes the shubha that arose from the unity of the object and the previous hadd.
The example illustrates 'tabaddul' (change) as the key factor.
3
Understand the reasoning (wajh al-dalalah)
The transformation (tabaddul) of the property erases its legal identity, so the new theft is considered a theft of a different object. The shubha that would have blocked a second cutting (because it is the same object) no longer exists.
Shubha is defined as an ambiguity that prevents hadd; here, the possibility that the same object was stolen twice creates shubha, but transformation removes it.
4
Note the cross‑madhhab status
The passage indicates this ruling is muttafaq alayh (agreed upon) within the Hanafi school; no intra‑Hanafi ikhtilaf is mentioned.
The ruling is from Zahir al‑Riwayah and is the mufta bihi position.
5
Apply the principle to a parallel case
The same principle is applied in liability: a usurper (ghasib) who changes the form of the property (e.g., yarn into cloth) is only liable for the original value, not the transformed product, because transformation severs legal identity.
This confirms that 'tabaddul' is a general sign of change in legal identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَدْ تَبَدَّلَتْ وَلِهَذَا يَمْلِكُهُ الْغَاصِبُ بِهِ وَهَذَا هُوَ عَلَامَةُ التَّبَدُّلِ فِى كُلَّ مَحَلَّ وَإِذَا تَبَدَّلَ انْتَفَتِ الشُّبُهَةُ النَّاشِيَةُ مِنْ اِتِّحَادِ الْمَحَلَّ وَالْقَطْعِ فِيْهِ فَوَجَبَ الْقَطْعُ ثَانِيًا ترجمہ قدوری نے کہا۔ اب اگر وہ مال جسے ایک مرتبہ چرایا گیا تھا پہلی حالت میں نہیں رہا بلکہ بدل گیا۔ مثلاً کسی نے سوت چرایا اور اس کی وجہ سے اس کا ہاتھ کاٹا گیا۔ پھر وہ سوت بھی مالک کو واپس کر دیا اور مالک نے اس سوت سے کپڑا بنوا لیا جسے اسی پہلے چور نے دوبارہ چرالیا تو اب اس کا ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا کیونکہ پہلا مال یعنی سوت اپنی حالت پر نہیں رہا بلکہ کپڑے سے بدل گیا ہے۔ اسی بناء پر اگر کوئی شخص کسی سے سوت چھین کر اس کا کپڑا بنوا لے۔ تو وہ صرف اس سوت کی قیمت کا ضامن ہوگا کپڑے کا ضامن نہ ہو گا۔ اسی طرح ہر جگہ تبدیلی کی یہی علامت ہے اس نے جگہ کے ایک ہونے اور سزا ہاتھ کاٹے جانے کے بعد بھی ایک ہی ہونے کی وجہ سے جو شبہ پیدا ہو گیا تھاوہ ختم ہو گیا اسی لئے قطع کی سزا دوبارہ واجب ہوگئی۔ تشریح . قَالَ فَإِنْ تَغَيَّرَتْ عَنْ حَالِهَا مِثْلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ غَزْلًا فَسَرْقة الخ مطلب ترجمہ سے واضح ہے۔
It has changed, and therefore the usurper owns it by transformation, and this is the sign of transformation in every context. When it has changed, the shubha (ambiguity) arising from the unity of the object and the cutting in it is removed, so cutting becomes wajib a second time. Translation: Al-Quduri said: Now if the property that was stolen once is no longer in its original state but has changed—for example, someone stole yarn and his hand was cut (as punishment). Then he returned the yarn to the owner, and the owner had cloth made from that yarn, which the same thief stole again. Then his hand will be cut because the first property, i.e., yarn, did not remain in its state but changed into cloth. Similarly, if someone grabs yarn from another and makes it into cloth, he is only liable for the value of the yarn, not the cloth. The same sign of change applies everywhere. This removed the shubha that had arisen due to the unity of the object and the fact that cutting had already occurred, so the punishment of cutting becomes wajib again. Tashrih: He said: If it changes from its state, such as if it was yarn and was stolen... The meaning is clear from the translation.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Al-Quduri's matn, explained in the commentary. The issue is whether a thief's hand can be cut again if the same property is stolen after having been returned and transformed. The ruling: if the stolen item has changed its essential form (e.g., yarn woven into cloth), the previous ambiguity (shubha) that prevented a second cutting due to the same object being stolen is removed. Therefore, cutting is obligatory again. The reasoning: transformation erases the identity of the original item, so the new theft is effectively of a different item. This principle is also applied in other areas, such as liability for usurped goods: a usurper who changes the form becomes liable for the original value only, not the transformed product. The Hanafi position is clear and relies on this rational analogy.

Key Terms
تبدل (tabaddul): Change in the essential form of property (e.g., yarn to cloth)
شبهة (shubhah): Ambiguity or legal impediment that prevents hadd punishment

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what condition allows a thief's hand to be cut again for stealing the same original property?
A) The property was returned to the owner
B) The property has changed its essential form
C) The thief repented
D) The owner forgave the thief

Key Takeaway
Transformation (tabaddul) of stolen property removes the shubha that prevented a second hadd, making the cutting punishment wajib again.

Theft from Mahram Relatives: The Hanafi Exception and Shafi'i Dispute
Ruling on no cutting for theft from mahram relatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft from mahram relatives and explain the basis for the Shafi'i disagreement.

Core Idea
Stealing from a mahram relative does not incur hand-cutting due to mutual access and shubha.

This passage distinguishes between theft from close relatives and theft from strangers. The Hanafi school rules that no hadd (hand-cutting) applies when a person steals from parents, children, or any mahram relative. This is because such relatives have open access to each other's property and homes, creating a legal ambiguity (shubha). The Shafi'i school disagrees and equates mahram relatives with distant relatives. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
List the relatives mentioned: parents, children, and mahram relatives. Note that parents and children are also mahram but are given separate mention.
Parents and children are also mahram, but the text distinguishes them for emphasis.
2
Understand the dual reasons for parents/children
The text gives two reasons (bisotah): freedom in wealth and freedom in entering protected places. Recognize that these create a shubha preventing hadd.
The first reason (bisotah fil mal) is unique to parents and children; the second reason (bisotah fil dukhul) extends to all mahram.
3
Note the evidence for mahram relatives
The passage cites the Shariah's permission to look at their adornment as proof of open access, supporting the second reason.
This evidence applies to all mahram, not just parents and children.
4
Identify the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i equates mahram relatives with distant relatives, so he applies cutting. Understand the basis: he does not see the same degree of closeness in access.
The Shafi'i view is that only parents and children are exempt, not other mahram.
5
Appreciate the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafis do not cut, Shafi'is do cut for theft from a mahram relative other than parents/children.
This difference significantly affects implementation of hudud.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحرز والاخذ منه ترجمه فصل حرز یعنی محفوظ جگہ اور اس میں سے لینے کا بیان۔ والدین، اولاد، قریبی رشتہ دار کا مال چوری کرنے سے ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا وَمَن سرق مِنْ أَبَوَيْهِ أَوْوَلَدِهِ أَوْذِى رَحْمٍ مَحْرَمٍ مِنْهُ لَمْ يُقْطَعُ فَالْاَوَّلُ وَهُوَ الْوِلَادُ لِلْبُسُوْطَةِ فِي الْمَالِ وَفِي الدُّخُولِ فِي الْحِرْزِ وَ الثَّانِي لِلْمَعْنَى الثَّانِي وَلِهَذَا اَبَاحَ الشَّرْعُ النَّظْرَ إِلَى مَوَاضِعِ الزِيْنَةِ الظَّاهِرَةِ مِنْهَا بِخِلَافِ الصَّدِيقَيْنِ لِأَنَّهُ عَادَاهُ بِالسَّرْقَةِ وَفِي الثَّانِي خِلَافُ الشَّافِعِيِّ لِأَنَّهُ اَلْحَقَهَا بِالْقَرَابَةِ الْبَعِيدَةِ وَقَدْ بَيَّنَّاهُ فِي الْعِتَاقِ ترجمہ اگر کسی نے اپنے والدین یا اپنی اولاد یاکسی ذی رحم محرم کے مال سے کوئی چیز چرائی تواس کا قطع نہیں ہو گا۔ اس لئے کہ والدین یا اولا د جن میں پیدائشی رشتہ داری ہے تو ان میں پہلی بات تو یہ ہے کہ ایک کو دوسرے کے مال سے لینے کی شرعا اجازت بھی ہوتی ہے اور دوسری بات یہ ہے کہ سیہ لوگ آپس میں ایک دوسرے کی محفوظ جگہوں میں آتے جاتے بھی ہیں اور ذی رحم محرم میں بھی یہ دوسری بات پائی جاتی ہے یعنی ایک دوسرے کی محفوظ جگہوں میں آمد و رفت رکھتے ہیں اسی بناء پر شریعت نے ان کی محرم عورتوں کی زینت کے ظاہری اعضاء کو دیکھنے کی اجازت بھی دی ہے ان سے پردہ نہیں رکھا ہے برخلاف دوستوں کے ( کہ اگر چہ یہ دوست ایک دوسرے کے کمروں میں آتے ہیں لیکن اگر یہ چوری کر لیں تو ان کا ہاتھ کاٹا جاتا ہے ) کیونکہ چوری کر لینے سے دوستی کی بجائے دشمنی بڑھا دیتے ہیں۔ پھر ذی رحم محرم قرابت داروں کے معاملہ میں امام شافعی رحمتہ اللہ علیہ کا اختلاف ہے۔ کیونکہ شافعی نے قرابت محرمہ کو دور کے رشتہ داروں سے ملایا ہے۔ اس بحث کو ہم کتاب العتاق میں بیان کر چکے ہیں۔ تشريح وَمَنْ سَرَقَ مِنْ اَبَوَيْهِ أَوْ وَلَدِهِ أَوْذِى رَحْمٍ مَحْرَمٍ مِنْهُ لَمْ يَقْطَعُ ... الخ مطلب ترجمہ سے واضح ہے۔
Section on protecting place (hiraz) and taking from it. Translation: Section on hiraz, i.e., protected place, and taking from it. Theft from parents, children, and close mahram relatives: hand will not be cut. And whoever steals from his parents or his child or a mahram relative of his is not cut. The first (parents/child) due to freedom in wealth and in entering the hiraz, and the second (mahram) for the second meaning. Therefore, the Law permitted looking at their apparent adornment, unlike friends, because he (the thief) enmities by theft. In the second (mahram) there is a disagreement with al-Shafi'i because he equated her with distant relatives, and we have explained this in the Book of Itaq. Translation: If someone steals from his parents, or his child, or any mahram relative, his hand will not be cut. Because parents and child with birth relationship have two reasons: first, one is permitted by Shariah to take from the other's wealth; second, such people enter each other's protected places. Mahram relatives also have the second quality, i.e., they have access to each other's protected places. For this reason, Shariah permitted looking at their apparent adornment without hijab, unlike friends (who may enter each other's rooms but if they steal, their hand is cut) because stealing turns friendship into enmity. Then regarding mahram relatives, Imam al-Shafi'i disagreed because he equated mahram with distant relatives. We have discussed this in Kitab al-Itaq. Tashrih: And whoever steals from his parents or his child or a mahram relative is not cut... the meaning is clear from the translation.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage opens a new section on hiraz (protected place) and then gives the ruling on theft from close relatives. The Hanafi ruling: no cutting for theft from parents, children, or any mahram relative. Two reasons are given: (1) for parents and children, there is mutual freedom in wealth and entry; (2) for mahram relatives, there is freedom of entry (as evidenced by permission to see adornments). Friends are distinguished because theft from them breaks friendship. The passage then notes Imam al-Shafi'i's disagreement: he equates mahram relatives with distant relatives and would apply cutting. The Hanafi view is based on established practice of open access and lack of strict guarding, preventing the theft from being considered a complete hadd violation.

Key Terms
حرز (hirz): A protected place where property is kept securely
ذو رحم محرم (dhu rahm mahram): A relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited due to blood or milk relationship
بسوطة (bisotah): Freedom or openness in dealing, having unrestricted access

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, always
B) No, never
C) Yes, if the relative is not a parent or child
D) No, except if the relative is a friend

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not impose hand-cutting for theft from mahram relatives due to the inherent ambiguity in access and permission.

Theft from a Relative's House: Hirz and Hadd
Distinguishes cutting for theft of another's property from relative's house vs. own property from non-relative's house

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of hirz to determine whether the hadd punishment for theft applies in cases involving a relative's house.

Core Idea
Hirz is relative: a mahram’s house is not secure for him, so no cutting; a stranger’s house is secure for him, so cutting applies.

This lesson clarifies a nuanced ruling in Hanafi fiqh: theft from a relative’s house is not always subject to the hadd punishment. The key is not the relationship to the owner, but the thief’s access to the place. Let’s examine two scenarios from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Scenario A: thief steals a stranger's goods from a mahram's house. Scenario B: thief steals a mahram's goods from a stranger's house.
2
Determine the ruling for each
Scenario A: no cutting. Scenario B: cutting. The text states clearly.
3
Extract the underlying principle
The ruling hinges on whether the place is a secure hirz (protected storage) for the thief. If the thief can enter freely, it is not a hirz; if barred, it is.
4
Apply the principle to each scenario
In a mahram's house, the thief has unimpeded access, so no hirz → no cutting. In a stranger's house, he has no right of entry, so hirz exists → cutting.
5
Note the practical consequence
The hadd punishment depends on the thief's relationship to the place, not the ownership of the stolen goods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْسَرَقَ مِنْ بَيْتِ ذِى رَحْمٍ مَحْرَمٍ مَتَاعَ غَيْرِهِ يَنْبَغِي أَنْ لَا يُقْطَعَ وَلَوْسَرَقَ مَالَهُ مِنْ بَيْتِ غَيْرِهِ يُقْطَعُ اعْتِبَارًا لِلْحِرْزِ وَعَدَمِهِ ترجمہ اگرکسی نے اپنے ذی رحم کے کمر سے کسی اجنبی کا سامان چرالیا تو بھی اس کا ہاتھ نہیں کانا چاہئے (پوری حفاظت نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے ) اور اگر اپنے ذی رحم محرم کا مال کسی غیر کے کمرہ سے چرایا تو اس کا ہاتھ کاٹنا چاہئے۔ حفاظت کے ہونے اور نہ ہونے کے اعتبار سے۔ ( یعنی ذی رحم محرم کے کمرہ میں اس شخص سے حفاظت اور بچاؤ نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے کیونکہ اس کے لئے وہاں جانے سے کسی قسم کی رکاوٹ نہیں ہے لیکن غیر کے کمرہ میں جانے سے رکاوٹ باقی ہے۔) تشريح ... وَلَوْسَرَقَ مِنْ بَيْتِ ذِى رَحْمٍ مَحْرَمٍ مَتَاعَ غَيْرِهِ يَنبَغِي ... الخ مطلب ترجمہ سے واضح ہے۔
And if he steals from the house of a mahram relative the goods of another person, it is fitting that he should not be cut. And if he steals his own property from the house of another, he is cut, considering the (presence of) hirz and its absence. Translation: If someone steals from his mahram relative's room the goods of a stranger, his hand should not be cut (due to lack of complete protection). And if he steals his mahram relative's property from the room of a stranger, his hand should be cut, based on the presence or absence of hirz. (i.e., in the mahram's room there is no barrier for him, but in the stranger's room there is a barrier.) Tashrih... meaning is clear from the translation.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage provides two scenarios involving theft from a mahram relative's house. First: if the thief steals property belonging to a stranger from the house of his mahram relative, there is no cutting. The reason is that the mahram's house is not a secure hirz with respect to him because he can enter freely. Second: if he steals his mahram relative's property from the house of a stranger (e.g., a shop), then cutting applies because the stranger's house is a secure hirz for him—he has no right of entry. The ruling is based purely on whether the place is considered a protected hirz for the thief. This demonstrates the Hanafi principle that the absence of guard (due to familial access) negates the full hadd punishment.

Key Terms
حرز (hirz): A place that is secure against the thief's access

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man steals his mahram aunt's jewelry from his neighbor's locked house. According to this passage, is his hand cut?
A) Yes, because the house is a secure hirz for him
B) No, because he is a mahram
C) No, because the property belongs to his aunt
D) Yes, because he stole a valuable item

Key Takeaway
The hadd for theft applies only when the place is a secure hirz relative to the thief; a mahram's house is not secure for the mahram.

Theft from a Foster Mother: Cutting Applies Despite Mahramiyyat
Ruling on theft from foster mother with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft from a foster mother, explain the basis for the dispute with Abu Yusuf, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Foster mother is not like a real mother: hirz remains intact, so cutting applies despite mahramiyyat.

Does a foster son’s familiar access to his foster mother’s home remove the protection (hirz) from her property? Abu Hanifa’s school says no—cutting is wajib. Abu Yusuf’s narration disagrees. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the relied-upon position and the significance of public versus private knowledge in defining hirz.

Guided Path
1
State the zahir al-riwaya position
Identify the ruling from the well-known narrations: cutting is wajib for theft from a foster mother.
2
State Abu Yusuf's narration
Abu Yusuf is reported to say no cutting because foster sons customarily enter without restriction.
3
Identify the key difference
The dispute hinges on whether the foster mother’s home is a valid hirz given customary free access. The majority holds that foster relationships are not publicly known, so caution prevents true freedom.
Unlike blood relatives, foster ties are private, preserving the hirz.
4
Apply the tarjih mechanism
The zahir al-riwaya is given weight; most scholars follow it. Abu Yusuf's view is a weaker narration.
Cite authority: 'most scholars follow the zahir al-riwaya' as per the text.
5
Note the mufta bihi
The operative ruling for fatwa is cutting (wajib).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ سَرَقَ مِنْ أُمِّهِ مِنَ الرَّضَاعَةِ قُطِعَ وَعَنْ أَبِى يُوسُفَ أَنَّهُ لَا يُقْطَعُ لِأَنَّهُ يَدْخُلُ عَلَيْهَا مِنْ غَيْرِ اسْتِيْدَانِ وَحَشْمَةٍ بِخِلَافِ الأُخْتِ مِنَ الرَّضَاعَةِ لِاِنْعِدَامِ هَذَا الْمَعْنَى فِيهَا عَادَةً وَجْهُ الظَّاهِرِ أَنَّهُ لَا قَرَابَةَ وَالْمَحْرَمِيَّةُ بِدُونِهَا لَا تُحْتَرَمُ كَمَا إِذَا ثَبَتَتْ بِالزِّنَاءِ وَالتَّقْبِيْلِ عَنْ شَهْوَةٍ وَأَقْرَبُ مِنْ ذَالِكَ الْأخْتُ مِنَ الرَّضَاعَةِ وَهَذَا لَانَّ الرَّضَاعَةَ مَا يَشْتَهِرُ فَلَابُسُوْطَة تَحَرُّزًا عَنْ مَوْقِفِ التُهمَةِ بِخِلَافِ النَّسَبِ ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے اپنی رضاعی ماں کی کوئی چیز چرائی تو ہاتھ کا تاجائے گا۔ ( یہی ظاہر الروایت ہے ) لیکن امام ابو یوسف سے ایک روایت ہے کہ اس صورت میں چور کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ آدمی اپنی رضاعی ماں کے پاس اور اس کے گھر میں کسی اجازت اور اطلاع اور بغیر کسی روک ٹوک کے آتا جاتا رہتا ہے بخلاف رضاعی بہن کے کیونکہ عموما اس کے پاس اتنی آزادی کے ساتھ کوئی نہیں جاتا ہے۔ (لیکن اکثر علماء کا قول وہی ہے جو ظاہر الروایت میں ذکر کیا گیا ہے ) اور ظاہر الروایہ کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ ان کے درمیان آپس میں اگر چہ حرمت نکاح ثابت ہے مگر حقیقت میں کوئی رشتہ داری نہیں ہے، اور جہاں کہیں بھی محرمیت کے سوا کوئی قرابت نہ ہو وہاں اس کا احترام نہیں ہوتا ہے جیسے کہ کسی نے کسی عورت سے زنا کیا یا شہوت کے ساتھ اس کا بوسہ لیا تو اگر چہ اس عمل کی وجہ سے عورت کی ماں اس مرد پر حرام ہو جاتی ہے لیکن مرد کے دل میں اس کا احترام نہیں ہوتا ہے اور اس سے زیادہ قریب اس کی رضاعی بہن ہے۔ (کیونکہ حرمت رضاعت کے احترام کے باوجود اس عورت کے مال کے چوری کرنے سے بالا جماع اس چور کاہاتھ کا شناواجب ہوتا ہے ) اس میں بھید یہ ہے کہ رضاعت کے رشتہ عام لوگوں میں کم ہی مشہور ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے تہمت سے بچنے کے لئے آپس میں زیادہ اختلاط اور آمد ورفت نہیں ہوتی ہے۔ بخلاف نسب کے (ف یعنی رشتہ نسب تو تمام لوگوں میں مشہور ہوتا ہے اس لئے آپس میں زیادہ میل جول ہونے سے بھی کوئی غلط بات اور تہمت نہیں ہوتی ہے۔ تشريح ... وَإِن سَرَقَ مِنْ أَمِهِ مِنَ الرَّضَاعَةِ قُطِعَ وَعَنْ أبِى يُوسُف ... الخ مطلب ترجمہ سے واضح ہے۔
And if he steals from his foster mother, he is cut. And from Abu Yusuf (a narration) that he is not cut because he enters upon her without seeking permission or formality, unlike a foster sister because this meaning is normally absent in her. The apparent (Hanafi) argument is that there is no real kinship, and mahramiyyat without it is not respected—just as if it is established by zina or kissing with lust. Closer than that is the foster sister. And this is because foster relationship is not widely known, so there is no freedom (of access) out of caution against suspicion, unlike lineage. Translation: And if someone steals from his foster mother, his hand will be cut. (This is the zahir al-riwaya). But from Imam Abu Yusuf there is a narration that the thief's hand will not be cut because a person comes and goes to his foster mother without permission or hindrance, unlike the foster sister, because generally no one goes to her with such freedom. (But most scholars follow the zahir al-riwaya). The reason for the zahir al-riwaya is that even though there is prohibition of marriage between them, there is no real blood relationship, and wherever there is mahramiyyat without kinship, there is no respect—as when a man commits zina with a woman or kisses her lustfully, then the woman's mother becomes haram for him, but he does not have respect for her in his heart. Closer than that is his foster sister. The secret is that foster relationships are rarely known among people, so they do not freely mix to avoid suspicion, unlike lineage (which is well-known, so mixing does not cause suspicion).
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage addresses theft from a foster mother. The relied-upon Hanafi position (zahir al-riwaya) is that the thief's hand is cut, despite the foster mother being a mahram. The reasoning: foster kinship is not real lineage; the prohibition of marriage does not create the same degree of familiarity and trust as blood relation. People do not freely enter foster mothers' homes without caution because the relationship is not publicly known, thus the hirz is intact. Abu Yusuf is reported to have a different view: no cutting, because in practice people do enter freely. However, the majority of scholars follow the zahir al-riwaya, emphasizing that the lack of publicity prevents true openness. The analogy with zina-related mahramiyyat supports this—such relationships do not breed respect. Thus, cutting is wajib.

Key Terms
رضاعة (radā'ah): The relationship established through breastfeeding
ظاهر الرواية (zāhir al-riwāyah): The well-known transmitted narrations within the Hanafi school (the six books of Imam Muhammad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon Hanafi ruling on cutting for theft from a foster mother?
A) The hand is cut because foster relationship lacks real kinship and public knowledge
B) The hand is not cut due to mahramiyyat
C) The hand is not cut if the foster mother allows entry
D) The hand is cut only if the property exceeds the nisab

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school relies on the public knowledge of relationships to determine hirz; foster ties unknown to the public preserve the integrity of the hirz.

No Cutting for Theft Between Spouses and Master-Slave: The Shafi'i Dispute
Ruling on no cutting with Shafi'i disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on cutting for theft between spouses and from a separate room, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Customary permission and mutual freedom in wealth create a shubha that blocks the hadd penalty of cutting.

This passage addresses a critical boundary of the hadd penalty for theft: when the relationship between thief and owner implies a customary sharing of property. The Hanafi school holds that the mutual openness in wealth between spouses, as well as between slave and master, removes the element of stealth required for cutting. Even when property is taken from a separate, unshared room, the same principle applies. This lesson examines the reasoning and contrasts with Imam al-Shafi'i's view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: no cutting for theft between spouses (both directions), or theft by a slave from master or master's family. List the categories explicitly.
The passage mentions husband/wife, slave from master, master's wife, mistress, husband of mistress.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i dissents specifically when the theft occurs from a separate room (a distinct hirz) not shared by the couple.
Shafi'i views the separate room as a protected space where customary permission does not apply.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Shafi'i reasoning: the separate room is a specific hirz; the absence of shared residence removes the shubha of implied consent, so the full hadd should apply.
Hirz is a secure location; taking from it without permission constitutes theft in the usual sense.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school counters with the principle of bisotah (mutual freedom in wealth) between spouses, which overrides the specificity of the hirz. This is supported by custom (urf) and by analogy with the permissibility of testimony between spouses.
The analogy with testimony reinforces the idea that marriage creates a legal closeness that modifies property relations.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Note the practical consequence: Hanafis do not cut the hand; Shafi'is do, even when the stolen item is from a separate room. The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.
This difference is crucial for actual sentencing in hudud cases.

وَإِذَا سَرَقَ أَحَدُ الزَّوْجَيْنِ مِنَ الْآخَرِ اَوِ الْعَبْدُ مِنْ سَيّدِهِ أَوْ مِنْ اِمْرَأَةِ سَيِّدِهِ أَوْ مِنْ زَوْجِ سَيِّدَتِهِ لَمْ يُقْطَعُ لِوُجُودِ الْإِذْنِ بِالدُّخُولِ عَادَةً وَإِنْ سَرَقَ أَحَدُ الزَّوْجَيْنِ مِنْ حِرْزِ الاَ خَرِ خَاصَّةً لَا يَسْكُنَانِ فِيْهِ فَكَذَالِكَ الْجَوَابُ عِنْدَنَا خِلَافًا لِلشَّافِعِيِّ لِبُسُوْطَةٍ بَيْنَهُمَا فِي الْأَمْوَالِ عَادَةً وَدِلَالَةٌ وَهُوَ نَظِيرُ الْخِلَافَ فِي الشَّهَادَةِ ترجمہ اگر میاں بیوی میں سے ایک نے دوسرے کا مال چوری کر لیا۔ یا غلام نے اپنے آقا کا یا اپنے آقا کی بیوی یا اپنی مالکہ کا یا اس کے شوہر کا تو ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا کیونکہ عادتاً ان کو گھر میں آنے کی اجازت ہوتی ہے اور اگر میاں بیوی میں سے ایک نے دوسرے کے اس خاص کمرہ سے جس میں دونوں نہیں رہتے چوری کی تو بھی ہمارے نزدیک وہی جواب ہے خلافاً للشافعی کیونکہ ان کے درمیان مال میں عادتاً بسوطة ہوتی ہے اور دلیل سے بھی ثابت ہے اور یہ شہادت کے مسئلہ کی طرح ہے۔
And when one of the spouses steals from the other, or the slave from his master, or from his master's wife, or from the husband of his mistress, he is not cut due to the existence of customary permission to enter. And if one of the spouses steals from a specific hirz of the other in which they do not live together, the answer is the same with us, contrary to al-Shafi'i, due to the mutual freedom in wealth customarily and by indication, and it is analogous to the difference in testimony. Translation: If one of the spouses steals from the other, or a slave from his master, or from his master's wife, or from his mistress, or from her husband, the hand will not be cut because customarily they have permission to enter the house. And if one of the spouses steals from a specific room of the other which they do not share, the answer is still the same with us, contrary to al-Shafi'i, because there is always mutual freedom in wealth between them customarily and by evidence, and this is similar to the difference in the matter of testimony.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage covers several categories where cutting is not applied due to customary access and mutual sharing. The spouse (husband or wife) has customary permission to enter the other's property; similarly, a slave has access to the master's house. Even if the spouse takes from a separate room not commonly shared, the Hanafi view still denies cutting, based on the general freedom in wealth between spouses (custom and evidence). The passage notes a dispute with al-Shafi'i, who would apply cutting in such a case. The analogy is drawn to the difference regarding testimony between spouses: Hanafis allow it while others may not. The core principle is that the existence of mutual access removes the element of stealth required for the hadd penalty.

Key Terms
بسوطة (bisotah): Mutual freedom and openness in dealing with each other's wealth
إذن بالدخول (idhn bil dukhool): Customary permission to enter the other's domain
حرز (hirz): A secure or protected location where property is kept

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Yes, because it is a separate hirz
B) No, because of mutual freedom in wealth between spouses
C) Yes, because he had no permission
D) No, because the wife owns it

Key Takeaway
Theft between spouses or slave and master does not trigger hadd cutting due to customary permission and mutual freedom in wealth, even from a separate room according to Hanafis.

Theft from a Foster Mother: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Hadd
Intra-Hanafi dispute on theft from foster mother

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school (majority) cuts the hand for theft from a foster mother and evaluate Abu Yusuf's counter-argument.

Core Idea
Stealing from a foster mother incurs hadd because fosterage does not grant customary free access, unlike blood lineage.

The hadd penalty for theft (qat‘ al-yad) requires that the stolen property be protected (muhraz). This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute: does a foster mother's home count as a protected space? While Abu Yusuf argues that customary permission to enter removes protection, the majority (zahir al-riwayah) holds that the hand is cut because fosterage does not establish real lineage or unrestricted access. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the conditions of hirz and differentiate between types of mahram relationships.

Guided Path
1
State the general principle for theft from a mahram
Recall that theft from a dhi rahm mahram's house does not incur cutting because the thief has customary access, so the property is not fully protected. However, if the mahram's property is stolen from a non-mahram's house, the hand is cut.
This establishes the baseline: protection (hirz) depends on customary barriers, not just legal mahram status.
2
Identify the specific case of the foster mother
The text focuses on whether the foster mother is treated like a birth mother (no cutting) or like a non-mahram (cutting). The majority (zahir al-riwayah) holds that the hand is cut.
The dispute is about whether the foster mother's house is considered a protected space for the thief.
3
Present Abu Yusuf's objection and his dalil
Abu Yusuf argues that the foster mother is like a birth mother because the thief enters freely without permission. Therefore, her house lacks full protection, and the hadd is dropped.
His dalil is customary practice ('urf): people freely visit foster mothers.
4
Analyze the majority response and its 'illa
The majority responds that fosterage does not create real lineage; it only establishes marriage prohibition. Without lineage, customary access is not guaranteed. They draw an analogy: a woman made mahram through fornication or lustful kissing does not grant unrestricted access. Also, fosterage is not publicly known, so suspicion prevents frequent visits.
The 'illa (effective cause) for dropping hadd in the birth mother case is both lineage and public knowledge; fosterage lacks both.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
The practical difference: under the majority, the hand is cut; under Abu Yusuf, it is not. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the majority view (zahir al-riwayah), as most scholars and the text's author prefer it.
The tarjih authority is the author of Ashraf al-Hidayah, who follows the zahir al-riwayah.

کباب ما يقطع فيه و مالا يقطع .
۲۳۲
اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ششم ترجمہ اگرکسی نے اپنے ذی رحم کے کمر سے کسی اجنبی کا سامان چرالیا تو بھی اس کا ہاتھ نہیں کانا چاہئے (پوری حفاظت نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے ) اور اگر اپنے ذی رحم محرم کا مال کسی غیر کے کمرہ سے چرایا تو اس کا ہاتھ کاٹنا چاہئے۔ حفاظت کے ہونے اور نہ ہونے کے اعتبار سے۔ ( یعنی ذی رحم محرم کے کمرہ میں اس شخص سے حفاظت اور بچاؤ نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے کیونکہ اس کے لئے وہاں جانے سے کسی قسم کی رکاوٹ نہیں ہے لیکن غیر کے کمرہ میں جانے سے رکاوٹ باقی ہے۔)
تشريح ... وَلَوْسَرَقَ مِنْ بَيْتِ ذِى رَحْمٍ مَحْرَمٍ مَتَاعَ غَيْرِهِ يَنبَغِي ... الخ مطلب ترجمہ سے واضح ہے۔ رضاعی ماں کی چوری کرنے کا حکم
وَإِنْ سَرَقَ مِنْ أُمِّهِ مِنَ الرَّضَاعَةِ قُطِعَ وَعَنْ أَبِى يُوسُفَ أَنَّهُ لَا يُقْطَعُ لِأَنَّهُ يَدْخُلُ عَلَيْهَا مِنْ غَيْرِ اسْتِيْدَانِ وَحَشْمَةٍ بِخِلَافِ الأُخْتِ مِنَ الرَّضَاعَةِ لِاِنْعِدَامِ هَذَا الْمَعْنَى فِيهَا عَادَةً وَجْهُ الظَّاهِرِ أَنَّهُ لَا قَرَابَةَ وَالْمَحْرَمِيَّةُ بِدُونِهَا لَا تُحْتَرَمُ كَمَا إِذَا ثَبَتَتْ بِالزِّنَاءِ وَالتَّقْبِيْلِ عَنْ شَهْوَةٍ وَأَقْرَبُ مِنْ ذَالِكَ الْأخْتُ مِنَ الرَّضَاعَةِ وَهَذَا لَانَّ الرَّضَاعَةَ مَا يَشْتَهِرُ فَلَابُسُوْطَة تَحَرُّزًا عَنْ مَوْقِفِ التُهمَةِ بِخِلَافِ النَّسَبِ ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے اپنی رضاعی ماں کی کوئی چیز چرائی تو ہاتھ کا تاجائے گا۔ ( یہی ظاہر الروایت ہے ) لیکن امام ابو یوسف سے ایک روایت ہے کہ اس صورت میں چور کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ آدمی اپنی رضاعی ماں کے پاس اور اس کے گھر میں کسی اجازت اور اطلاع اور بغیر کسی روک ٹوک کے آتا جاتا رہتا ہے بخلاف رضاعی بہن کے کیونکہ عموما اس کے پاس اتنی آزادی کے ساتھ کوئی نہیں جاتا ہے۔ (لیکن اکثر علماء کا قول وہی ہے جو ظاہر الروایت میں ذکر کیا گیا ہے ) اور ظاہر الروایہ کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ ان کے درمیان آپس میں اگر چہ حرمت نکاح ثابت ہے مگر حقیقت میں کوئی رشتہ داری نہیں ہے، اور جہاں کہیں بھی محرمیت کے سوا کوئی قرابت نہ ہو وہاں اس کا احترام نہیں ہوتا ہے جیسے کہ کسی نے کسی عورت سے زنا کیا یا شہوت کے ساتھ اس کا بوسہ لیا تو اگر چہ اس عمل کی وجہ سے عورت کی ماں اس مرد پر حرام ہو جاتی ہے لیکن مرد کے دل میں اس کا احترام نہیں ہوتا ہے اور اس سے زیادہ قریب اس کی رضاعی بہن ہے۔ (کیونکہ حرمت رضاعت کے احترام کے باوجود اس عورت کے مال کے چوری کرنے سے بالا جماع اس چور کاہاتھ کا شناواجب ہوتا ہے ) اس میں بھید یہ ہے کہ رضاعت کے رشتہ عام لوگوں میں کم ہی مشہور ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے تہمت سے بچنے کے لئے آپس میں زیادہ اختلاط اور آمد ورفت نہیں ہوتی ہے۔ بخلاف نسب کے (ف یعنی رشتہ نسب تو تمام لوگوں میں مشہور ہوتا ہے اس لئے آپس میں زیادہ میل جول ہونے سے بھی کوئی غلط بات اور تہمت نہیں ہوتی ہے۔)
تشريح ... وَإِن سَرَقَ مِنْ أَمِهِ مِنَ الرَّضَاعَةِ قُطِعَ وَعَنْ أبِى يُوسُف ... الخ مطلب ترجمہ سے واضح ہے۔
Chapter: What necessitates cutting and what does not. Translation: If someone stole the goods of a stranger from the house of his dhi rahm mahram, his hand should not be cut (due to lack of full protection). And if he stole the property of his own dhi rahm mahram from the house of a stranger, his hand should be cut, based on the presence or absence of protection. (Because in the mahram's house there is no barrier for him, but in a stranger's house there is a barrier.) Commentary: ... If he stole from the house of a dhi rahm mahram the goods of another... meaning clear from translation. Ruling on stealing from a foster mother. And if he stole from his foster mother, his hand is cut. And from Abu Yusuf: he is not cut because he enters upon her without permission and hesitation, unlike the foster sister because this meaning is absent in her customarily. The proof of the apparent view: there is no lineage, and mahramiyyah without it is not respected, just as when it is established by fornication or kissing with desire. And closer than that is the foster sister. And this is because fosterage is not widely known, so there is no freedom to avoid suspicion, unlike lineage. Translation: And if someone stole something from his foster mother, his hand will be cut. (This is the zahir al-riwayah.) But from Imam Abu Yusuf there is a narration that in this case the thief's hand is not cut, because a person goes to his foster mother's house without permission and notice, unlike the foster sister, because generally no one goes to her with such freedom. (But most scholars follow the zahir al-riwayah.) And the reason for the zahir al-riwayah is that although marriage prohibition is established between them, there is no actual lineage, and wherever there is only mahramiyyah without lineage, that respect is not present, like when someone commits fornication with a woman or kisses her with desire, then although the woman's mother becomes prohibited to that man, there is no respect in his heart. And closer than that is his foster sister. The secret is that the fosterage relationship is rarely known among people, so to avoid suspicion, there is not much intermixing and coming-and-going, unlike lineage.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the theft from a foster mother. The passage first discusses the general principle: theft from a mahram's house does not incur cutting because the thief has customary access, so the property is not fully protected. However, if the thief steals the mahram's own property from a non-mahram's house, the hand is cut because protection is present. Then the specific case of the foster mother is addressed. The relied-upon position (zahir al-riwayah) is that stealing from one's foster mother requires cutting the hand. This is because fosterage does not establish real lineage; it only creates marriage prohibition. Without lineage, the same respect and unrestricted access do not apply. Abu Yusuf holds that the hand is not cut because a person has customary permission to enter the foster mother's house freely, just like with a birth mother. The text refutes this by analogy: if mahramiyyah alone sufficed for unrestricted access, then a woman one has fornicated with or kissed with desire would also give free access, which is not the case. Foster sister is an even closer analogy: she is also a mahram through fosterage, yet customary access is not free because fosterage is not widely known, so suspicion is avoided. Thus, the ruling differs from that of a real sister (who is a blood relative). The Hanafi school emphasizes the lack of public knowledge of fosterage as the 'illa for cutting.

Key Terms
ذو رحم محرم (dhi rahm mahram): A relative within the prohibited degrees of marriage (mahram) due to blood relation.
رضاعة (radā‘ah): Fosterage through breastfeeding that establishes marriage prohibition.
قطع (qat‘): Amputation of the hand as the fixed penalty (hadd) for theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the zahir al-riwayah, what is the ruling on stealing from one's foster mother?
A) The hand is not cut because of customary permission to enter.
B) The hand is cut because fosterage does not create real lineage or unrestricted access.
C) The hand is cut only if the stolen goods are from a stranger.
D) The matter is discretionary (ta‘zir).

Key Takeaway
Theft from a foster mother incurs the hadd penalty because fosterage does not confer customary access, unlike blood lineage.

Hadd Theft Between Spouses and Slave-Master: The Customary Permission Exception
Ruling on cutting hand for theft between spouses and between slave and master

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft between spouses and between master and slave, identify the condition of complete hirz, and explain why the Hanafi school exempts even theft from a private exclusive room, contrasting with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Customary permission to enter negates complete hirz, so no hadd cutting between spouses or master-slave.

The hadd punishment for theft (cutting the hand) requires that the stolen property be in a complete hirz (secure place). This lesson examines a special exception: when the thief has customary permission to enter the premises, as between spouses or a slave and his master. Even if the property is in a private room exclusively belonging to one spouse, the Hanafi school still does not apply the cutting penalty, while Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees. We will explore the reasoning, the evidence, and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the general Hanafi rule
Identify the basic condition for hadd theft: complete hirz. Then note the exception for those with customary entry permission.
The key term is 'idhn 'ādatan (customary permission).
2
Apply the exception to spouses and slaves
Understand why spouses and slaves are considered to have implicit permission to enter each other's spaces, thus preventing full hirz.
This is based on customary practice, not explicit permission.
3
Analyze the dispute over a private exclusive room
Contrast the Hanafi stance (no cutting even from a private room) with the Shafi'i view (cutting applies because the room is a complete hirz).
The Hanafi response: even a private room does not sever the customary wealth-sharing relationship.
4
Examine the analogy with testimony
The text cites qiyas: spouses are not credible witnesses for each other in certain cases; similarly, their mutual access prevents complete hirz.
This strengthens the Hanafi argument by showing a consistent principle.
5
Review companion practice as evidence
Look at the incident with Caliph 'Umar and the mirror, and the narration from Ibn Mas'ud, which support the Hanafi position and indicate companion consensus.
This adds weight to the Hanafi view.

میاں بیوی کا ایک دوسرے کی چیز ، غلام کا آقا کی چیز ، آقا کی بیوی کی چیز یا ملکہ کے شوہر کی چیز چوری کرنے پر قطع ید کا حکم
وَإِذَا سَرَقَ أَحَدُ الزَّوْجَيْنِ مِنَ الْآخَرِ اَوِ الْعَبْدُ مِنْ سَيّدِهِ أَوْ مِنْ اِمْرَأَةِ سَيِّدِهِ أَوْ مِنْ زَوْجِ سَيِّدَتِهِ لَمْ يُقْطَعُ لِوُجُودِ الْإِذْنِ بِالدُّخُولِ عَادَةً وَإِنْ سَرَقَ أَحَدُ الزَّوْجَيْنِ مِنْ حِرْزِ الاَ خَرِ خَاصَّةً لَا يَسْكُنَانِ فِيْهِ فَكَذَالِكَ الْجَوَابُ عِنْدَنَا خِلَافًا لِلشَّافِعِيِّ لِبُسُوْطَةٍ بَيْنَهُمَا فِي الْأَمْوَالِ عَادَةً وَدِلَالَةٌ وَهُوَ نَظِيرُ الْخِلَافَ فِي الشَّهَادَةِ ترجمہ اگر میاں بیوی میں سے ایک نے دوسرے کا مال چوری کر لیا۔ یا غلام نے اپنے آقا کا یا اپنے آقا کی بیوی یا اپنی مالکہ کا یا اس کے شوہر کا مال چرالیا تو قطع نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ عموما غلام کو مالکوں اور اپنے لوگوں کے پاس آنے اور جانے کی اجازت ہوا کرتی ہے اور اگر میاں بیوی کا کوئی محفوظ خاص کمرہ ہو جس میں کوئی بھی رہتا نہ ہو۔ یعنی ان میں سے صرف ایک کے لئے مخصوص ہو اور وہاں سے دوسرے نے مال چرا لیا تو بھی ہمارے نزدیک یہی حکم ہے کیونکہ عموما اور عادتا اور دلالتا کچھ تعلقات ہوا کرتے ہیں۔ لیکن امام شافعی کا اس میں اختلاف ہے اور یہ اس اختلاف کی نظیر ہے جو گواہی میں ہے۔ ......
تشریح وَإِذَا سَرَقَ أَحَدُ الزَّوْجَيْنِ مِنَ الْاحَرَ اَوِ الْعَبْدُ مِنْ سَيّدِهِ أَوْ مِنْ اِمْرَأَةِ الحَ وَإِذَا سَرَقَ أَحَدُ الزَّوْجَيْنِ مِنَ الْآخَرِ اَوِ الْعَبْدُ مِنْ سَيَدِهِ أَوْ مِنْ امْرَأَةِ سَيّدِهِ أَوْ مِنْ زَوج سيدي الخ چوری کے بعد ہاتھ کاٹنے کا حکم اسی صورت میں ہے کہ مال مکمل طور پر محفوظ ہو اسی بناء پر آقا کے مال سے چوری کرنے پر غلام کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا کیونکہ غلام کے لئے عادتا گھر میں آمد ورفت کی آزادی رہتی ہے۔ اور اس سے مال محفوظ نہیں رکھا جاتا ہے۔ ف۔ چنانچہ عبداللہ بن عمیر والحضری نے ایک مرتبہ حضرت عمر رضی اللہ عنہ کے پاس آ کر یہ دعوی کیا کہ میرے غلام نے میرا مال چوری کیا ہے اس لئے آپ اس کا ہاتھ کاٹ دیں فرمایا اس نے کیا چوری کیا ہے۔ انہوں نے کہا کہ اس نے میری بیوی کا ایک آئینہ چرایا ہے جس کی قیمت ساٹھ درہم تھی۔ تب حضرت عمر رضی اللہ عنہ نے فرمایا کہ تم اسے چھوڑ دو۔ اس کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا اس لئے کے تمہارے غلام نے تمہارا ہی مال چرایا ہے۔ اس کی روایت مالک شافعی نے کی ہے۔
اسی طرح حضرت عبداللہ بن مسعود رضی اللہ عنہ سے مروی ہے اور کسی صحابی سے اس کے مخالف نہیں پایا جاتا ہے لہذا یہ حکم اجماعی طور پر ہوا۔
The ruling on cutting the hand for theft between spouses, a slave from his master, the master's wife, or the mistress's husband. And if one spouse stole from the other, or the slave from his master, or from his master's wife, or from his mistress's husband, it is not cut due to customary permission to enter. And if one spouse stole from a private secure room of the other in which neither lives, the answer is likewise with us, contrary to al-Shafi'i, because of customary and implicit sufficiency between them in wealth, and it is analogous to the disagreement in testimony. Translation: If one of the spouses stole the other's property, or the slave stole his master's or his master's wife's or his mistress's or her husband's property, then cutting is not done because customarily the slave has permission to come and go to the owners. And if the spouses have a private secure room in which no one lives, i.e., it is reserved for only one of them, and the other stole from there, the same rule applies in our view, because customarily and implicitly there are some connections. But al-Shafi'i differs in this, and it is analogous to the disagreement in testimony. Commentary: The ruling on cutting after theft applies only if the property is completely secure. For this reason, a slave's hand is not cut for stealing from his master, because customarily the slave has freedom to come and go in the house, and thus the property is not fully protected. Accordingly, 'Abdullah bin 'Umar and al-Hadrami once came to 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) and claimed that his slave stole his property, so he should cut his hand. 'Umar asked what he stole. He said, 'He stole a mirror of my wife worth sixty dirhams.' 'Umar said, 'Release him; his hand shall not be cut because your slave stole your own property.' This was narrated by Malik and al-Shafi'i. Similarly, it is narrated from 'Abdullah bin Mas'ud, and no Companion is found opposing it, so this is an agreed-upon ruling.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning theft between spouses and between master and slave. The Hanafi position is clear: cutting the hand is not required in these cases because the thief has customary permission to enter the place where the property is kept, thus the property is not in a complete hirz (secure place). For example, a spouse normally has access to the other's belongings, and a slave has access to the master's house. Even if the theft occurs from a private room belonging exclusively to one spouse, the Hanafi view still does not cut, because there is still a customary and implicit sharing of wealth between spouses, which prevents the property from being fully secured against each other. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees in such a case, arguing that if the room is exclusively for one spouse, the other does not have customary access, so the property is secure. The Hanafis counter by analogy (qiyas) with the ruling on testimony: spouses are not considered credible witnesses for each other in certain matters, and similarly, their mutual access prevents the property from being considered theft-proof. The passage also cites a practice from Caliph 'Umar (and from 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud) to support the ruling, indicating a level of consensus among the Companions.

Key Terms
حرز (hirz): A secure place or condition that protects property from theft; customarily considered secure.
إذن عادة (idhn 'ādatan): Customary permission to enter; implies that the property is not fully protected against that person.
بسوطة (basūṭa): Sufficiency or connection; here meaning a customary and implicit sharing of wealth between spouses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the hand not cut when a husband steals from his wife's private locked safe inside their shared home?
A) Because the safe is not a hirz.
B) Because the husband has customary permission to be in the home, and that extends to the safe due to the marital relationship.
C) Because the value is below nisab.
D) Because the husband is the owner of the property.

Key Takeaway
Customary permission to enter prevents the existence of complete hirz, so hadd cutting is not applied for theft between spouses or master-slave, even in private exclusive rooms.

Theft from Mukatib and Ghanimah: The Principle of Shared Right
Ruling on cutting hand for master stealing from mukatib and soldier stealing from booty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for theft from a mukatib or ghanimah and explain the 'illa (shared right) that prevents the hadd penalty.

Core Idea
A thief is not cut if he has a share in the stolen property.

In Hanafi criminal law, the hadd penalty for theft requires strict conditions. One essential condition is that the stolen property must belong exclusively to another (milk khalis). This lesson examines two cases where this condition is absent: when a master steals from his mukatib (a slave under a manumission contract), and when a soldier steals from the joint booty (ghanimah). In both scenarios, the thief has a legal right or share in the property, thus the hadd penalty of hand-cutting is not applied. The ruling is supported by the practice of 'Ali (ra) and other Companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Both acts involve stealing: a master from his mukatib, a soldier from ghanimah. The ruling is the same: no hand-cutting (hadd) is applied.
2
Find the 'illa (effective cause)
The text explicitly states the reason: 'because he has a right in his earnings' (for the mukatib) and 'because he has a share in it' (for the ghanimah). The shared right or ownership is the preventing factor.
The 'illa is the existence of a portion of ownership in the stolen property.
3
Examine the proof (dalil)
The ruling is supported by the practice of 'Ali (ra), who refused to cut the hand of a soldier who stole a helmet from the ghanimah, reasoning that the soldier had a share. This is a qawl sahabi (statement of a Companion) and is taken as a proof in Hanafi jurisprudence.
Narrations from al-Daraqutni and Ibn Majah confirm this.
4
Apply the principle to analogous cases
The principle is general: whenever the thief has any legal right or share in the stolen property, the hadd is not applied. This applies to joint owners, partners, etc.
The principle is based on the condition that the property must be fully owned by another (milk khalis) for hadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آقا نے مکاتب کا مال چرایا یا مال غنیمت کو کسی نے چرایا تو ہاتھ نہ کاٹیں گے
وَلَوْسَرَقَ الْمَوْلَى مِنْ مُكَاتَبِهِ لَمْ يُقْطَعُ لِاَنَّ لَهُ فِي إِكْسَابِهِ حَقًّا وَكَذَلِكَ السَّارِقُ مِنَ الْمَغْنَمِ لِأَنَّ لَهُ فِيْهِ نَصِيبًا وَهُوَ مَا تُورٌ عَنْ عَلِيٌّ دَرْءٌ وَتَعْلِيْلًا
ترجمہ اور اگر مولی نے اپنے مکاتب کے مال میں سے چرالیا تو بھی قطع نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ مولی کو اپنے مکاتب کی کمائی میں حق ہوتا ہے۔ اسی طرح اگر کسی لشکری نے غنیمت کے مال سے چرالیا تو بھی قطع نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس غنیمت میں اس چرانے والے کا بھی کچھ حق ہوتا ہے اور حضرت علی کرم اللہ وجھہ سے یہی منقول بھی ہے اور آپ نے بھی یہی علت بیان کی اور اس پر حد جاری نہیں کی۔
تشریح وَكَذَلِكَ السَّارِقُ مِنَ الْمَعْنم اسی طرح اگر غنیمت کے مشترکہ مال میں سے کسی لشکر نے کچھ چھالیا تو بھی اس کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ غنیمت میں اسی چور کا بھی حصہ ہے اور حضرت علی کرم اللہ وجہ سے ایسا ہی منقول ہے اور یہی علت بیان کرتے ہوئے آپ نے اس کا ہاتھ نہیں کا نا۔ (یہ اثر عبدالرزاق نے اس طرح نقل کیا ہے کہ حضرت علی کرم اللہ وجہہ کے پاس ایک شخص لایا گیا جس نے مال غنیمت میں سے ایک مغز یعنی میدان جنگ میں سر چھپانے کے لئے لوہے کی ٹوپی (خود) چھائی تھی تو آپ نے فرمایا کہ اس مال میں اس کا بھی تو کچھ حق ہے اس کہنے کے بعد
اس کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا۔ دارقطنی نے اس کی روایت کی ہے۔ اسی کے مانند ابن ماجہ نے بھی حضرت ابن عباس رضی اللہ عنہما سے روایت کی ہے۔
If a master stole from his mukatib, his hand is not cut because he has a right in his earnings. Similarly, the thief from the ghanimah (spoils) is not cut because he has a share in it, and it is narrated from 'Ali that he repelled (the hadd) and gave the same reasoning. Translation: And if the master stole from the property of his mukatib, then cutting will not be done, because the master has a right in the earnings of his mukatib. Similarly, if a soldier stole from the ghanimah property, then cutting will not be done, because the thief also has some right in that ghanimah. And this is narrated from 'Ali (may Allah honor his face), and he gave the same reasoning and did not apply the hadd. Commentary: Similarly, the thief from the ghanimah: if a person from the army stole something from the joint property of the ghanimah, his hand will not be cut, because the thief also has a share in the ghanimah. And it is narrated from 'Ali (may Allah honor his face) that he acted likewise and gave this reason and did not cut his hand. (This athar was narrated by 'Abd al-Razzaq that a man was brought to 'Ali who had stolen a helmet (a metal cap for protecting the head) from the ghanimah. 'Ali said, 'He has some right in this property.' After saying this, he did not cut his hand. Al-Daraqutni narrated it. Similarly, Ibn Majah narrated from Ibn 'Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them both).
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning theft from a mukatib (a slave on a contract of manumission) and from ghanimah (spoils of war). The established principle is that the hadd of cutting is not applied if the thief has any right or share in the stolen property. For the mukatib, the master has a right in his earnings (because the mukatib is still under the master's ownership and his earnings ultimately belong to the master until full payment). Therefore, if the master steals from the mukatib, his hand is not cut. Similarly, for a soldier (ghazi) who steals from the joint booty (ghanimah), he has a share in it, so the hadd is not applied. The text cites a specific narration from 'Ali (ra) as proof: he did not cut the hand of a man who stole a helmet from the ghanimah, reasoning that the man had a right in it. This is corroborated by narrations from Ibn 'Abbas. The 'illa (effective cause) is the existence of a rightful share in the property, which creates a kind of ownership right that prevents the property from being considered entirely someone else's, thus negating the condition for the hadd penalty.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukātib): A slave who has entered a contract (kitabah) with his master to purchase his freedom by paying installments.
غنيمة (ghanīmah): Spoils of war taken from the enemy after victory, distributed among soldiers.
علة (ʿillah): The effective cause or reason that determines a legal ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The soldier has a share in the ghanimah, so the property is not entirely someone else's.
C) The ghanimah is not in a proper hirz.
D) The soldier's commander has not authorized the theft.

Key Takeaway
Had punishment for theft is not applied when the thief has any ownership right or share in the stolen property.

Non-Application of Hadd for Theft from Shared Property
Ruling on non-application of hudud due to shared property rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that hadd is not applied when the thief has a proprietary interest (shubha) in the stolen property, using the cases of the master and the soldier.

Core Idea
When ownership is shared, hadd is suspended — shubha in property rights prevents the fixed penalty.

The Hanafi school recognizes that the fixed penalty of hand-cutting (hadd al-sariqa) is not applied when the thief has some right in the stolen property. This lesson examines two cases: a master stealing from his mukatib (a slave under contract of manumission) and a soldier stealing from war booty (ghanima). Both involve a shubha (doubt) due to the thief's own share. The ruling is supported by a narration from Ali ibn Abi Talib.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State clearly that in both cases—master stealing from mukatib and soldier from ghanima—the hadd penalty is not applied. The thief’s hand is not cut.
2
Analyze the Rationale (Shubha)
Understand that the reason is the thief’s own right in the property: the master has a right in the mukatib’s earnings, and the soldier has a share in the booty. This shared ownership creates a doubt (shubha) that blocks the hadd.
The shubha is not full ownership but a partial right sufficient to prevent the extreme penalty.
3
Locate the Dalil
Recognize the proof: a narration from Ali radi Allahu anhu, who did not cut the hand of a soldier who stole from the ghanima. The principle is derived from this precedent.
The dalil is a qawl sahabi (statement of a Companion), which has zanni thubut but is accepted in Hanafi usul.
4
Explain the Hanafi Principle
The Hanafi school holds that hadd is not applied when there is a shubha in the property right. This is an extension of the maxim al-hudud tudra'u bi al-shubuhat (fixed penalties are repelled by doubts).
This applies only to hadd penalties, not to ta'zir (discretionary punishment).
5
Compare Cross-Madhhab View
Note that Imam al-Shafi'i may disagree in cases where the share is not immediately in the thief's possession (e.g., before distribution). For the Hanafi school, even a potential right suffices.
The practical difference: a soldier who steals before distribution in the Shafi'i school may face hadd, while in Hanafi he does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ سَرَقَ الْمَوْلَى مِنْ مُكَاتَبِهِ لَمْ يُقْطَعْ لِأَنَّ لَهُ فِي إِكْسَابِهِ حَقًّا. وَكَذَلِكَ السَّارِقُ مِنَ الْمَغْنَمِ لِأَنَّ لَهُ فِيهِ نَصِيبًا. رُوِيَ عَنْ عَلِيٍّ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُ.
And if the master steals from his mukatib, he is not cut because he has a right in his earnings. Similarly, one who steals from the war booty [is not cut] because he has a share in it. It is narrated from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him).
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage extends the principle that hadd is not applied when the thief has a proprietary interest in the stolen property. The master has a right in the earnings of his mukatib (a slave contracted for manumission), so stealing from him is like taking his own property. Similarly, a soldier who steals from the ghanima (war booty) has a share, so the theft is not a complete violation of another's exclusive right. This is supported by the narration of Ali ibn Abi Talib, who did not cut the hand of a soldier who stole a helmet from the booty, citing the shared ownership. These rulings are based on the concept of shubha (semblance of right) in the property.

Key Terms
مُكَاتَب (mukatab): A slave under a contract of manumission for a fixed payment.
غَنِيمَة (ghanima): War booty captured from the enemy, shared among soldiers.
شُبْهَة (shubha): A doubt or semblance of right that prevents the application of a fixed penalty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because booty is not property
B) Because he has a share in the booty
C) Because the theft was not from a secure place
D) Because the value is usually low

Key Takeaway
Hadd is not applied when the thief has any proprietary right in the stolen property, due to the principle of shubha.

Definition of Hirz and Its Two Types
Defines hirz and its two types with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define hirz, state its two types, and apply the condition of hirz to determine whether the hadd of theft is applicable.

Core Idea
Hirz is of two types: place-based and guardian-based; theft must occur from a hirz for the hadd penalty.

In the chapter on hudud for theft, the Hanafi school establishes that cutting the hand requires the theft to be from a secure place (hirz). This lesson defines hirz and explains its two types—by the place itself and by a guardian—using the hadith of Safwan as proof. Understanding hirz is crucial because it determines whether the thief's hand can be amputated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal concept: Hirz
Hirz is a condition for applying the hadd of theft. The passage defines it broadly as any protective means that prevents clandestine removal.
It is not limited to locked buildings.
2
Break down the two types
The text states two types: hirz bi ma'nahu (by its own nature, like houses and chests) and hirz bil hafiz (by a guardian, like a person guarding property even in an open area).
3
Examine the hadith proof
The Prophet cut the hand of a thief who stole Safwan's cloak from under his head while he slept in the mosque. This shows that a sleeping guardian establishes hirz.
The hadith is authentic and narrated by Abu Dawud, Nasa'i, etc.
4
Distinguish the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school accepts guardian-based hirz, while some other schools may require a locked place. This difference affects practical rulings.
The reliance is on the explicit action of the Prophet.
5
Apply to scenarios
Consider theft from a mosque where the owner is asleep: hadd applies. Theft from an unattended public bench: no hadd, only ta'zir.

باب ما يقطع فيه ومالا يقطع .
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ششم
مُخرَزْبِهِ وَقَدْ قَطَعَ رَسُولُ اللهِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ مَنْ سَرَقَ رِدَاءَ صَفْوَانَ مِنْ تَحْتِ رَأْسِهِ وَهُوَ نَائِمٌ فِي الْمَسْجِدِ ترجمہ اور قدوری نے کہا ہے ۔ حرز و حفاظت اور مکان محفوظ دو طرح سے ہوتا ہے ایک وہ حفاظت ہے جو اپنے معنی کی وجہ سے محفوظ ہے جیسے کمرے اور گھر (اور صندوق، جانور، گائے اور گھوڑے باندھنے کی جگہیں) اور دوسری قسم کی حفاظت وہ ہوتی ہے جس کی حفاظت کے لئے خاص طور سے کوئی منرر ہو تو وہ چیز حفظ وامان میں ہوگی اگر چہ وہ میدان میں رکھی ہوئی ہو۔ اوپر میں مصنف نے یہ جو فرمایا ہے کہ حرز و حفاظت ضروری چیز ہے اس لئے کہ اس کے بغیر خفیہ طور سے نکالنا نہیں کیا جاسکتا ہے اور حفاظت کبھی مکان کے ساتھ بھی ہو سکتی ہے اور محافظ وہی مکان سمجھا جائے گا جو سامان کی حفاظت کے لئے ہی بنایا گیا ہو۔ جیسے گھر ، کمرے، صندوق اور دکان وغیرہ اور حفاظت کا تعلق کبھی محافظ کے ساتھ بھی ہوتا ہے مثلاً کوئی شخص راستہ پا مسجد میں بیٹھ گیا اور اس کے پاس اس کا مال رکھا ہوا ہو تو اس جگہ کا مال اسی آدمی کی وجہ سے حفاظت میں ہے اور رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے اس شخص کا ہاتھ کاٹنے کا حکم دیا جس نے حضرت صفوان کی چادر ان کے سر کے نیچے سے چرالی تھی۔ اس وقت جبکہ یہ صفوان مسجد میں سور ہے تھے۔ تشریح قَالَ وَالْحِرْزُ عَلَى نَوْعَيْنِ حِرْزَ لِمَعْنى فِيْهِ كَالْبُيُوتِ وَالدُّوَرِ وَحِرْزَ بِالْحَافِظِ ... الخ ترجمہ سے مطلب واضح ہے۔ وقد قطع رسول الله صلى عليه وسلم مال کی حفاظت بھی صرف مخصوص جگہ میں رکھ دینے سے ہوتی ہے۔ اور کبھی محافظ آدمی کے وہاں پر موجود رہنے کی وجہ سے ہوتی ہے۔ مثلا کوئی شخص راستہ یا مسجد میں بیٹھ گیا اور اپنے پاس مال رکھ لیا تو اس شخص کی وجہ سے حرز و حفاظت میں سمجھا جائے گا۔ جیسا کہ رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے اس شخص کا ہاتھ کاٹنے کا حکم فرمایا تھا جس نے صفون کے سرہانے سے چادر چرائی تھی جبکہ وہ مسجد میں سور ہے تھے۔ ف۔ صاحب تنقیح نے کہا ہے کہ یہ حدیث صحیح ہے اور ابوداؤ د ونسائی واحمد و حاکم وغیر ہم نے یہ حدیث روایت کی ہے پوری حدیث یہ ہے کہ صفوان بن امیہ سے مکہ میں کہا گیا کہ جو شخص ہجرت کر کے مدینہ نہیں گیاوہ برباد ہوا اس لئے انہوں نے مکہ چھوڑ کر مدینہ کی طرف ہجرت کی اور مسجد میں پہنچ کر اپنی چادر سر کے نیچے رکھ کر سو گئے۔ اتنے میں ایک چور نے اللہ میں یہ نہیں چاہتا تھا کہ اس کا ہاتھ کاٹا جائے اور اب میں نے یہ چادر اسے صدقہ کر دی۔ رسول اللہ صلى الله عليه وسلم نے فرمایا کہ اب کیا ہوتا ہے۔ اس کو میرے پاس لانے سے پہلے ہی تم نے ایسا کیوں نہیں کیا۔ مالک نے موصول اس کی روایت کی کہ اور ابن عبدالبر نے کہا کہ امام مالک کے تمام اصحاب ایسے مرسل روایت کرتے ہیں سوائے ابو عاصم النبیل اور شبانہ بن سوار کے انہوں نے موصول کیا ہے اور نسائی کی روایت میں ہے کہ یہ چادر میں درہم کی تھی۔
Chapter: What the hand is cut for and what not. Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary on Hidayah volume six. And indeed the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, cut off the hand of the one who stole Safwan's cloak from under his head while he was asleep in the mosque. Translation: And Quduri said: Hirz (secure place) and protection are of two types: one protection that is secure by its own meaning, like rooms and houses (and chests, animals, pens for cows and horses). The second type of protection is that for which there is a specific guardian; then that item is under protection even if it is placed in an open field. Above, the author has said that hirz is necessary because without it, the item cannot be secretly removed. Protection can sometimes be through a place, and a place is considered protective only if it was built for safeguarding items, like houses, rooms, chests, shops, etc. And protection is sometimes through a guardian, for example, a person sits in a street or mosque with his property near him, then that property is protected by his presence. And the Prophet ordered the cutting of the hand of the one who stole Safwan's cloak from under his head while Safwan was asleep in the mosque. Commentary: He said: Hirz is of two types: hirz by its own meaning like houses and buildings, and hirz by a guardian... The meaning is clear from the translation. And the Prophet cut off the hand. Protection of property also occurs by simply placing it in a specific place, and sometimes by the presence of a guardian. For example, a person sits in a road or mosque and places his property near him, then it is considered in protection due to that person. As the Prophet ordered the cutting of the hand of the one who stole the cloak from under Safwan's head while he was asleep in the mosque. Then the author of Tanqih said this hadith is authentic, and Abu Dawud, Nasa'i, Ahmad, and Hakim narrated it. The full hadith is that Safwan bin Umayyah was told in Mecca that whoever does not migrate to Madina is ruined, so he left Mecca and migrated to Madina. He arrived at the mosque, placed his cloak under his head, and slept. A thief came and took his cloak. Safwan caught him and brought him to the Prophet, who ordered his hand to be cut. Safwan said, 'O Prophet, I did not want his hand to be cut, and I have given the cloak to him as charity.' The Prophet said, 'What is done is done. Why did you not do this before bringing him to me?' Malik narrated it as connected, and Ibn Abd al-Barr said that all of Malik's companions narrate it as mursal except Abu Asim al-Nabil and Shabana bin Sawwar who narrated it as connected. In Nasa'i's narration, the cloak was worth one dirham.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling from the chapter on hudud for theft. It defines the concept of hirz (secure enclosure) which is a necessary condition for cutting the thief's hand. Two types of hirz are identified: the first is hirz by its own nature, like houses, rooms, chests, and similar places built for protection. The second is hirz by a guardian, meaning a person who actively safeguards the property even in an open place like a mosque or road. The hadith of Safwan is cited as proof: the Prophet cut the hand of a thief who stole a cloak from under Safwan's head while he slept in the mosque, even though a mosque is not a place-based hirz. This shows that the guardian's presence creates hirz. The passage also explains that the hadith is authentic and gives details of the story, including Safwan's later forgiveness and the different narrations. This establishes the fundamental principle that hirz is not limited to locked buildings but can be established by a vigilant guardian, whether awake or sleeping.

Key Terms
حِرْز (hirz): A secure place or protective means that prevents clandestine removal of property.
حَافِظ (haafiz): A guardian or protector who actively safeguards property, even while sleeping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in this lesson, what are the two types of hirz?
A) Hirz by a locked door and hirz by a guard
B) Hirz by the place itself and hirz by a guardian
C) Hirz in a house and hirz in a mosque
D) Hirz by day and hirz by night

Key Takeaway
Hirz is a necessary condition for the hadd of theft; it can be established either by the place itself or by a guardian, as proven by the hadith of Safwan.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate
6 min
The Ruling on Theft from Place-Based vs. Guardian-Based Hirz
Distinguishes rulings for theft from place-based and guardian-based hirz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for theft from place-based and guardian-based hirz, and apply the qiyas reasoning to determine when the hadd is incurred.

Core Idea
For place-based hirz, the hand is cut only upon removal; for guardian-based, upon taking.

This passage addresses a key distinction in Hanafi hudud law: the moment at which theft is considered complete and the hadd punishment becomes obligatory depends on the type of security (hirz) protecting the property. The Hanafi school holds that for property secured by a place (like a house), the thief's hand is cut only when he physically removes the item from the place, because the owner's constructive possession (yad) persists until removal. For property guarded by a person (guardian-based hirz), the hand is cut as soon as the thief takes the item, since the owner's dominion ends immediately. This ruling is derived through qiyas and has practical implications for the liability of custodians.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of hirz
Recognize that the passage distinguishes between 'muhraz bil-makan' (place-based) and 'muhraz bil-hafiz' (guardian-based). This is the core conceptual split.
2
State the ruling for each type
For place-based: hand cut upon removal from the place. For guardian-based: hand cut upon taking into possession. Note the key phrases: 'لا يجب القطع إلا بالإخراج' and 'يجب القطع كما أخذ'.
3
Trace the qiyas reasoning
The asl (original case) is guardian-based hirz where cutting is immediate due to removal of owner's yad. The far' (new case) is place-based hirz; the illa (effective cause) is the removal of owner's dominion. In place-based hirz, removal occurs only upon physical exit, so cutting is delayed.
This qiyas is jaliy (explicit) and uses tahrir al-manat to extract the illa.
4
Apply to the depositary/borrower case
The principle extends to someone who sleeps near a deposited item: if he is customarily considered a guardian, he is not liable unless negligent. The Fatawa adds nuance: during residence, liability depends on sleeping position; during travel, no liability.
This shows how the same principle governs tort liability.

Source Text
