مکان میں محفوظ چیز کی حفاظت کے بارے میں حافظ کا اعتبار ہے یا نہیں
وَفِي الْمُحْرَزِ بِالْمَكَانِ لَا يُعْتَبَرُ الْإِحْرَازُ بِالْحَافِظِ وَهُوَ الصَّحِيحُ لِأَنَّهُ مُحْرَزٌ بِدُونِهِ وَهُوَ الْبَيْتُ وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ لَهُ بَابٌ أَوْ كَانَ وَهُوَ مَفْتُوحٌ حَتَّى يُقْطَعَ السَّارِقُ مِنْهُ لِاَنَّ الْبِنَاءَ لِقَصْدِ الْإِحْرَازِ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ لَا يَجِبُ الْقَطْعُ إِلَّا بِالْإِخْرَاجِ مِنْهُ لِقِيَامِ يَدِهِ قَبْلَهُ بِخِلَافِ الْمُحْرَزِ بِالْحَافِظُ حَيْثُ يَجِبُ الْقَطْعُ فِيهِ كَمَا أَخَذَلِزَوَالِ يَدِ الْمَالِكِ بِمُجَرَّدِ الاخْدِفَيَتِمُّ السَّرْقَةُ وَلَا فَرْقَ بَيْنَ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْحَافِظِ مُسْتَيْقِظًا اَوْنَائِمًا وَالْمَتَاعُ تَحْتَهُ أَوْعِندَهُ هُوَ الصَّحِيحُ لِأَنَّهُ يُعَدُّ النَّائِمُ عِنْدَ مَتَاعِهِ حَافِظَالَهُ فِي الْعَادَةِ وَعَلَى هَذَا لَا يُضْمَنُ الْمُوْدَعُ وَالْمُسْتَعِيْرُ بِمِثْلِهِ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِتَضْيع
بخلافِ مَا اخْتَارَهُ فِي الْفَتَاوى
ترجمہ اور جو چیز مکان کے اندر محفوظ ہو اس جگہ کسی محافظ کے ہونے یا نہ ہونے کا کوئی اعتبار نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ یہی قول صحیح ہے کیونکہ گھر میں ہونے کی وجہ سے کسی محافظ کے بغیر بھی حفاظت میں رکھی ہوئی کبھی جاتی ہے۔ اگر چہ اس گھر کا کوئی دروازہ نہ ہو۔ یا ہومگر کھلا ہوا ہو پھر بھی حرز اور حفاظت میں ہے۔ اسی بناء پر اس میں سے مال چرانے والے کا ہاتھ کا نا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ ایسی جگہ میں کوئی چیز حفاظت کی غرض سے ہی رکھی جاتی ہے۔ البتہ وہاں سے چرانے والے کا ہاتھ اسی صورت میں کاٹا جائے گا جبکہ اس جگہ سے مال کال کر باہر لے آیا ہو۔ کیونکہ باہر لانے سے پہلے تک وہ چیز مالک کی حفاظت ہی میں کبھی جاتی ہے اور اس کا قبضہ باقی رہتا ہے۔ بخلاف اس مال کے جو محافظ کی حفاظت میں ہو کہ اس میں سے کچھ مال چور کے ہاتھ میں لیتے ہی اس کے ہاتھ کا کا شنا واجب ہوتا ہے کیونکہ چور کے لیتے ہی مالک کا قبضہ ختم ہو گیا۔ اس لئے پورے طور پر چوری پائی گئی۔ پھر یہ معلوم ہونا چاہئے کہ محافظ جس حال میں بھی ہو خواہ وہ جاگتا ہو یا سوتا ہو اور وہ سامان اس کے نیچے ہو یا اس کے پاس رکھی ہوئی ہو۔ اس سے کچھ فرق نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ یہی قول صحیح ہے۔ کیونکہ جو شخص اپنے سامان کے پاس سوتا ہے عادت کے طور پر اسے مال کا محافظ ہی مانا جاتاہے۔اسی بناء پر ایسی صورت میں اپنے پاس امانت رکھنے والا یا رعایت پر لینے والا ضامن نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ یعنی جس کے پاس کوئی مال امانت یا رعایت کے طور پر ہوا اگر وہ اپنے پاس رکھ کر سو گیا اور مال چور لے گیا تو ضامن نہ ہوگا کیونکہ یہ ضائع کرنے میں شمار نہیں ہے۔ مگر فتاوی میں اس کے خلاف کہا گیا ہے۔ ف۔ چنانچہ فتاوی ا ظہیر یہ میں ہے کہ اگر امانت رکھنے والا کھڑا کھڑا سو گیا اور مال امانت اپنے سامنے رکھا ہو تو (اس کے ضائع ہونے سے اس کا) ضامن نہ ہوگا اور اگر کروٹ پر سو یا ہو تو ضامن ہوگا۔ یہ حکم حضر یا اقامت کی حالت میں رہنے کا ہے ۔ اور اگر حالت سفر میں ایسی صورت پیش آجائے تو کسی حال میں ضامن نہ ہوگا یعنی وہ خواہ بیٹھے بیٹھے سویا ہو یا کروٹ پر سو یا ہو یا کسی اور حال میں ہو۔
Regarding the protection of an item secured by a place, the presence of a guardian is not considered. This is the correct view, because it is secured without him, as it is a house even if it has no door or is open, so the thief's hand may be cut for theft from it because the building was built for the purpose of securing. However, cutting is not obligatory except upon removal from it, because the owner's hand (dominion) remains before that. Contrary to that which is secured by a guardian, where cutting is obligatory as soon as he takes it, due to the removal of the owner's hand. And there is no difference whether the guardian is awake or asleep, and the goods are under him or near him; this is the correct view, because a sleeping person near his goods is customarily considered a guardian. On this basis, the depositary and borrower are not liable for similar (loss), because it is not negligence. Contrary to what was chosen in the Fatawa. Translation: And for an item secure inside a place, the presence or absence of a guardian is not considered. That is the correct view, because being in a house makes it protected even without a guardian, even if the house has no door or it is open. Therefore, the hand of the thief will be cut. However, his hand is only cut when he removes the item from that place, because before removal the item remains under the owner's protection and his possession remains. Unlike the item guarded by a guardian, where cutting becomes obligatory as soon as the thief takes it into his hand, because by taking it the owner's possession ends, so the theft is complete. Then it should be known that the guardian, whether awake or asleep, and whether the item is under him or near him, makes no difference. That is the correct view, because a person who sleeps near his goods is customarily considered a guardian. On this basis, the depositary or borrower is not liable in such a case, meaning if someone has a deposit or borrowed item and he sleeps near it and it is stolen, he is not liable because this is not considered negligence. However, in the Fatawa, the opposite is stated. So in Fatawa Zahiriyyah, it is that if the depositary fell asleep while standing and the deposit was in front of him, he is not liable, but if he slept on his side, he is liable. This ruling is for the state of residence. If this occurs during travel, he is not liable in any state, whether he slept sitting, on his side, or any other state.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the distinction between place-based hirz and guardian-based hirz. For place-based hirz (like a house), the presence or absence of a guardian is irrelevant because the physical structure itself provides security. Even if the house has no door or is open, it is still considered hirz because it was built for that purpose. The hand of the thief is cut only when he removes the stolen item from the place, because until removal, the owner's constructive possession (yad) remains. For guardian-based hirz, the hand is cut as soon as the thief takes the item into his possession because the owner's dominion ends immediately. This applies whether the guardian is awake or asleep, and whether the item is under him or near him. The reasoning is that a sleeping person near his goods is customarily considered a guardian. The passage then applies this principle to the responsibility of a depositary (mudi') or borrower (musta'ir): if they sleep near the deposited or borrowed item, they are not liable if it is stolen, unless they were negligent. However, later fatwas (Fatawa Zahiriyyah) make a distinction based on the position of sleep: if sleeping while standing, not liable; if lying on the side, liable during residence. During travel, no liability regardless of position. This shows the depth of Hanafi fiqh in deriving practical rulings.

Key Terms
مُحْرَزٌ بِالْمَكَان (muhraz bil-makan): Property secured by virtue of being in a place built for protection, such as a house or shop.
يَد (yad): Owner's dominion or constructive possession over property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is the hand of a thief cut for theft from a place-based hirz (e.g., a house)?
A) As soon as he takes the item
B) Only after he removes the item from the house
C) Only if he steals at night
D) Only if the house has a door

Key Takeaway
The moment when the hadd of theft applies depends on the type of hirz: for place-based hirz, removal is required; for guardian-based, taking suffices.

The Owner's Presence as a Valid Hirz
Concise ruling on the condition for amputation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which theft from a non-hirz incurs the hadd penalty and apply it to a scenario.

Core Idea
Owner’s presence creates a guardian-based hirz, making theft from any location eligible for amputation.

The Hanafi school holds that the essence of hirz lies in protection, not physical enclosure. Even an open field becomes a valid hirz if the owner is present and guarding the property. This ruling from Quduri clarifies that amputation is not limited to theft from locked buildings; the owner's watchful presence suffices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The passage states that whoever steals something from a hirz or a non-hirz while its owner is guarding it, his hand is cut. Identify the act (theft) and the ruling (amputation).
The ruling applies whether the location is a conventional secure place or not.
2
Recognize the Key Condition
The critical condition is the owner's presence: 'wa sahibuhu indahu yahfazuhu' — the owner is with it, guarding it. Without this condition, theft from a non-hirz does not incur the hadd.
The owner must be actively guarding, not merely present.
3
Understand the Reasoning (Talil)
The passage gives the reason: 'because he stole property that is secure by one of the two types of hirz' – place-based or guardian-based. The owner's presence is a form of protection, making the property mahruz.
This is based on the hadith of Safwan where the cloak was not in a building but protected by Safwan's presence.
4
Apply the Ruling
Consider a scenario: A person leaves his wallet on a park bench and sits next to it reading. A thief snatches it. Since the owner is present and guarding, the property is considered in a valid hirz, and the hadd applies.
If the owner were not present, the theft from a park bench would not incur the hadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَمَنْ سَرَقَ شَيْئًا مِنْ حِرْزِ أَوْ مِنْ غَيْرِ حِرْرٍ وَصَاحِبُهُ عِنْدَهُ يَحْفَظُهُ قُطِعَ لِأَنَّهُ سَرَقَ مَالًا مُخْرَزًا بِأَحَدِ الْحِرْزَيْنِ ترجمہ اور قدوری نے کہا ہے کہ جس شخص نے مال حرز یا غیر حرز سے چرایا حالانکہ اس کا مالک اس مال کے پاس ہی موجود ہوا اور اس کی حفاظت کر رہا ہو تو اس چور کا ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ اس نے ایسے مال کو چرایا ہے جو حفاظت کے دو طریقوں میں سے ایک طریقہ سے حفاظت میں ہے۔ یعنی مال والے کے مال کے پاس ہونا بھی ایک طرح کی حفاظت ہے۔ اس لحاظ سے حقیقت میں اس نے محفوظ مال ہی چرایا ہے۔ اس لئے اس کا قطع واجب ہوگا۔
He said: Whoever steals something from a hirz or from a non-hirz while its owner is with it, guarding it, his hand is cut, because he stole property that is secure by one of the two types of hirz. Translation: And Quduri said that if a person steals property from a hirz or a non-hirz while the owner is present with that property and guarding it, then the thief's hand will be cut. Because he stole property that is protected by one of the two methods of protection. That is, the owner being near the property is also a form of protection. In this respect, in reality, he stole protected property. Therefore, his cutting is obligatory.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a concise ruling from Quduri's matn. It states that even if property is not in a physical hirz (like an open field), if the owner is personally present and guarding it, it is considered protected by the guardian-based hirz. Therefore, theft from such property incurs the hadd of cutting the hand. The reasoning is that the owner's presence itself constitutes one of the two types of hirz. This principle is based on the hadith of Safwan, where the cloak was not in a building but was protected by Safwan's presence. The ruling is clear and straightforward, emphasizing that the essence of hirz is protection, whether by place or by person.

Key Terms
غَيْر حِرْز (ghayr hirz): A place that is not customarily considered a secure enclosure, such as an open field or public road.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Quduri's statement, what is required for the hand to be cut when theft occurs from a non-hirz?
A) The owner must be present and guarding the property
B) The property must be worth ten dirhams
C) The theft must occur at night
D) The thief must be a repeat offender

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the owner's presence transforms any location into a valid hirz for the application of the hadd penalty.

Theft from Public Places: The Hirz Condition by Time
Ruling on theft from public-access places with a temporal distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on hadd for theft from public places (baths, shops, inns) by distinguishing between daytime and nighttime based on the hirz being compromised by permission.

Core Idea
A public place is not a hirz during the day due to permission; at night it becomes one — so no hadd for daytime theft.

This lesson examines a nuanced aspect of the hadd punishment for theft: the condition of hirz (secure place) and how it is affected by public access. The Hanafi school distinguishes between day and night for places like baths, shops, and inns. By understanding the rationale based on permission (idhn), you will see how the school applies the hirz condition contextually.

Guided Path
1
Identify the places and the ruling
List the locations mentioned: bathhouse (hammam), house with public permission, shops (hawanit al-tijarah), and inns (khanat). Note the general ruling: no cutting for theft from these places.
The exception is theft at night, which we will examine next.
2
Understand the basis: permission (idhn)
The key factor is permission to enter, either customarily (for baths and shops) or actually (for a house). This permission compromises the hirz because it removes the element of secrecy (ikhfa). Analyze how permission undermines the security required for hirz.
Permission can be explicit or implicit through custom.
3
Apply the temporal distinction
The text makes an exception for theft at night from shops and inns: 'illa idha saraqa minha laylan' (except if he steals from them at night). At night, permission is absent, so hirz is intact and cutting applies.
Baths are not mentioned for night theft, likely because they are typically closed at night.
4
Trace the reasoning
The text states these buildings were built for securing property (li-ihraz al-amwal). Permission is only specific to daytime. So during the day, the purpose of hirz is defeated; at night, it is restored.
This shows that hirz is not a static property of a place; it can change with time.
5
Expand the principle to other places
Consider other public-access places like libraries, government offices, etc. The same distinction applies: if the public has customary permission to enter during the day, hirz is compromised; at night, it is restored.
Always check the 'urf (custom) regarding permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا قَطعَ عَلَى مَنْ سَرَقَ مَالًا مِنْ حَمَامٍ أَوْ مِنْ بَيْتٍ أُذِنَ لِلنَّاسِ فِي دُخُولِهِ فِيهِ لِوُجُودِ الْإِذْنِ عَادَةً أَوْ حَقِيقَةٌ فِي الدُّخُولِ فَاخْتَلَّ الْحِرْرُ وَيَدْخُلُ فِي ذَالِكَ حَوَانِيتُ التَّجَارَة وَالْخَانَاتُ إِلَّا إِذَا سَرَقَ مِنْهَا لَيْلًا لِأَنَّهَابُنِيَتْ لإِحْرَازِ الْأَمْوَالِ وَإِنَّمَا الْإِذْنُ يَخْتَصُّ بِالنَّهَارِ
ترجمہ اورا اگر کسی نے حمام (غسل خانہ) سے یا ایسے گھر یا جگہ سے مال چرایا جہاں عام لوگوں کو جانے کی اجازت دی گئی ہے تو اس پر قطع لازم نہ ہو گا کیونکہ عموما ہر شخص کو جانے کی اجازت ہوتی ہے اور گھر میں حقیقی اجازت ہے۔ اس لئے حرز و حفاظت مکمل نہیں ہو سکی بلکہ اسے اس میں خلل پڑ گیا اور اسی قسم میں تاجروں کی دکانیں اور سرائیں، مسافر خانے بھی داخل ہیں۔ البتہ اگر ان جگہوں سے رات کے وقت چوری کی تب ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ یہ جگہیں اور عمارتیں بالوں کی حفاظت کی غرض سے بنائی گئی ہیں۔ لیکن ان میں داخل ہونے کی اجازت صرف دن کے لئے مخصوص ہوتی ہے۔
There is no cutting for the one who steals property from a bathhouse or from a house that people are permitted to enter, due to the existence of permission customarily or actually for entry, so the hirz is compromised. Included in that are shops and inns, except if he steals from them at night, because they were built for securing property and permission is only specific to daytime. Translation: And if someone stole from a bathhouse (hamam) or from a house or place where the public is allowed to enter, then cutting is not obligatory, because generally everyone has permission to enter, and in the case of a house, there is actual permission. Therefore, the hirz and protection were not complete; rather, it was compromised. Included in this category are merchants' shops and inns (caravansaries). However, if theft occurs from these places at night, then the hand will be cut, because these places and buildings were built for the purpose of protecting property, and permission to enter is only for the daytime.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on theft from places that are open to the public. The key principle is that if a place is generally accessible to people by permission (customary or actual), then the hirz is considered compromised because the accessibility undermines the security. Therefore, no hadd penalty applies for theft during the day from such places: baths (hamam), houses that allow entry, shops, and inns (khan). However, at night, these same places regain their character as hirz because permission to enter is typically limited to daytime. The reasoning is that these buildings are constructed for protection of property, but the public permission during the day negates the hirz. At night, the permission is absent, so the hirz is intact. This ruling shows the nuanced application of the hirz condition based on time and circumstance.

Key Terms
حَمَّام (hammam): A public bathhouse where people enter with permission.
خَان (khan): An inn or caravansary where travelers stay, typically open to the public during the day.
حِرْز (hirz): A secure place where property is kept; a condition for hadd al-sariqa.
إِذْن (idhn): Permission, either customary (urf) or actual, that undermines the exclusivity of a place.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is the hand of a thief cut for theft from a shop (dokan) according to this passage?
A) Always, because a shop is a hirz
B) Only if the theft occurs at night
C) Only if the owner is present
D) Never, because shops are public places

Key Takeaway
The hadd for theft requires a hirz; public permission during the day removes hirz, so no cutting for daytime theft from baths, shops, or inns—only at night does it apply.

Theft with Owner as Guardian (Hafiz)
Ruling on theft with owner as guardian, with extension to amanah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft when the owner is present as a guardian, explain the concept of hirz by hafiz, and apply the same principle to determine the liability of a trustee who sleeps with trust property.

Core Idea
Owner's presence makes any place a hirz; this ruling extends to exempt sleeping trustees from liability.

The Hanafi school has a unique view on what constitutes a secure place (hirz) for the hadd punishment of theft. While other schools require a physical enclosure, the Hanafis recognize the owner's active guardianship as a valid form of hirz. This lesson unpacks the key passage from Al-Hidayah that establishes this ruling and then traces how the same legal reasoning is applied to the liability of a trustee who sleeps with entrusted property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is theft (sariqah) when the owner is present and guarding the property. The ruling is that the thief's hand is cut (qat' al-yad).
This is the hadd punishment, wajib upon fulfillment of conditions.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is the matn of Al-Quduri quoting the Prophet's teaching: the hand is cut whether the theft is from a hirz or not, if the owner is present and guarding.
The dalil is a qawl sahabi (statement of a companion), considered reliable.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal implication)
The phrase 'min hirz aw min ghayr hirz' indicates that the condition of hirz is satisfied by the owner's presence, overriding the need for a physical secure place.
The imperative 'quti'a' implies obligation.
4
Identify the ikhtilaf (scholarly difference)
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) require a physical hirz and may not consider owner presence alone sufficient. The Hanafi view is declared sahih (correct) here.
The commentator (Ashraf al-Hidayah) adopts this as the relied-upon position.
5
Apply the same illa to amanah (trust)
The same principle – that a person who is customarily a guardian (hafiz) makes the property protected – is extended: a trustee who sleeps with the trust property is not liable for theft because he is considered a guardian.
An exception is made for sleeping on one's side during residence, per later fatawa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اسی بناء پر اس میں سے مال چرانے والے کا ہاتھ کا نا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ ایسی جگہ میں کوئی چیز حفاظت کی غرض سے ہی رکھی جاتی ہے۔ البتہ وہاں سے چرانے والے کا ہاتھ اسی صورت میں کاٹا جائے گا جبکہ اس جگہ سے مال کال کر باہر لے آیا ہو۔ کیونکہ باہر لانے سے پہلے تک وہ چیز مالک کی حفاظت ہی میں کبھی جاتی ہے اور اس کا قبضہ باقی رہتا ہے۔ بخلاف اس مال کے جو محافظ کی حفاظت میں ہو کہ اس میں سے کچھ مال چور کے ہاتھ میں لیتے ہی اس کے ہاتھ کا کا شنا واجب ہوتا ہے کیونکہ چور کے لیتے ہی مالک کا قبضہ ختم ہو گیا۔ اس لئے پورے طور پر چوری پائی گئی۔ پھر یہ معلوم ہونا چاہئے کہ محافظ جس حال میں بھی ہو خواہ وہ جاگتا ہو یا سوتا ہو اور وہ سامان اس کے نیچے ہو یا اس کے پاس رکھی ہوئی ہو۔ اس سے کچھ فرق نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ یہی قول صحیح ہے۔ کیونکہ جو شخص اپنے سامان کے پاس سوتا ہے عادت کے طور پر اسے مال کا محافظ ہی مانا جاتا ہے۔اسی بناء پر ایسی صورت میں اپنے پاس امانت رکھنے والا یا رعایت پر لینے والا ضامن نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ یعنی جس کے پاس کوئی مال امانت یا رعایت کے طور پر ہوا اگر وہ اپنے پاس رکھ کر سو گیا اور مال چور لے گیا تو ضامن نہ ہوگا کیونکہ یہ ضائع کرنے میں شمار نہیں ہے۔ مگر فتاوی میں اس کے خلاف کہا گیا ہے۔ ف۔ چنانچہ فتاوی ا ظہیر یہ میں ہے کہ اگر امانت رکھنے والا کھڑا کھڑا سو گیا اور مال امانت اپنے سامنے رکھا ہو تو (اس کے ضائع ہونے سے اس کا) ضامن نہ ہوگا اور اگر کروٹ پر سو یا ہو تو ضامن ہوگا۔ یہ حکم حضر یا اقامت کی حالت میں رہنے کا ہے ۔ اور اگر حالت سفر میں ایسی صورت پیش آجائے تو کسی حال میں ضامن نہ ہوگا یعنی وہ خواہ بیٹھے بیٹھے سویا ہو یا کروٹ پر سو یا ہو یا کسی اور حال میں ہو۔
تشريح وَفِي الْمُحْرَزِ بِالْمَكَانِ لَا يُعْتَبَرُ الْإِحْرَارُ بِالْحَافِظِ وَهُوَ الصَّحِيحُ because هُ مُخرز الخ مطلب ترجمہ سے واضح ہے۔ مال محرز یا غیر محرز جس کے مالک اس کی حفاظت کر رہا ہو سارق کا ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا
قَالَ وَمَنْ سَرَقَ شَيْئًا مِنْ حِرْزِ أَوْ مِنْ غَيْرِ حِرْرٍ وَصَاحِبُهُ عِنْدَهُ يَحْفَظُهُ قُطِعَ لِأَنَّهُ سَرَقَ مَالًا مُخْرَزًا بِأَحَدِ الْحِرْزَيْنِ ترجمہ اور قدوری نے کہا ہے کہ جس شخص نے مال حرز یا غیر حرز سے چرایا حالانکہ اس کا مالک اس مال کے پاس ہی موجود ہوا اور اس کی حفاظت کر رہا ہو تو اس چور کا ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ اس نے ایسے مال کو چرایا ہے جو حفاظت کے دو طریقوں میں سے ایک طریقہ سے حفاظت میں ہے۔ یعنی مال والے کے مال کے پاس ہونا بھی ایک طرح کی حفاظت ہے۔ اس لحاظ سے حقیقت میں اس نے محفوظ مال ہی چرایا ہے۔ اس لئے اس کا قطع واجب ہوگا۔ تشريح . قَالَ وَمَنْ سَرَقَ شَيْئًا مِنْ حِرْزِ أَوْ مِنْ غَيْرِ حِرْزَ وَصَاحِبُهُ عِنده ... الخ مطلب ترجمہ سے واضح ہے۔
Based on this, the hand of one who steals from it will be cut. Because in such a place, items are kept for protection. However, the hand will be cut only if he takes the item out of the place. Because before taking it out, the item remains in the owner's protection and his possession continues. Unlike property that is under a guardian's protection: as soon as the thief takes it into his hand, cutting his hand becomes obligatory because upon the thief taking it, the owner's possession ceases. Therefore, the theft is fully accomplished. Then it should be known that whatever state the guardian is in, whether awake or asleep, and whether the item is under him or placed near him, it makes no difference. This is the correct view. Because a person who sleeps near his belongings is customarily considered their guardian. On this basis, in such a situation, one who holds an amanah (trust) or takes something on loan is not liable. That is, if someone has property as an amanah or loan, and he goes to sleep with it and a thief takes it, he is not liable because this is not considered negligence. However, in the fatawa, the opposite is stated. Thus, in Fatawa Zahiriyyah, it is said: if the trustee fell asleep while standing and the trust property was placed in front of him, he is not liable if it is lost. But if he slept on his side, he is liable. This ruling applies in the state of residence. If such a situation occurs during travel, he is not liable in any case, whether he slept sitting, on his side, or otherwise. Commentary: Regarding the place of hirz (secure place), the guarding by the guardian is not considered. This is the correct view because it is [muhraz?]... The meaning is clear from the translation. Whether the property is in a secure place or not, if the owner is protecting it, the thief's hand is cut. He (al-Quduri) said: Whoever steals something from a secure place or from an unsecured place while the owner is present and guarding it, his hand is cut because he stole property that is secured by one of the two means of security. Translation: Al-Quduri said: Whoever steals property from a secure place or an unsecured place while the owner is present and guarding it, his hand is cut because he stole property that is secure through one of the two means of security. That is, the owner being near the property is also a form of security. So in reality, he stole protected property, therefore cutting his hand is obligatory. Commentary: The meaning is clear from the translation.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (dalil) and commentary from the Hanafi school. It discusses the concept of hirz (secured place) in theft. The main ruling: if the owner is present and guarding his property, even if the place is not inherently secure (like an open field), the thief's hand is cut because the owner's presence itself constitutes a valid hirz. The passage clarifies that taking the item out of the place is required for the cutting (ikhraj min al-hirz). It also discusses the guardian's state: the same ruling applies whether the guardian is awake or asleep, as long as he is customarily considered a protector. There is an exception from later fatawa regarding the trustee's sleeping position. The Arabic matn from al-Quduri is quoted: stealing from a secure place or unsecured place with the owner present results in cutting. The commentary explains that the owner's presence is one of two means of security (the other being the physical place). The passage is from the commentary of Hidayah (Ashraf al-Hidayah) expanding on the rulings of theft.

Key Terms
حرز (hirz): A secure place where property is kept protected.
محافظ (muhafiz / hafiz): The owner or guardian who is present and actively guarding the property.
قطع (qat'): Cutting off the hand as the hadd punishment for theft.
إخراج (ikhraj): Taking the stolen item out of the hirz – a condition for the hadd punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a thief takes a mobile phone from a table in a park where the owner is sitting ten meters away, is the hand cut?
A) Yes, because the owner's presence makes the park a hirz, and the thief took the phone off the table (ikhraj).
B) No, because the park is not a physical hirz.
C) No, because the owner was not guarding the phone actively.
D) Yes, but only if the owner was asleep.

Key Takeaway
The owner's presence as a guardian creates a valid hirz for theft, making any location potentially secure; this principle extends to exempt a sleeping trustee from liability, with exceptions for sleeping position in residence.

Theft from Mosque with Owner Present: The Role of Hirz
Ruling on theft from mosque with owner present

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft from a mosque when the owner is present, and distinguish it from theft in a bathhouse by applying the principle of hirz (secure keeping).

Core Idea
In the mosque, the owner's presence secures the property; in the bathhouse, the place itself is the hirz—cutting depends on which hirz is active.

This passage clarifies a nuanced point in Hanafi hudud: theft from a mosque. Although the mosque is open to all, if the owner is present, the property is considered secured by his guardianship, and the hadd (cutting) applies. The author contrasts this with theft from buildings designed for wealth protection, like bathhouses and public houses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage deals with theft of goods from a mosque while the owner is present. The ruling is that the thief's hand is cut (qati').
This ruling is specific to when the owner is in the mosque with the stolen property.
2
Understand the talil (legal reasoning)
The reason: the mosque was not built for securing wealth, so it is not a hirz by place. Therefore, the property becomes muhraz (secured) by the owner's presence as a guardian (hafiz). The cutting is due to this effective guardianship.
The key is that the place does not provide hirz, so the owner's personal guardianship becomes the decisive factor.
3
Contrast with bathhouse and public house
In a bathhouse (hammam) or a house where people are allowed to enter, the buildings were constructed for wealth protection, so the place itself is a hirz. Even if the owner is present, the place's hirz status is compromised by the public access, but the cutting is not applied because the owner's guardianship is surplus and the place's hirz is not effective due to permission to enter.
This distinction shows that the ruling depends on the building's purpose: worship vs. wealth protection.
4
Apply the condition of owner presence
In the mosque, without owner presence, the property is not muhraz by place, so no cutting. With owner presence, cutting applies. This is a tailored rule balancing public access and personal security.
This is unique to Hanafi fiqh; other schools may treat the mosque as a hirz regardless.

Source Text

مسجد سے ایسی چیز چوری کی جس کا مالک موجود ہو قطع ید ہو گا
وَمَنْ سَرَقَ مِنَ الْمَسْجِدِ مَتَاعًا وَصَاحِبُهُ عِنْدَهُ قُطِعَ لِأَنَّهُ مُحْوَةٌ بِالْحَافِظِ لِأَنَّ الْمَسْجِدَ مَابُنِيَ لِإِحْرَازِ الْأَمْوَالِ فَلَمْ يَكُنِ الْمَالُ مُخَرَزًا بالْمَكَانِ بِخِلَافِ الْحَمَامِ وَالْبَيْتِ الَّذِى أَذِنَ لِلنَّاسِ فِي دُخُولِهِ حَيْثُ لَا يُقْطَعُ لَانَّهُ بُنِيَ لِلْإِحْرَازِ فَكَانَ الْمَكَانُ حِرْزًا فَلَا يُعْتَبَرُ الْإِحْرَازُ بِالْحَافِظِ ترجمہ .... اور اگر کسی نے مسجد سے کسی ایسے شخص کا سامان چرایا جو اس کے پاس موجود تھا تو اس چور کا ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا کیونکہ یہ سامان اپنے محافظ کی حفاظت میں تھا۔ اگر چہ مسجد کی جگہ حفاظت کی نہیں ہوتی ہے کیونکہ وہ تو اس کے لئے بنائی جاتی ہے۔ اس لئے ہر شخص کو وہاں آمد و رفت کی اجازت ہوتی ہے لہذا مسجد کے اندر اور سامان کی جگہ کی وجہ سے محفوظ سامان نہیں ہوگا۔ بخلاف حمام اور اس گھر کے جس میں لوگوں کو جانے کی اجازت ہوا کرتی ہے اس لئے اگر چہ وہاں مالک موجود ہو پھر بھی چور کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ یہ مکان اس لئے بنائے گئے ہیں کہ مالوں کی حفاظت ہو تو وہ مکان خود ہی حرز اور محافظ ہے اس لئے دوسرے محافظ کی حفاظت کا اعتبار نہ ہوگا۔
تشريح وَ مَنْ سَرَقَ مِنَ الْمَسْجِدِ مَتَاعًا وَصَاحِبُهُ عِنْدَهُ قُطِعَ لأَنَّهُ مُحْرَةٌ بِالْحَافِظ الخ مطلب ترجمہ سے واضح ہے۔
Theft from mosque when owner present: hand is cut. And whoever steals goods from the mosque while the owner is near him, his hand is cut because it is secured by the guardian, for the mosque was not built for securing wealth, so the property was not secured by the place, unlike the bathhouse and the house that people are permitted to enter, where it is not cut because that was built for secure keeping, so the place itself is a hirz, and the securing by the guardian is not considered. Translation: And if someone steals the item of a person from the mosque while the owner was present, the thief's hand is cut, because this item was in the protection of its guardian. Although the mosque place is not for protection, because it is built for worship, so everyone has permission to come and go. Therefore, inside the mosque, the item is not secure due to the place. Unlike the bathhouse and the house where people are allowed to enter, even if the owner is present, the thief's hand is not cut, because these buildings are built for the protection of wealth, so the building itself is a hirz and guardian, so the protection of another guardian is not considered. Commentary: The meaning is clear from the translation.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with comparison (tafruq) between mosque and bathhouse/public house. The key point: The mosque is not a hirz by virtue of its place because it is built for worship, not for wealth storage. So when the owner is present, his guardianship makes the property muhraz (secure), and the thief's hand is cut. In contrast, a bathhouse or a house with public access is built for wealth protection, so the place itself is a hirz. If the owner is present there, his guardianship is redundant, and the place's hirz status is considered compromised by the permission to enter, so no cutting. The distinction hinges on whether the building was constructed for protection of goods (as in hammam and shops) or for other purposes (as in mosque).

Key Terms
مسجد (masjid): Mosque; a place of worship not built for wealth protection.
مُحْرَز (muhraz): Property that is secured (by place or guardian).
حِرْز (hirz): A secure place or container for storing wealth.
حَافِظ (hafiz): Guardian; a person whose presence secures property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) In the mosque the hand is cut, in the hammam it is not cut.
B) In both the hand is cut.
C) In the mosque it is not cut, in the hammam it is cut.
D) In neither is the hand cut.

Key Takeaway
Theft from a mosque triggers hadd only when the owner is present, because the mosque itself is not a hirz; the owner's guardianship provides the required security.

Theft by a Guest: Why the Hand is Not Cut
Ruling on theft by a guest from his host

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a guest's theft and explain why the condition of hirz is not met.

Core Idea
A guest's permission to enter removes the hirz, turning theft into betrayal – no hand cutting.

A guest entrusted with access to the host's home is not subject to the hadd of theft if he misappropriates property. This ruling hinges on the nature of the secure place (hirz) and the trust relationship. The guest's permission to enter the house fundamentally changes the legal classification of his act from theft (sariqa) to betrayal (khiyanah). This lesson unpacks the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a guest stealing property from his host. The ruling is that the hand is not cut (no hadd punishment).
This is a clear ruling from the Hanafi school, agreed upon by the majority.
2
Understand the basis: loss of hirz
The house is no longer a hirz (secure place) relative to the guest because he has been given permission to enter freely.
Hirz is a key condition for sariqa; without it, the taking cannot be classified as sariqa.
3
Classify the act as khiyanah
The guest's act is considered khiyanah (breach of trust) rather than sariqa (theft).
Khiyanah carries different penalties (tazir) and does not require a hirz.
4
Note the cross-madhhab agreement
This ruling is not unique to the Hanafi school; it is also the position of Malik, Shafi'i, and Ahmad (may Allah have mercy on them).
This indicates a strong legal basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مہمان کے چوری کرنے سے قطع ید ہے یا نہیں
وَلَا قَطعَ عَلَى الضَّيْفِ إِذَا سَرَقَ مِمَّنْ أَضَافَهُ لِاَنَّ الْبَيْتَ لَمْ يَبْقَ حِرْزًا فِي حَقِّهِ لِكَوْنِهِ مَا دُونَا فِي دُخُولِهِ وَلِأَنَّهُ بِمَنْزِلَةِ أَهْلِ الدَّارِ فَيَكُونُ فِعْلُهُ خِيَانَةٌ لَا سَرِقَةٌ
ترجمہ اگر کسی مہمان نے اپنے کسی میزبان کا کوئی سامان چرالیا تو اس کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا ۔ امام مالک و شافعی واحمد رحمہ اللہ علیہم کا یہی قول ہے) کیونکہ وہ مکان اس مہمان کے لئے محفوظ مکان نہیں رہا۔ اس لئے کہ اسے اس گھر میں آنے جانے کی اجازت دی گئی ہے اور اب وہ مہمان بھی گھر کے افراد میں سے ایک فرد کے حکم میں ہوگا ہے۔ لہذا اس کی اس حرکت کو خیانت کرنے سے تعبیر کیا جائے گا اور چوری نہیں کہی جائے گی۔
تشریح... وَلَا قُطِعَ عَلَى الضَّيْفِ إِذَا سَرَقَ مِمَّنْ أَضَافَهُ لِأَنَّ الْبَيْتَ لَمْ يَبْقَ حرزا ... الخ مطلب ترجمہ سے واضح ہے۔
Is hand cut for theft by guest or not? And there is no cutting upon the guest when he steals from the one who hosted him, because the house is no longer a hirz with respect to him due to being permitted entry, and because he is like a member of the house, so his act is betrayal (khiyanah) not theft. Translation: If a guest stole some item from his host, his hand is not cut (this is also the view of Imam Malik, Shafi'i, and Ahmad, may Allah have mercy on them) because the house is no longer a secure place for the guest, since he has been given permission to enter and leave, and now the guest is like one of the household members. Therefore, his action will be termed betrayal (khiyanah) and will not be called theft. Commentary: The meaning is clear from the translation.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement regarding theft by a guest. The Hanafi ruling: the guest's hand is not cut because the house is not a hirz (secure place) relative to him. He has permission to enter, so he is like a family member. His act is considered khiyanah (breach of trust), not sariqa (theft that incurs hadd). The ruling is consistent with the majority of schools (Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali). The basis is that the permission to enter removes the element of takhallus (clandestine taking from a secure place) which is essential for the hadd of theft.

Key Terms
ضيف (dayf): Guest; someone invited and given permission to enter the house.
خيانة (khiyanah): Betrayal of trust; a category of unlawful taking that does not incur hadd but may incur tazir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the guest has permission to enter, so the house is not a hirz for him.
B) Because the host is not a guardian.
C) Because the value of the item is usually low.
D) Because the guest is a family member.

Key Takeaway
A guest's theft is khiyanah, not sariqa, because permission to enter negates the hirz requirement for hadd.

Condition of Ikhraj for Theft Hadd: Removal from the Hirz
Ruling on theft when the item is not taken out of the house

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of ikhraj (removal from hirz) for the hadd of theft and explain why non-removal creates doubt (shubhah) that averts punishment.

Core Idea
For hadd of theft, the stolen item must be removed from the entire hirz; remaining within leaves ownership intact and creates doubt, so no cutting.

This passage from Al-Hidayah clarifies a crucial condition for the hadd of theft: ikhraj, or removing the stolen item from its place of security (hirz). Simply picking up an item within the house does not complete the act. The ruling is based on the principle that the entire house is a single hirz, and as long as the thief is inside, the owner's possession is not fully severed, generating a shubhah (doubt) that blocks the punishment. Understanding this condition illustrates how the Hanafi school applies the maxim that hadd punishments are averted by doubts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is stealing something from a house but not removing it from the house. The ruling is that the thief's hand is not cut.
2
Identify the condition for hadd
The condition is ikhraj – removing the stolen item from the entire hirz (secure place). Here, the house is one hirz, so removal from the house is required.
The term 'ikhraj' is used in the Arabic text.
3
Understand the reasoning (talil)
Two reasons: first, the entire house is one hirz; second, the owner retains possession of the house and its contents as long as the thief is inside, creating a doubt (shubhah) about complete taking.
The doubt is that the owner still has effective control over the property.
4
Apply the principle of shubhah
Doubt (shubhah) averts the hadd punishment. This is a fundamental Hanafi usul principle: hudud are averted by doubts.
The doubt here is that the theft is not fully completed.
5
Note the practical implication
The thief must physically exit the house (or the hirz) for the hadd to apply. Moving the item within the house does not suffice.
This is distinct from the act of taking possession; actual removal is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
چوری کی گھر سے باہر نہیں نکالا تھا تو قطع ید نہیں ہو گا
وَمَنْ سَرَقَ سَرِقَةٌ فَلَمْ يُخْرِجْهَا مِنَ الدَّارِ لَمْ يُقْطَعْ لِاَنَّ الدَّارَ كُلَّهَا حِرْزَ وَاحِدٌ فَلَابُدَّ مِنَ الْإِخْرَاجِ مِنْهَا وَلَانَّ الدَّارَ وَمَا فِيهَا فِي يَدِصَاحِبِهَا مَعْنَى فَيَتَمَكَّنُ شُبُهَةٌ عَدَمِ الْأَحْدِ
ترجمہ اگر کسی نے گھر سے کوئی سامان چرایا مگر باہر نہیں نکالا تھا کہ وہ پکڑا گیا) تو اس کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ پورا گھر ہی حرز حفاظت گاہ ہے۔ اس لئے اس مال کو گھر سے باہر نکال لینا ضروری ہوگا اور اس دلیل سے بھی کہ مکان اپنے اندر کے تمام سامان کے ساتھ اس وقت تک مکان والے کے قبضہ میں ہے۔ اس طرح یہ شبہہ ہو گیا کہ چور نے مال نہیں لیا ہے ( کیونکہ جب تک چور مکان کے اندر موجود ہے تب تک مالک کا قبضہ باقی ہے اور شبہہ پائے جانے کی وجہ سے حدختم ہو جاتی ہے۔
If the theft was not taken out of the house, there will be no cutting: And whoever stole something and did not take it out of the house, he is not cut, because the entire house is one hirz, so removal from it is necessary. And also because the house and what is in it are in the owner's possession effectively, so a doubt arises that the thief did not take the property (since as long as the thief is inside the house, the owner's possession remains, and due to doubt, the hadd is averted). Translation: If someone stole an item from a house but did not take it outside (i.e., he was caught), his hand is not cut. Because the entire house is a single secure place (hirz). Therefore, taking the item out of the house is necessary. And also because the house along with all its contents is in the owner's possession; thus a doubt arises that the thief did not actually take the property (because as long as the thief is inside the house, the owner's possession continues, and due to the existence of doubt, the hadd is dropped).
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage states a fundamental condition for the hadd of theft: the item must be removed from the hirz (ikhraj). The entire house is considered one hirz; moving the item within the house does not complete the theft. As long as the thief is inside the house, the owner's possession is not fully severed, which creates a shubha (doubt) that precludes the hadd. This is a clear ruling: without taking the item out of the place of hirz, there is no cutting. The doubt (shubha) is a key principle in Hanafi usul: hadd punishments are averted by doubts.

Key Terms
إخراج (ikhraj): Taking the stolen item out of the hirz (secure place); a condition for the hadd of theft.
شبهة (shubhah): Doubt that prevents application of hadd punishments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, a thief enters a house and picks up a valuable vase. Before he can exit, he is caught. Is his hand cut?
A) No, because the item was not taken out of the house.
B) Yes, because he took possession of the item.
C) Yes, if the vase is worth more than 10 dirhams.
D) No, because the owner was not present.

Key Takeaway
The hadd of theft requires ikhraj (removal from the hirz); without it, the doubt of continued ownership averts the cutting punishment.

Theft from Mosque: Hadd Applies When Owner Present
Ruling on theft from mosque with owner present

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft from a mosque and explain how the presence of the owner establishes hirz.

Core Idea
Hadd applies in a mosque only if the owner is present—the mosque alone is not a hirz.

In this passage, the author clarifies a key condition for the application of the hadd penalty for theft: the item must be taken from a secure place (hirz). While a mosque is not inherently a hirz, the owner's presence can create security. This lesson explores the rationale and contrasts with places like bathhouses that are inherently secure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
State the act (theft from a mosque while the owner is present) and the ruling (hadd applies).
2
Understand the rationale for the mosque
The mosque was not built for storage, so the place alone is not a hirz. The owner's presence acts as a guardian (hafiz), making the item muhraz.
Contrast with places built for security.
3
Contrast with bathhouse and permitted house
In a bathhouse or a house that people are permitted to enter, hadd does not apply because those places were built for securing goods (they are hirz by themselves). The guardian's presence is not considered.
The passage states 'his hand is not cut' in these places.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ سَرَقَ مِنَ الْمَسْجِدِ مَتَاعًا وَصَاحِبُهُ عِنْدَهُ قُطِعَ لِأَنَّهُ مُحْرَزٌ بِالْحَافِظِ لِأَنَّ الْمَسْجِدَ مَا بُنِيَ لِإِحْرَازِ الْأَمْوَالِ فَلَمْ يَكُنِ الْمَالُ مُحْرَزًا بِالْمَكَانِ بِخِلَافِ الْحَمَامِ وَالْبَيْتِ الَّذِي أَذِنَ لِلنَّاسِ فِي دُخُولِهِ حَيْثُ لَا يُقْطَعُ لِأَنَّهُ بُنِيَ لِلْإِحْرَازِ فَكَانَ الْمَكَانُ حِرْزًا فَلَا يُعْتَبَرُ الْإِحْرَازُ بِالْحَافِظِ
And whoever steals an item from the mosque while its owner is present with him, his hand is cut because it is secured by the guardian, because the mosque was not built for securing goods, so the item was not secured by the place, unlike the bathhouse and the house which the people are permitted to enter, where his hand is not cut because it was built for securing, so the place is a hirz, thus the securing by the guardian is not considered.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling and its rationale. The ruling: if a person steals an item from a mosque while the owner is present, hadd applies. The reason is that the mosque itself is not considered a secure place (hirz) because it was built for worship, not for storing goods. However, the presence of the owner acts as a guardian, providing security. In contrast, a bathhouse or a house that people are allowed to enter is considered a hirz by itself because it was built for securing goods. Therefore, in those places, the presence of a guardian is not considered; if theft occurs, hadd does not apply because the item is already secured by the location. This distinction is based on the fact that the mosque is not a hirz by nature, so the guardian's presence makes it a hirz, while the bathhouse and permitted house are hirz by nature, so the guardian's presence is irrelevant.

Key Terms
حِرْز (hirz): A secure place or container that protects goods from theft.
مُحْرَز (muhraz): An item secured in a hirz.
حَافِظ (hafiz): A guardian or keeper who secures the item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a mosque, if a person steals an item while the owner is not present, does hadd apply according to this passage?
A) Yes, because the mosque is a sacred place.
B) No, because the mosque is not a hirz and there is no guardian.
C) Yes, because the item is in a place of worship.
D) No, because theft in a mosque is not considered theft.

Key Takeaway
Hadd for theft from a mosque requires the owner's presence to establish hirz.

Theft by a Guest: No Hadd Due to Absence of Hirz
Ruling and rationale for dropping hadd in guest theft

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a guest who steals from his host and explain the two reasons (the house not being a hirz and the guest being like a household member) that prevent hadd.

Core Idea
A guest is exempt from hadd because the house is not a hirz for him and his act is khianah, not sariqah.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the law of theft (sariqah) in Hanafi fiqh. When a guest invited by the host steals from the host, the hadd penalty of amputation does not apply. The passage provides two interconnected reasons rooted in the definition of hirz and the nature of the guest's relationship to the household.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is theft by a guest from his host. The ruling is that no hadd (cutting of hand) is applied.
2
Understand the concept of hirz
Hirz is a secure place relative to the thief. For the guest, the house does not remain a hirz because he is permitted to enter. Without hirz, the taking cannot be classified as sariqah for hadd purposes.
Hirz is relative to the person, not absolute.
3
Recognize the classification as khianah
The guest is like a household member (ahl al-dar). His taking is betrayal (khianah), not theft (sariqah), because sariqah requires taking from a hirz without permission.
Khianah does not incur hadd.
4
Note the consequence and adjacent rulings
Hadd is dropped, but the guest may be civilly liable for the stolen property and may receive a discretionary punishment (tazir). If the guest's permission to enter is revoked, hadd may apply.
Hadd is not the only consequence; restitution and tazir remain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا قَطْعَ عَلَى الضَّيْفِ إِذَا سَرَقَ مِمَّنْ أَضَافَهُ لِأَنَّ الْبَيْتَ لَمْ يَبْقَ حِرْزًا فِي حَقِّهِ لِكَوْنِهِ مَأْذُونًا فِي دُخُولِهِ وَلِأَنَّهُ بِمَنْزِلَةِ أَهْلِ الدَّارِ فَيَكُونُ فِعْلُهُ خِيَانَةً لَا سَرِقَةً
And there is no cutting of the hand upon the guest when he steals from the one who hosted him, because the house does not remain a hirz with respect to him, due to him being permitted to enter it, and because he is like a member of the household, so his action is betrayal, not theft.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage addresses the case of a guest. If a guest steals from his host, the hadd of cutting is not applied. The reason: the house is not considered a secure place (hirz) for the guest because he is permitted to enter. Moreover, the guest is treated like a member of the household. Therefore, his taking is classified as khianah (breach of trust) rather than sariqah (theft), which requires the element of taking from a hirz without permission. This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi principle that a custodian or family member does not have hadd because of the shared access and trust.

Key Terms
ضَيْف (dayf): A guest who has been invited and permitted to enter the house.
خِيَانَة (khiyanah): Betrayal or breach of trust, as opposed to theft which requires taking from a hirz.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is hadd not applied to a guest who steals from his host?
A) Because the guest is poor.
B) Because the guest is permitted to enter, making the house not a hirz for him.
C) Because the host is responsible for the guest's actions.
D) Because the guest did not intend to steal.

Key Takeaway
The hadd of theft is dropped for a guest because the house is not a hirz for him and his act is classified as betrayal (khianah), not theft (sariqah).

Requirement of Removal from Entire Hirz for Hadd
Ruling with proof on condition of removal from entire hirz for hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for hadd of theft and explain why removal from the entire hirz is required, applying the principle of shubhah.

Core Idea
Hadd for theft requires removal from the entire secure place; otherwise doubt prevents punishment.

This lesson examines a key condition for the application of the hadd penalty for theft in Hanafi fiqh. The passage clarifies that mere taking from within a house is insufficient; the stolen item must be physically removed from the entire house. This condition is based on the concept that the whole house is a single hirz and that any remaining attachment to the owner's possession creates a doubt that blocks the hadd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
Note the explicit ruling: if a thief does not remove the stolen item from the house, his hand is not cut. The condition for hadd is physical removal from the entire house (the hirz).
Hadd is avoided by doubts, and this is a case where doubt arises.
2
Analyze the first proof: unity of the hirz
The author states that the entire house is one secure place (hirz wahid). Therefore, removal must be from the whole boundary, not just from a room or partial area.
In Hanafi law, a hirz is defined based on custom (urf). A house is typically a single hirz.
3
Analyze the second proof: shubhah of non-taking
Because the house and its contents are considered in the owner's possession (fi yadihi ma'nan), there is a doubt that the thief has actually taken possession. This doubt prevents the application of the hadd.
The principle that 'hadd is avoided by doubts' is applied here.
4
Extend the principle to other contexts
Consider other secure places such as a shop, a compound, or a locked room within a larger building. The same rule applies: removal from the entire boundary of the hirz is required.
If the thief moves from one room to another within the same house, hadd is still not applied until he leaves the outer boundary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ سَرَقَ سَرِقَةً فَلَمْ يُخْرِجْهَا مِنَ الدَّارِ لَمْ يُقْطَعْ لِأَنَّ الدَّارَ كُلَّهَا حِرْزٌ وَاحِدٌ فَلَابُدَّ مِنَ الْإِخْرَاجِ مِنْهَا وَلِأَنَّ الدَّارَ وَمَا فِيهَا فِي يَدِ صَاحِبِهَا مَعْنًى فَيَتَمَكَّنُ شُبْهَةُ عَدَمِ الْأَخْذِ
And whoever steals a theft and does not remove it from the house, his hand is not cut, because the entire house is one secure place, so removal from it is necessary, and because the house and what is in it are in the possession of its owner in meaning, so a doubt of non-taking arises.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage states that for hadd to apply, the thief must physically remove the stolen item from the entire house. The house is considered a single hirz. Until the item exits the boundary of the house, it is still under the effective control of the owner. Therefore, if the thief is caught inside the house with the item, hadd is not applied due to the doubt (shubhah) that he has not fully taken possession. This doubt is sufficient to prevent hadd, as hadd is avoided by doubts. The same principle applies to any compound that is considered a single hirz.

Key Terms
إِخْرَاج (ikhraj): Physical removal of the stolen item from the boundary of the hirz.
شُبْهَة (shubhah): Doubt that prevents the application of hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a thief steals an item from inside a house but is caught before leaving the house, does hadd apply?
A) Yes, because he took the item from a hirz.
B) No, because he did not remove it from the house.
C) Yes, because the house is a hirz.
D) No, because the item is still in the house.

Key Takeaway
For hadd to apply in Hanafi fiqh, the stolen item must be physically removed from the entire secure place (hirz); otherwise, doubt (shubhah) blocks the penalty.

Theft Between Partitioned Rooms: Hadd Applies Due to Separate Hirz
Ruling on theft from partitioned room to courtyard

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft from one partitioned room to the courtyard and explain the reasoning that each maqsurah is a separate hirz.

Core Idea
Each partitioned room in a shared house is a separate hirz; removal to the common courtyard completes the taking and triggers hadd.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in theft (sariqa) where a compound contains multiple partitioned rooms (maqasir). The key question is whether moving stolen goods from one room to the shared courtyard constitutes removal from the hirz (secure place). The Hanafi school rules that it does, because each room is an independent hirz for its occupant. Understanding this helps clarify the boundaries of hirz in shared living spaces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that the house has multiple partitioned rooms (maqasir), each occupied by a different resident. The stolen item is moved from one such room to the courtyard of the same house.
The courtyard (sahn) is a common area, not assigned to any occupant.
2
Recognize the act of theft
The thief removes the item from the secure place (maqsurah) to the courtyard. This constitutes taking from a hirz to a non-hirz area.
The act of moving to the courtyard is considered removal from the original hirz.
3
Apply the concept of hirz
Each maqsurah is a separate hirz because it is occupied by a specific inhabitant. The courtyard is not a hirz; it is a shared space.
The proof is: 'because each maqsurah, with respect to its inhabitant, is a separate hirz.'
4
Derive the ruling
Since the stolen item has been removed from its hirz, the condition for hadd is fulfilled. Therefore, the thief's hand is cut.
This ruling applies even if the item is still within the physical boundaries of the house.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ كَانَتْ دَارٌ فِيهَا مَقَاصِيرُ فَأَخْرَجَهَا مِنْ مَقْصُورَةٍ إِلَى صَحْنِ الدَّارِ قُطِعَ لِأَنَّ كُلَّ مَقْصُورَةٍ بِاعْتِبَارِ سَاكِنِهَا حِرْزٌ عَلَى حِدَةٍ وَإِنْ أَغَارَ إِنْسَانٌ مِنْ أَهْلِ الْمَقَاصِيرِ عَلَى مَقْصُورَةٍ فَسَرَقَ مِنْهَا قُطِعَ لِمَا بَيَّنَّا
If there is a house with partitioned rooms [maqasir] and he removes it from one partitioned room to the courtyard of the house, his hand is cut, because each partitioned room, with respect to its inhabitant, is a separate secure place. And if a person from the inhabitants of the partitioned rooms raids upon a partitioned room and steals from it, his hand is cut for what we have explained.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage clarifies the scenario when a compound (dar) contains multiple partitioned rooms (maqasir), each occupied by different inhabitants. In such a case, each room is considered an independent hirz for its occupant. Therefore, if someone steals from one room and moves the item to the shared courtyard, that constitutes removal from the original hirz, and hadd applies. Similarly, if one resident from another room intrudes into a different room and steals, hadd applies because each room is a separate hirz. The ruling is based on the principle that each room is a hirz by virtue of being assigned to its inhabitant, even though the compound shares a common area.

Key Terms
مَقْصُورَة (maqsurah): A partitioned room within a larger compound, considered a separate hirz.
صَحْن (sahn): Courtyard of the house.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No, because it is still within the same compound.
B) Yes, because each partitioned room is a separate hirz.
C) No, because the courtyard is also part of the house.
D) Yes, because the thief crossed a boundary.

Key Takeaway
In shared compounds, each partitioned room is a separate hirz; moving stolen goods to the common courtyard triggers the hadd punishment.

Theft via Accomplice and Throwing Goods Out: Hanafi Rulings on Completion of Removal
Ruling on hadd when a thief uses an accomplice or throws goods out.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on theft when an accomplice receives the goods or when the thief throws goods out, and explain the role of valid hand and trickery.

Core Idea
Theft requires removal from hirz; an accomplice's hand drops hadd, but throwing out is a trick that counts as removal.

This passage from the Hidayah addresses two common scenarios in theft: using an accomplice and using a trick like throwing goods out. The rulings depend on whether the thief is considered to have completed the removal (ikhraj) from the protected place (hirz). Understanding these nuances is key to applying the hadd penalty correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the source and identify the two distinct situations: (1) handing goods to an accomplice outside, (2) throwing goods out and then retrieving them.
2
For the accomplice scenario, note the general rule
The general Hanafi ruling is that neither the inside thief nor the outside accomplice is subject to hadd. Understand why: the inside thief did not complete removal due to the accomplice's hand, and the accomplice did not break the hirz. Then note Abu Yusuf's differentiation based on who extends their hand.
Abu Yusuf's view is a known intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
For the throwing scenario, note the dispute
The Hanafi school holds that hadd applies when the thief throws goods out and then retrieves them. Zufar dissents, arguing that throwing alone is not removal. Identify the core disagreement.
This is a clear intra-Hanafi dispute on the definition of removal.
4
Analyze the reasoning
The Hanafi argument: throwing is a trick (hilah) commonly used by thieves to overcome difficulty in exiting with the goods. Since no valid hand (yad mu'tabarah) intervenes, the entire sequence (entering, throwing, exiting, retrieving) is considered one continuous act of removal.
This istihsan-based reasoning prevents evasion of hadd through stratagems.
5
Apply the rule
Given a new scenario (e.g., thief breaks a window, throws out a laptop, climbs out and picks it up), determine whether hadd applies according to the Hanafi school. Explain your reasoning using the concepts from the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا نَقَبَ اللِّصُّ الْبَيْتَ فَدَخَلَ وَأَخَذَ الْمَالَ وَنَاوَلَهُ آخَرَ خَارِجَ الْبَيْتِ فَلَا قَطْعَ عَلَيْهِمَا لِأَنَّ الْأَوَّلَ لَمْ يُوجَدْ مِنْهُ الْإِخْرَاجُ لِاعْتِرَاضِ يَدٍ مُعْتَبَرَةٍ عَلَى الْمَالِ قَبْلَ خُرُوجِهِ وَالثَّانِي لَمْ يُوجَدْ مِنْهُ هَتْكُ الْحِرْزِ فَلَمْ يَتِمَّ السَّرِقَةُ مِنْ كُلٍّ وَاحِدٍ وَعَنْ أَبِي يُوسُفَ إِنْ أَخْرَجَ الدَّاخِلُ يَدَهُ وَنَاوَلَهَا الْخَارِجُ فَالْقَطْعُ عَلَى الدَّاخِلِ وَإِنْ أَدْخَلَ الْخَارِجُ يَدَهُ فَتَنَاوَلَهَا مِنْ يَدِ الدَّاخِلِ فَعَلَيْهِمَا الْقَطْعُ وَهِيَ بِنَاءً عَلَى مَسْأَلَةٍ تَأْتِي بَعْدَ هَذَا إِنْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى وَإِنْ أَلْقَاهُ فِي الطَّرِيقِ وَخَرَجَ فَأَخَذَهُ قُطِعَ وَقَالَ زُفَرُ لَا يُقْطَعُ لِأَنَّ الْإِلْقَاءَ غَيْرُ مُوجِبٍ لِلْقَطْعِ كَمَا لَوْ خَرَجَ وَلَمْ يَأْخُذْ وَكَذَا الْأَخْذُ مِنَ السُّعَةِ كَمَا لَوْ أَخَذَهُ غَيْرُهُ وَلَنَا أَنَّ الرَّمْيَ حِيلَةٌ يَعْتَادُهَا السُّرَّاقُ لِتَعَذُّرِ الْخُرُوجِ مَعَ الْمَتَاعِ أَوْ لِيَتَفَرَّغَ لِقِتَالِ صَاحِبِ الدَّارِ أَوْ لِلْفِرَارِ وَلَمْ تَعْتَرِضْ عَلَيْهِ يَدٌ مُعْتَبَرَةٌ فَاعْتُبِرَ الْكُلُّ فِعْلًا وَاحِدًا فَإِذَا خَرَجَ وَلَمْ يَأْخُذْهُ فَهُوَ مُضَيِّعٌ لَا سَارِقٌ
And when a thief digs through a wall into the house, enters, takes the goods, and hands it to another person outside the house, then there is no cutting on either of them, because the first did not perform the removal due to an intervening valid hand over the goods before its exit, and the second did not perform the breaking of the hirz, so the theft is not completed from each one. And from Abu Yusuf: if the inside person extends his hand and hands it to the outside person, then the cutting is on the inside person. And if the outside person inserts his hand and takes it from the hand of the inside person, then cutting is on both. And it is built upon a question that comes after this, if Allah wills. And if he throws it into the road and exits and then takes it, his hand is cut. And Zufar said: he is not cut, because throwing does not entail cutting, just as if he exited and did not take it, and likewise taking from the open space is like if someone else took it. But we [Hanafis] argue: throwing is a trick that thieves often use due to the difficulty of exiting with the goods, or to free himself to fight the owner or to flee, and no valid hand intervened, so all is considered one action. So if he exits and does not take it, he is a waster, not a thief.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage addresses two scenarios involving an accomplice or a trick. First, when a thief enters a house and hands goods to an accomplice outside: the general ruling is that neither is cut. The inside thief did not complete removal because before the goods exit, they are intercepted by the valid hand of the accomplice (which is not a valid hirz-breaking hand? Actually the reasoning: the inside person did not remove it because the hand of the accomplice intercepts before exit, and the accomplice did not break the hirz. So neither fulfills all conditions. However, Abu Yusuf distinguishes: if the inside thief extends his hand and hands over, then the inside thief is cut because he initiated removal. If the outside accomplice inserts his hand, then both are cut. This is based on a later principle. Zufar differs on the case of throwing the goods out: he says no hadd because throwing alone does not constitute removal. But the Hanafi position (ours) is that throwing is a common trick, and since no valid hand intervenes, the entire act of entry, throwing, and taking outside is considered one continuous action, so hadd applies. If he exits and does not take it, he is not a thief but a waster (mudi'). This shows the Hanafi use of istihsan (juristic preference) to treat the combined actions as one theft.

Key Terms
نَقَب (naqaba): To dig through a wall to break into a house.
يَدٌ مُعْتَبَرَةٌ (yadun mu'tabarah): A valid hand that has a legal claim or control over the item, interrupting the thief's removal.
حِيلَة (hilah): A trick or stratagem used to achieve an end.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does a thief who throws stolen goods out of a house and then retrieves them face hadd?
A) Because throwing alone constitutes removal.
B) Because no valid hand intervened and the throwing is a trick considered part of the theft.
C) Because the goods left the hirz the moment they were thrown.
D) Because Zufar agreed with it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the use of an accomplice or a trick does not automatically negate hadd; the key is whether the thief personally completes removal without an intervening valid hand.

Theft with Accomplice: Handing Over and Throwing Out Goods
Intra-Hanafi dispute on theft with accomplices

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft when goods are handed to an accomplice or thrown out, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar.

Core Idea
No hadd if the insider does not remove goods or the outsider does not break the hirz.

This passage explores the intricacies of theft with accomplices. The key is that both elements—breaking the guarded place (hirz) and removing the goods (ikhraj)—must be completed by the same individual for the hadd penalty to apply. The passage examines scenarios where these elements are split between two thieves, leading to differing rulings within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Recognize that the passage addresses two distinct situations: (1) a thief enters, takes goods, and hands them to an accomplice outside; (2) a thief throws goods out of the house and later retrieves them.
The first scenario involves two thieves acting together; the second involves a single thief who uses a trick.
2
State the basic ruling (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad)
In the handing-over scenario, neither thief is cut because the insider did not remove the goods (owner's hand still on them) and the outsider did not break the hirz. In the throwing scenario, the thief is cut because the act is considered one continuous removal.
This is the position of the majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) except Zufar on throwing.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's differentiation
Abu Yusuf distinguishes based on who extends the hand: if the insider extends his hand outside and hands over, only the insider is cut; if the outsider inserts his hand inside and takes, both are cut. This is built on a principle that extending the hand out of the hirz constitutes removal.
Abu Yusuf's view is a minority within the school; the relied-upon position is the majority.
4
Examine Zufar's objection on throwing
Zufar argues that throwing alone is not a cause for cutting, analogizing to exiting without picking up or another person picking it up. He sees no cutting because the removal is not simultaneous with leaving the hirz.
Zufar's view is rejected by the majority.
5
Understand the Hanafi response (Hila)
The majority responds that throwing is a common trick (hila) used by thieves to evade detection or fight, and no valid hand intervenes during the transfer. Therefore, the entire act—throwing and later picking up—is considered one continuous removal from the hirz.
This prevents evasion of punishment.

Source Text

چور نے گھر میں سوراخ کیا اور داخل ہو گیا اور مال اٹھا کر باہر کھڑے شخص کو دے دیا قطع ید کس پر ہے
وَإِذَا نَقَبَ اللصُّ البَيْتَ فَدَخَلَ وَاَخَذَ الْمَالَ وَنَاوَلَهُ اخَرَ خَارِجَ الْبَيْتِ فَلَا قَطعَ عَلَيْهِمَا لِأَنَّ الْأَوَّلَ لَمْ يُوجَدُ مِنْهُ الْإِخْرَاجُ لِاعْتِرَاضِ يَدِمُعْتَبَرَةٍ عَلَى الْمَالِ قَبْلَ خُرُوجِهِ وَالثَّانِي لَمْ يُوْجَدْمِنْهُ هَتْكُ الْحِرْزِ فَلَمْ يُتِمَّ السَّرِقَةُ مِنْ كُلّ وَاحِدٍ وَعَنْ أَبِى يُوْسُفَ إِنْ اَخْرَجَ الدَّاخِلُ يَدَهُ وَنَاوَلَهَا الْخَارِجُ فَالْقَطْعُ عَلَى الدَّاخِلِ وَإِنْ أَدْخَلَ الْخَارِجُ يَدَهُ فَتَنَا وَلَهَا مِنْ يَدِالدَّاخِلِ فَعَلَيْهِمَا الْقَطْعُ وَهِيَ بِنَاءً عَلَى مَسْأَلَةٍ تَانِى بَعْدَ هَذَا إِنْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى وَإِنْ الْقَاهُ فِي الطَّرِيقِ وَخَرَجَ فَاَخَذَهُ قُطِعَ وَقَالَ زُفَرٌ لَا يُقْطَعُ لِاَنَّ الْإِلْقَاءَ غَيْرُ مُوْجِبِ لِلْقَطْعِ كَمَا لَوْ خَرَجَ وَلَمْ يَأْخُذُوكَذَا الْأَحْـدُمِنَ السُّعَةِ كَمَا لَوْ أَخَذَهُ غَيْرُهُ وَلَنَا أَنَّ الرَّمْيَ حِيْلَةٌ يَعْتَادُهَا السُّرَّاقُ لِتَعَدُّرِ الْخُرُوجِ مَعَ الْمَتَاعِ اَوْ لِيَتَفَرَّعَ لِقِتَالِ صَاحِبِ الدَّارِ أَوْ لِلْفِرَارِ وَلَمْ تَعْتَرِضْ عَلَيْهِ يَدْمُعْتَبَرَةٌ فَاعْتُبِرَ الْكُلُّ فِعْلًا وَاحِدًا فَإِذَا خَرَجَ وَلَمْ يَأْخُذْهُ فَهُوَ مُضِيعٌ لَا سَارِقٌ
ترجمہ اور اگر کسی چور نے کسی مکان میں کہیں سے سوراخ کر کے اس میں داخل ہو کر اس میں سے کچھ مال لیا اور ہاتھ بڑھا کر اپنے اس ساتھی کو دید یا جو اس گھر کے باہر کھڑا ہو تو ان دونوں میں سے کسی کا ہاتھ کا شنا واجب نہیں ہے کیونکہ اس میں داخل ہونے والے مکان کا مال و سامان باہر نکال لینا ثابت نہیں ہوتا ہے کیونکہ اس شخص کے اس گھر سے نکلنے تک پہلے مالک کا معتبر قبضہ ختم نہیں ہوا بلکہ موجود ہے اور باہر سے اس مال کو جس شخص نے لیا ہے اس پر یہ صادق نہیں ہے کہ اس نے مال محفوظ نکالا ہے۔ لہذا ان دونوں میں سے کسی ایک پر بھی چوری کی پوری تعریف نہیں پائی گئی اور امام ابوحنیفہ سے روایت ہے کہ اگر گھر میں گھس جانیوالے شخص نے اپنا ہاتھ نکال کر باہر والے شخص کو دید یا تو اس گھسنے والے کا ہاتھ کا ٹا جائے گا اور اگر باہر والے نے اپنا ہاتھ اندر داخل کرو کے مال ہاتھ سے لیا تو دونوں کا قطع واجب ہوگا۔ یہ مسئلہ در اصل اس مسئلہ پر مبنی ہے۔ جو انشاء اللہ تعالی ابھی بیان کیا جاتا ہے کہ اگر گھر میں جانے والے نے مال لے کر باہر راستہ پر ڈال دیا اور وہاں سے نکل کر اسے اٹھالیا تو ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا اور امام زفر نے کہا ہے کہ نہیں کاٹا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ باہر پھینک دینا ہاتھ کاٹنے کا سبب نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ چنانچہ اگر وہاں سے نکل کر اس کو وہ ہیں پڑارہنے دیا اور نہیں اٹھایا تو بالاتفاق اس کا قطع واجب نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ اسی طرح اگر راستہ اور گلی سے پڑا ہوامال کوئی چور اٹھا لے تو بھی زفر کے نزدیک قطع لازم نہیں آتا ہے۔ جب کہ اگر چور کے ماسوا کوئی دوسرا شخص لے جائے تو بالا تفاق اس کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جاتا ہے اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ گھر سے مال لے کر باہر پھینک دینا در اصل چوری کا ایک طریقہ اور حیلہ ہے جو چوروں کی عادت ہوتی ہے۔ کیونکہ سامان کے ساتھ گھر سے نکلنا بہت مشکل ہوتا ہے یا ایسا اس لئے کرتا ہے کہ ہاتھ خالی ہو جانے کے بعد اگر ضرورت پڑی تو گھر کے مالک کے ساتھ مقابلہ کر سکے یا خالی ہاتھ بھاگنے کی سہولت ہو جائے اور جیسا کہ بتایا گیا ہے کہ گھر میں رہنے تک اس پر مالک کا قبضہ رہتا ہے۔ دراصل اس کا قبضہ اس کے مقابل نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ لہذا چور کا مال کو لے کر پہلے باہر پھینک دینا یا ساتھ لے کر نکلنا برابر ہی سمجھا جائے گا اور اگر مال باہر پھینک کر چورا سے یوں ہی پڑا ہوا چھوڑ کر چلا جائے اور نہ اٹھائے تو اسے مال برباد کرنے والا کہا جائے گا اور وہ چور نہیں ہوگا۔
تشريح وَإِذَانَقِبَ اللصُّ البَيْتَ فَدَخَلَ وَأَخَذَ الْمَالَ وَنَاوَلَهُ اخَرَ خَارِجَ البيت الخ مطلب ترجمہ سے واضح ہے۔
The thief made a hole in the house, entered, took the goods, and handed them to another person outside the house. Who is liable for cutting? And if the thief makes a hole in the house, enters, takes the goods, and hands them to another outside the house, then there is no cutting upon either of them, because from the first person the removal is not found due to the intervention of a valid hand (ownership) over the goods before their exit, and from the second person the breaking of the guarded place is not found, so the theft is not complete from either. And from Abu Yusuf: if the insider extends his hand and hands it to the outsider, then cutting is on the insider; and if the outsider inserts his hand and takes it from the insider's hand, then cutting is upon both. This is based on a matter that will follow after this, Allah willing. And if he throws it onto the road and then exits and picks it up, he is cut. Zufar said: he is not cut, because throwing is not a cause for cutting, just as if he exited and did not pick it up, and just as if someone else picked it up from the road. Our argument: that throwing is a trick customary among thieves due to the difficulty of exiting with the goods, or to be free to fight the owner of the house, or to flee; and no valid hand intervenes against him, so the whole is considered one act. If he exits and does not pick it up, then he is a waster, not a thief. Translation: And if a thief makes a hole in a house, enters, takes some goods, and extends his hand to hand them to his accomplice standing outside, then neither of them is liable for cutting, because the one who entered does not have proven removal of the goods from the house, since until he exits, the owner's valid possession has not ceased; and the one outside cannot be said to have removed goods from a guarded place. So neither completes the definition of theft. And from Imam Abu Hanifa: if the entrant extends his hand and hands to the outsider, then the entrant's hand is cut; if the outsider inserts his hand and takes from the entrant's hand, then both are cut. This matter is based on a subsequent issue. That if the entrant takes the goods and throws them out onto the road, then exits and picks them up, his hand is cut. Imam Zufar said: he is not cut, because throwing is not a cause for cutting. If he exits and leaves it, consensus that his hand is not cut. Similarly, if another thief picks it up from the road, Zufar says no cutting. Our proof: throwing is a trick customary among thieves because leaving with the goods is difficult, or to be free for confrontation or escape; no valid hand intervenes, so the whole is considered one act. If he throws and exits without picking up, he is a waster, not a thief. Commentary: And if the thief makes a hole... etc. The meaning is clear from the translation.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding theft when an accomplice is involved. The core ruling from the matn states that when one person enters a house and hands the goods to another outside, neither is cut because the first did not remove the goods (the owner's hand is still upon them until the thief exits) and the second did not break the guarded place. However, Abu Yusuf distinguishes: if the insider extends his hand and hands over, the insider is cut (as the removal is attributed to him by extending his hand out of the hirz). If the outsider inserts his hand inside and takes, then both are cut (the outsider participates in removal and the insider in breaking the hirz). The passage then addresses a related scenario: if the thief throws the goods out of the house and then exits and picks them up, the majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) say he is cut because the throwing and picking up are considered one continuous act of removal. Zufar disagrees, arguing that throwing alone is not removal. The Hanafi argument is that it is a common trick (hila) by thieves, and no valid hand (of the owner) intervenes during the transfer, so it is treated as one act. If he exits without picking up, he is not a thief but a waster (mudi'). The commentary clarifies these positions.

Key Terms
نَقَب (naqaba): To make a hole or breach in a wall, specifically for entering a house to steal.
إِخْرَاج (ikhraj): Removal of stolen goods from the guarded place (hirz); a key element for the hadd penalty.
يَد مُعْتَبَرَة (yad mu'tabarah): A valid possessory hand (ownership or lawful custody) that intervenes to prevent the stolen goods from being considered removed as a completed theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), if a thief enters a house and hands the stolen goods to an accomplice standing outside the door, what is the ruling?
A) Both thieves are cut.
B) Only the insider is cut.
C) Only the outsider is cut.
D) Neither is cut.

Key Takeaway
The hadd penalty for theft requires both breaking the hirz and removal by the same person; splitting these elements between accomplices prevents the penalty, unless a trick like throwing is used to circumvent the rule.
